PhD Programme in Architecture, Built Environment and Construction Engineering - ABC-PhD (38th Cycle) DIGITAL TRANSFORMATION OF ADMINISTRATIVE PROCEDURES Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management By Simone Balin (simone.balin@polimi.it) ****** Supervisors and Reviewer: Prof. Cecilia Maria Bolognesi1, Supervisor Prof. Paolo Borin2, Co-Supervisor Prof. Dragana Nikolić3, Reviewer Prof. Hans Hoogenboom4, Reviewer Politecnico di Milano 21 September 2025 1 Department of Architecture, Built Environment and Construction Engineering (DABC), Politecnico di Milano, 20133 Milan, Italy; cecilia.bolognesi@polimi.it 2 Department of Civil Engineering, Architecture, Territory, Environment and Mathematics (DICATAM), Università degli Studi di Brescia, 25121 Brescia, Italy; paolo.borin@unibs.it 3 School of the Built Environment, University of Reading, Reading RG6 6DF, United Kingdom; d.nikolic@reading.ac.uk 4 Faculty of Architecture and the Built Environment, Architectural Engineering + Technology, TU Delft, 2628 BL Delft, Netherlands, J.J.J.G.Hoogenboom@tudelft.nl Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management KEYWORDS AEC (Architecture, Engineering, and Construction) AI (Artificial Intelligence) Augmented Reality (AR) Blockchain BIM (Building Information Modeling) Collaborative Process Decision Making Design Review Extended Reality (XR) Facility Management Immersive Environments Immersive Realities Industry 5.0 Internet of Things (IoT) Level of Detail (LOD) Level of Information Need (LOIN) Metaverse Mixed Reality (MR) Remote Collaboration Service Conference Virtual Model Virtual Reality (VR) Virtual Reality (VR) Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management ABSTRACT The evolution of representation techniques has expanded the range of tools available to support decision-making processes in complex collaborative contexts, including administrative procedures in the construction industry. Despite their widespread adoption in sectors such as gaming, real estate, and medicine, these technologies are still rarely implemented in a systematic manner within professional and institutional workflows in construction practice. While information management standards such as ISO 19650 provide consolidated guidelines for data structuring and exchange, comparable methodological requirements for the integration of representation systems remain limited. This research addresses this gap by proposing a methodological framework that connects representation systems with information management processes in collaborative and decision-making contexts, with a specific focus on administrative procedures. The study adopts a Design Science Research methodology and a mixed methods approach, combining a systematic literature review with empirical validation through two case studies. The proposed framework is based on an ecosystem-oriented perspective, in which the interaction between users, digital objects, and experiential environments constitutes the operational basis of representation processes. Use cases and cognitive experiences are explicitly defined to support the configuration of representational ecosystems as structured components of information management workflows. The framework extends existing standards (ISO 19650, ISO 7817-1) by introducing prerequisites and experiential requirements that address both technical aspects, such as interoperability and scalability, and experiential dimensions, including usability, engagement, and presence. The case studies indicate that the application of the proposed framework is associated with improvements in the organization of representation systems and in collaborative and collective decision-making processes. The research discusses the implications, limitations, and potential applicability of integrating experiential representation systems within administrative and decision-making contexts. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management PhD TIMELINE In  we have summarized the tasks, objectives and methodologies of the research based on the bibliographic collection and the analysis of the literature. We have divided these elements into three-time frames: short term (A), medium term (B) and long term (C). Some planned activities have been listed, although not exhaustively. Level A, covering the first year, includes a detailed phase in which we structure and analyse the key concepts and most important aspects present in the literature. The aim is to identify current gaps and formulate a targeted research question. In the medium-term phase B, which extends from the first to the second year, it involves finding answers and solutions to the research question through analysis, development of new concepts, methodologies and application of case studies. The aim is to solve or provide indications and well-structured concepts on how to solve the gaps present in the literature. Finally, phase C represents the long term of the research, covering the third year. In this more mature phase of the work, the ideas developed and tested during the previous phases are verified and compared with the current state, scientific dissemination activities are proposed through publications and conferences. As mentioned above, the research focuses on the development of points A, B and C, which are further divided into time frames WP1, WP2 and WP3. In point A, after a literature review and analysis, the following research question emerged: “What are the effects of a structured configuration on the integration of representation systems in collaborative and decision-making processes, particularly in administrative procedures such as service conferences?” In point B, we will try to answer this question through the creation and definition of structured processes, application methodologies and case studies structured according to the specific needs of data and geometries for the use of technologies during the collaborative process and BIM methodology. Furthermore, the necessary requirements and prerequisites will be defined. In point C, through case studies, we will test what was structured in the previous phases through concrete models. Figure 1 and Table 1, clearly illustrates the entire path. It includes an initial phase WP1, further divided into WP1.1 and WP1.2, which aim to identify the research area, gaps, structure the research project and formulate the research question. Afterwards, we have a phase WP2, also divided into two sub-paths, WP2.1 and WP2.2. The aim of this part is to answer the question through the creation of case studies and operational workflows to identify classes that allow developing guidelines and vocabularies. Finally, in the final phase WP3, we will compare the results with the current state, develop conclusions and current limitations. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Tab. 1 Project Research: Tasks, Output and Methodologies - Workplan (A, B, C). PROJECT RESEARCH Y. A B C TASKS Collection and analysis of the existing literature on technologies applied to decision-making processes with the BIM method. Structuring, analyzing and studying case studies of containers, trying to organize the workflow as already happens in BIM speaking of BIM Autoring, i.e. through objects, attributes and relationships. Comparison of the results obtained with the current state to define information contents and tools that allow you to regulate and set immersion levels and classes during collaborations with technologies. OUTPUT Identification of gaps in the literature, - identification of the areas of intervention, - structuring of the research project, - Research question formulation. -Surveys and case studies to understand and develop improvements and address gaps, - greater clarity of the information levels necessary in processes, - easier sharing in environments. - Definition of reference models for virtual worlds (informative container + container). Evaluation and Analysis of the results obtained, - Comparison with the previous state, - Discussions and recommendations based on the results obtained, - Limitations of the search. METHODOLOGIES PRISM Protocol, Scientometric analysis, - SWOT Analysis, - Literature review. Analysis and evaluation of national and international standards, industry protocols and guidance documents, - Participation in conferences and seminars, - Participation in workshops, - Laboratory tests. Promotion and dissemination, - testing and evaluation, - involvement of stakeholders on the results obtained, - interviews with professionals to test the results (RUP, etc.), - analysis for future developments and directions. ................................................................................................................................ 207 Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 1 INTRODUCTION Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 1.1 Context and Motivation Administrative procedures in the construction sector are characterized by high levels of technical, organizational, and informational complexity. These procedures typically involve multiple stakeholders with heterogeneous roles, competencies, and institutional responsibilities, making effective collaboration, shared understanding, and informed decision-making particularly challenging. In such contexts, administrative processes are not merely regulatory steps, but critical moments in which divergent perspectives must be aligned and consensus must be reached. To function effectively, administrative procedures therefore require not only compliance with regulatory requirements, but also adequate support for decision-making and consensus-building among stakeholders. Communication and information flows play a central role in this regard. A continuous, structured, and comprehensible flow of information throughout the construction supply chain is widely recognized as a key factor in improving cooperation, reducing uncertainty, and generating added value for both clients and end users [1,2]. However, the fragmentation of the construction sector and the coexistence of heterogeneous technical and non- technical competencies often result in operational inefficiencies and a limited shared understanding of information [3]. These issues are particularly evident in public administrative procedures, where stakeholders are legally and operationally allowed to interpret and implement digitalization in markedly different ways. This complexity is further amplified when innovative methodologies such as Building Information Modeling (BIM) are introduced in contexts where digital culture and practices are not yet uniformly consolidated. Over the last two decades, BIM has reached a significant level of maturity, particularly in technical and design-oriented phases of the construction process. However, this maturity has not been equally reflected in administrative and authorization phases. In the Italian context of the Conferenza dei Servizi, for instance, BIM integration remains limited and uneven. Similar patterns can be observed in international research on e-permitting and automated compliance checking, where experimental applications exist but their incorporation into institutional and regulatory structures remains fragmented, especially in public-sector procedures characterized by multi- stakeholder decision-making [1]. In recent years, advanced representation technologies have been increasingly investigated for their potential to support understanding, interaction, and collaboration throughout the construction project lifecycle [4]. These technologies range from non-immersive visualization tools to highly experiential representation, and are characterized by their ability to engage users through interactive and experiential forms of representation. In this research, the term representation technologies is adopted as an overarching conceptual category to describe representation systems designed to support decision-making and collaboration through user experience. Within this category, technologies such as Virtual Reality, Augmented Reality, Mixed Reality, and other immersive or extended systems are considered specific technological instances, characterized by different levels of immersion, interaction, and integration with physical environments. The emphasis on the experiential dimension does not aim to downplay the central role of visual perception, which is widely acknowledged as a dominant sense in information processing. Rather, it seeks to highlight how representation systems can be structured beyond predominantly visual outputs in order to support deeper cognitive engagement, interpretation, and collaborative decision-making in complex and multi-stakeholder contexts. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Within this broader spectrum, approaches such as Serious Gaming have also been explored, particularly for learning, scenario exploration, and stakeholder engagement. However, the focus of this research is primarily on experiential representations explicitly designed to support understanding, collaboration, and decision-making within administrative procedures, rather than on game mechanics themselves. In this sense, structured and understandable information has been shown to directly influence supply chain performance, and several studies suggest that information technologies and innovative methodologies can act as levers for improving productivity in the construction sector [5,6]. Despite their demonstrated potential, the effective adoption of representation technologies in professional and institutional contexts remains limited. The literature consistently highlights that the mere introduction of advanced technologies, in the absence of clear methodological, organizational, and regulatory frameworks, is insufficient to ensure their diffusion and effective use [5]. As a result, representation systems often remain confined to experimental or isolated applications, struggling to achieve systematic integration into professional workflows. Their effective exploitation depends on adequate organizational, procedural, and managerial implementation strategies [2]. In this perspective, representation technologies could build upon the consolidated information architecture provided by BIM, extending its potential beyond data organization toward enhanced user-oriented understanding and interaction. Nevertheless, although legislation mandates the use of BIM for public works in several contexts, such obligations are typically linked to specific contractual thresholds, and BIM adoption remains predominantly confined to design and execution phases. Consequently, BIM still offers limited support to the decision-making phases of administrative procedures. This situation reveals an unresolved gap: the lack of structured methodological and technological support for decision-making processes within administrative procedures, which represent a critical yet under-supported phase of the construction process [7]. Integrating BIM as an information architecture with representation technologies as tools for understanding and interaction implies a profound transformation of organizational processes, introducing new challenges for all stakeholders involved. Public organizations, in particular, are frequently characterized by difficulties in adopting and fully leveraging new technologies and collaborative methodologies. These difficulties are not solely technological, but also organizational and cultural, and are closely related to the complexity of modifying established processes and workflows. Several studies emphasize that no technology can generate concrete benefits without a parallel adaptation of management and strategic processes, and that change management and innovation implementation remain largely unsystematic in the construction sector [2,8]. Implementing an experiential, BIM-oriented approach as a form of process innovation therefore requires meticulous planning and a coordinated strategy capable of integrating technological, organizational, and methodological dimensions [5,9]. As highlighted by Bille et al. [10], the introduction of methodologies such as BIM is not merely a matter of technological infrastructure, but also necessitates the adoption of well-defined frameworks capable of supporting organizational transformation. Focusing exclusively on technological or instrumental aspects is insufficient to unlock the full potential of representation systems [2,11]. Against this background, a clear need emerges for a structured framework capable of systematically capturing and organizing both information-related and experiential requirements. Such a framework would support the effective integration of representation technologies into administrative procedures, aligning technological capabilities with Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management organizational processes, stakeholder needs, and information management practices. This research is positioned within this gap, aiming to contribute to a more coherent and methodologically grounded integration of representation systems into collaborative decision-making processes in the construction sector. 1.2 Research problem Digital technologies, ranging from conventional information systems to advanced immersive and interactive solutions, are widely recognized in the literature for their potential to support data interpretation, knowledge exchange, and multi-stakeholder communication throughout the project lifecycle. Their contribution is frequently associated with improved coordination and management in complex AEC contexts [5]. Despite this acknowledged potential, their effective implementation in professional and institutional practices remains limited, due to a combination of technological, organizational, and, above all, methodological shortcomings [12]. A critical issue emerges in the way representation is commonly interpreted and operationalized. In many current practices, representation is predominantly reduced to visualization, overlooking a broader set of experiential dimensions that play a fundamental role in information interpretation, cognitive engagement, and collaborative processes [4]. This instrumental and primarily visual interpretation constrains the ability of digital systems to support decision-making, particularly in contexts characterized by complexity and stakeholder heterogeneity. The persistent gap between the technological potential of digital and experiential systems and the benefits actually achieved in practice can be largely attributed to systemic difficulties in organizational adoption [9]. The introduction of new technologies affects not only tools, but also representation, communication, and collaboration mechanisms, thereby impacting the entire socio-technical infrastructure of Information Management processes [13]. Both public administrations and private organizations often struggle to adapt organizational structures, workflows, and professional cultures to evolving technological paradigms, encountering resistance to change and limited strategic alignment [14]. There is a tangible risk of reproducing the shortcomings observed during earlier phases of construction sector digitalization, when tools and methodologies were adopted in a fragmented and poorly coordinated manner. Such approaches generated interoperability issues, inefficiencies, and implementations driven more by regulatory compliance than by genuine strategic intent [1,15]. In this sense, the adoption of representation technologies risks following a similar trajectory if not supported by a coherent methodological foundation. One of the underlying causes of these criticalities lies in a partial or distorted understanding of representation technologies. They are frequently framed as standalone visualization tools, rather than as components of integrated information, communication, and interaction strategies [16]. Consequently, their implementation is often limited to isolated applications, tied to specific projects or representational outputs, instead of being conceived as a systemic innovation requiring alignment with Information Management and Project Management processes [17]. Although the scientific literature provides a growing number of case studies highlighting both opportunities and challenges associated with immersive and experiential systems, there remains a relative lack of systematic research addressing their adoption dynamics, organizational implications, and methodological requirements at a comprehensive level. In particular, limited attention has been devoted to defining how representation technologies should be configured, managed, and integrated to support structured decision-making and collaboration across heterogeneous stakeholder groups. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management In response to this gap, the present research reframes representation technologies not as advanced visualization tools, but as a representation and communication methodology characterized by specific rules, requirements, and procedural implications. From this perspective, their value lies in their capacity to generate measurable impacts on technical understanding, organizational coordination, and collaborative decision-making processes, rather than in their technological sophistication alone. Achieving such outcomes requires the availability of well-defined, shared, and operational guidelines capable of supporting strategic planning and coherent management approaches for the integration of representation systems within organizational workflows [12]. However, to date, there is limited consensus on how these processes should be conducted, particularly with regard to managerial, procedural, and decision-making dimensions [14,18,19]. This lack of structured guidance not only increases the risk of underutilization or failure, but also hinders the diffusion and institutional legitimacy of these technologies. These issues become especially critical in administrative procedures, where diverse stakeholders with heterogeneous technical, managerial, and institutional competencies are required to collaborate in evaluating and authorizing complex projects. In the absence of structured processes that facilitate shared understanding and coordinated decision-making, representation systems struggle to realize their full potential, namely the democratization of access to technical information, enhanced process transparency, and improved quality of collective decisions. The synthesis of the issues outlined above highlights the need for a rigorous investigation into the challenges associated with the adoption and integration of representation technologies in the construction sector. In parallel, it underscores the necessity of systematically analyzing existing practices and emerging approaches in order to inform the development of structured methodological guidance capable of supporting their effective use in administrative and multi-stakeholder decision-making contexts [15]. 1.3 Research Aims, Questions, and Objectives The overall objective of this research is to define and develop an organizational and methodological framework that enables the use of structured information configurations capable of supporting experiential representation environments within organizational contexts in the construction sector. This, in turn, is intended to support stakeholder participation, engagement, and shared understanding. The research focuses specifically on administrative procedures, such as Service Conferences, in which technical evaluations are closely intertwined with organizational, decision-making, and communication dimensions. In these contexts, heterogeneous stakeholders with different roles, expertise, and institutional responsibilities are called upon to collaboratively evaluate complex projects, often in the absence of structured mechanisms that facilitate shared understanding and balanced participation. The goal is therefore to develop a model capable of making these procedures more understandable, transparent, and accessible by explicitly linking information structures, representation methods, and user experience. In this context, the research investigates how structured information representation can influence collaborative dynamics and the quality of decision-making in public sector administrative processes. Particular attention is paid to the legislative and regulatory framework governing the digital transformation of administrative procedures, Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management with a focus on the diffusion of information management practices and their integration into construction process data management structures. This analysis aims to clarify how organizational and regulatory conditions interact with representation strategies and Representative Ecosystems5. On this basis, the research is guided by the following overarching research question: What effects does a structured information configuration have on the integration and effectiveness of representation systems in collaborative and decision-making processes, particularly within administrative procedures such as Service Conferences? This overarching research question leads to three interconnected sub-questions: RQ1 – Framework Structure: How can information requirements, prerequisites, and organizational conditions be systematically structured to enable effective Representative Ecosystems in collaborative and decision-making processes? RQ2 – Implementation Effects: What measurable effects does the adoption of a structured framework have on the quality of collaboration, the effectiveness of the decision-making process, and the overall stakeholder experience? RQ3 – Professional Adoption: What factors influence the acceptance, usability, and effectiveness of structured and experience-oriented representation solutions among the various professional profiles involved in administrative procedures? To answer these research questions, the study pursues the following specific objectives: To identify the main informational, organizational, and technological factors that influence the effective implementation of experiential representation approaches in construction industry processes, with particular reference to administrative and decision-making contexts. To develop a structured, step-by-step, and replicable framework for integrating experiential dimensions into Information Management and Project Management processes related to administrative procedures. To evaluate, through empirical investigations and concrete case studies, the impacts, benefits, and critical issues associated with the application of structured information representation approaches in real-world administrative contexts. Formulate operational, methodological, and management recommendations to support the future adoption, evolution, and institutionalization of representation systems within public sector practices. 5 Representative Ecosystem refers to a structured socio-technical system in which information models, representation methods, technological tools, and users are intentionally configured to support understanding, interaction, and collaborative decision-making. In this research, the Representative Ecosystem is not defined by the specific technology adopted (e.g., VR, AR, MR, XR), but by the explicit structuring of informational, procedural, and experiential requirements that govern how information is represented, accessed, and interpreted by different stakeholders within organizational and administrative processes. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 1.4 Significance Statement This research examines current approaches to digitalization in the construction sector by focusing on the integration of information management, project management, and experiential representation within administrative and collaborative decision-making processes. It frames experiential technologies not as standalone visualization tools, but as components of structured decision-support configurations that interact with organizational processes and information flows. From a methodological perspective, the research proposes a structured framework for configuring experiential representations based on explicitly defined informational, procedural, and experiential requirements. Rather than prioritizing the technical characteristics of representation technologies, the framework emphasizes the role of structured configuration in supporting user interaction, collaborative processes, and decision-making activities. This approach responds to patterns of fragmented and case-specific adoption of experiential solutions observed in both professional practice and public sector contexts. From a conceptual perspective, the research adopts an ecosystem-oriented approach that moves beyond traditional object-centric representation models commonly associated with geometry-based information systems. Representation is conceptualized as the outcome of interactions between users, digital entities, and experiential contexts, rather than as a static projection of information. This perspective provides an analytical framework for examining how digital environments can support shared understanding and interaction among multiple stakeholders in complex decision-making settings. The relevance of the research is reflected in its focus on administrative procedures characterized by regulatory constraints, institutional heterogeneity, and the need to coordinate diverse actors. By addressing these conditions explicitly, the proposed framework is examined in contexts where decision-making processes depend on transparency, traceability, and collaborative alignment. Overall, the research contributes to scholarly discussions on digital transformation in the construction industry by examining the relationships between information structuring, experiential representation, and organizational decision-making processes. It also outlines directions for future empirical applications, comparative analyses, and policy-oriented discussions concerning the role of structured representation systems in public sector governance and collaborative project environments. 1.5 Thesis Organization This research develops and validates a methodological framework for integrating experiential representation into the decision-making and collaborative processes that characterize the construction industry. The framework is designed to support both traditional and advanced forms of representation within a coherent experiential and information management perspective. To achieve this goal, an approach based on Design Science Research Methodology [20], was adopted for its suitability for addressing complex real-world problems through the design, development, and validation of an innovative artifact, in this case the proposed framework (see Figure 2). This methodological orientation enables a structured and iterative connection between problem identification, Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management requirements definition, artifact design, and empirical evaluation, ensuring that the proposed solution is both theoretically grounded and applicable to real-world operational contexts. The thesis is organized into eight chapters, each of which progressively contributes to the construction, validation, and discussion of the research artifact. Chapter 1: introduces the research context, frames the problem, defines the objectives and research questions, and outlines the overall design of the study. Chapter 2: presents a critical synthesis of the state of the art on representation and reports the results of a systematic literature review aimed at identifying key research gaps and the theoretical foundations relevant to the development of the framework. Chapter 3: describes the methodological approach adopted, illustrating the rationale behind the choice of Design Science Research as the study's cornerstone and outlining the logical process guiding the design and evaluation of the artifacts. Chapter 4: constitutes the theoretical core of the thesis and introduces the research artifact, namely the framework for structured experiential representation. The chapter describes its conceptual foundations, including prerequisites, requirements, and implementation principles. Chapter 5: presents the first empirical validation of the framework through the Borgo Pirelli case study, with particular attention to the collaborative dimension of administrative procedures and multi-stakeholder interaction. Chapter 6: describes the second empirical validation, based on the Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata case study, focusing on the decision-making dimension and examining how structured information and experiential representation support evaluation and deliberation processes. Chapter 7: discusses and summarizes the findings of the empirical investigations in relation to the three research questions, ensuring coherence between the research design, data analysis, and interpretation phases. Chapter 8: concludes the thesis by summarizing the main theoretical and practical contributions, highlighting the implications of the framework for structured representation and information management, and outlining directions for future research. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 2 LITERATURE REVIEW Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 2.1 Representation in the AEC Industry Looking at the current landscape of the construction industry, it is evident that digital transformation is progressively reshaping how projects are conceived, managed, and delivered [21]. Within this transformation, representation technologies have played a central role, evolving from early digital drafting tools to advanced systems capable of supporting interpretation, communication, and collaboration across increasingly complex project environments [9]. The digital transformation process began with tools such as CAD, which introduced the first forms of project digitalization, and has since evolved toward scenarios that were once considered unthinkable. While the broader digital transformation of the AEC sector involves a wide range of emerging technologies such as Artificial Intelligence, the Internet of Things, Blockchain, and platform-based environments this research focuses specifically on representation technologies, and in particular on experiential forms of representation, due to their direct impact on information interpretation, collaboration, and decision-making processes [12]. Disciplines that once occupied marginal roles, such as Life Cycle Analysis (LCA), Digital Twin technologies, and applied robotics, are now increasingly integrated into design and management processes [22]. This evolution is not the result of a technological trend in isolation, but rather reflects a dynamic process in which innovation generates new operational needs, which in turn stimulate further technological development. Several empirical studies have shown that this dynamic is particularly evident in the adoption of representation systems, especially when technologies such as Virtual and Augmented Reality are integrated within BIM-based environments [21,23]. For example, Jung and Whyte [24] demonstrate how immersive environments support collaborative design review processes by fostering shared understanding among heterogeneous stakeholders. Similarly, Wang et al. [21] and Du et al. [23] report measurable improvements in communication effectiveness, error detection, and decision- making efficiency through VR-based workflows applied to complex project scenarios. Taken together, these contributions suggest that the impact of representation systems on collaboration and decision-making extends beyond enhanced visual fidelity. Rather than being limited to improved visualization, their effectiveness lies in the ability of experiential forms of representation to structure shared experiences, mediate interaction dynamics, and support the collective interpretation of project information within collaborative decision-making contexts. In this sense, representation acts as an enabling mechanism that links information content with the cognitive and social processes involved in collective decision-making. However, despite these demonstrated benefits, the majority of existing studies identified in the literature remain focused on specific technological implementations or isolated use cases [10,25]. Limited attention is devoted to understanding how such effects can be systematically reproduced, scaled, and governed within organizational and administrative processes. As a result, the causal relationship between information structuring, experiential representation strategies, and decision-making outcomes is often left implicit, rather than being formalized through methodological or managerial frameworks. This limitation becomes particularly critical in administrative and multi-stakeholder procedures, where decision- making depends on shared understanding, traceability of information, and the ability to align heterogeneous perspectives within constrained procedural frameworks. Consequently, representation systems can be interpreted as components of a broader cycle of continuous innovation within the AEC sector, in which increasing project complexity amplifies the need for integration, coordination, and informed decision-making. Yet, without structured approaches that explicitly connect representation strategies to information management and process design, this potential remains only partially realized. Despite this potential, the digitalization process still faces Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management structural and cultural challenges that slow its systemic adoption. Analyses conducted by the McKinsey Global Institute highlight how the AEC sector remains among the slowest industries in terms of digitalization, revealing ample room for growth and a fertile ground for scientific investigation. In this context, a growing interest can be observed in the adoption of Information Management solutions and representation systems, whose application is increasing both quantitatively and qualitatively [9]. Given the terminological ambiguity highlighted in the literature, this research adopts a precise and consistent conceptual vocabulary. The term representation technologies is used as a general descriptor for digital systems that support information interpretation and communication. Within this category, the expression experiential representation is employed to denote representations explicitly designed to engage users through interaction, perception, and cognitive involvement in collaborative and decision-making processes. Technologies such as Virtual Reality, Augmented Reality, Mixed Reality, and other immersive or extended systems are therefore treated as technological instances or implementation modalities, rather than as distinct conceptual categories. Finally, the notion of Representative Ecosystem is introduced to describe the systemic configuration in which users, digital entities, and experiential contexts interact within structured information and organizational processes. A further critical issue concerns the absence of shared standards and consolidated operational methodologies for defining, managing, and validating representation systems in the AEC sector. This gap, widely acknowledged in both academic research and professional practice, results in fragmented implementations and hinders the systematic adoption of representation technologies within decision-making processes [9,13]. Unlike fields such as medicine or gaming, where experiential technologies have followed more structured and progressive integration paths, the AEC industry still suffers from a significant methodological delay [25]. This gap is not merely technical, but reflects deeper organizational and cultural challenges related to the ability to understand, structure, and strategically apply representation technologies. Current studies tend to focus predominantly on technological and performance- related aspects, while largely neglecting the procedural and managerial dimensions of digital integration [25]. This scenario of methodological fragmentation complicates the integration between structured information management methodologies and representation systems. However, it is precisely in this convergence that one of the most promising challenges for the future of the sector lies: the possibility of connecting the logic of information management with the potential of experiential representation, thereby generating new models of collaboration and decision-making based on shared and comprehensible experiences. The conjunction between information management and experiential representation should not be understood as a simple technological overlap, but rather as a process of redefining design and management paradigms [12]. The current lack of integration between collaboration practices and decision-making systems, particularly in administrative procedures, contributes to delayed adoption and uneven digitalization across the sector. Considering these considerations, this research aims to develop a structured framework and corresponding application case studies that support the integration of experiential representation within information and project management processes. By focusing on administrative procedures as a critical context for multi-stakeholder decision-making, the study seeks to investigate how structured configurations of representation systems can enhance collaboration, transparency, and the overall quality of decision-making processes. The research is therefore guided by the following question: What are the effects of a structured configuration on the integration of representation systems in collaborative and decision- making processes, particularly in administrative procedures such as service conferences? The dimensions and Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management representational aspects introduced in this section recur across the reviewed literature and are further examined and consolidated through the systematic review presented later in this chapter. 2.1.1  Evolution of representation in the AEC sector In the AEC sector, representation has traditionally played a predominantly descriptive and documentary role, supporting the communication of technical solutions through drawings, reports and documentation compliant with regulations. For decades, effective representation has largely been associated with accuracy, completeness, and adherence to established standards and norms. Although these aspects remain essential, a growing body of research suggests that, in complex and multi-stakeholder project environments, the success of decision-making processes depends not only on the availability of correct information, but also on the ability of stakeholders to interpret, contextualize and collectively understand such information within specific procedural and organizational contexts [3,9,12]. This change has progressively expanded the role of representation from a passive means of providing information to an active support for interpretation, communication and decision-making. In administrative procedures, where the collaboration of heterogeneous actors with different technical backgrounds and institutional responsibilities is required, representation increasingly acts as a mediator between structured information and shared understanding. In this perspective, representation is no longer limited to visual output, but becomes a process through which information is translated into cognitively accessible forms that support evaluation, discussion and coordination. Over the past two decades, technological advances have significantly transformed representation practices in the AEC industry. Improvements in data management, interoperability and visualization performance have enabled the transition from static representations towards interactive and navigable digital environments [26,27]. These developments have facilitated the involvement of non-technical stakeholders and reduced long-standing barriers to participation, particularly in contexts where spatial reasoning and scenario evaluation play a central role [28]. Consequently, representation systems have been increasingly explored as tools to improve collaboration and support decision making at different stages of the project life cycle [17]. Recent literature documents a wide range of applications of advanced representation systems, including model- based information management [29], safety training [30], construction planning [31], and collision detection [32]. Other studies focus on collaborative environments where multiple stakeholders interact with shared digital representations, highlighting improvements in communication effectiveness, coordination and mutual understanding [33]. Further contributions explicitly address decision-making contexts, showing how interactive representations can reduce interpretative ambiguities, support the evaluation of alternatives and improve the quality of collective judgments in complex design scenarios [33]. Despite these advances, a recurring limitation emerges from the literature: most contributions focus on specific technologies or isolated use cases, while relatively limited attention is dedicated to how representation strategies can be systematically integrated into organizational and procedural workflows [1]. In particular, the relationship between information structuring, representation methods and results of the decision-making process is often assumed rather than explicitly formalized [4]. This limitation becomes particularly critical in administrative procedures, where information traceability, procedural clarity and alignment between heterogeneous perspectives are essential requirements. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management From a conceptual point of view, representation technologies in the AEC sector are characterized by significant terminological ambiguity. Terms such as virtual reality, augmented reality, mixed reality, immersive technologies and, more recently, Metaverse-related environments are often used interchangeably, despite referring to different technological configurations and interaction paradigms [34]. In this research, these technologies are treated as modes of implementation within a broader category of representation, rather than as distinct conceptual domains. The analytical focus is therefore placed not on the specific technology adopted, but on how the representation systems are configured to support interpretation, interaction and decision tasks within structured information and administrative processes. Within this evolution, increasing attention has been paid to what is often described as multidimensional representation. In this research, multidimensionality does not coincide with a purely multisensory approach. Rather, it refers to the integration of multiple representational dimensions, such as spatial configuration, information content, interaction mechanisms, temporal dynamics, and experiential involvement, which jointly contribute to users' understanding of complex project information [34]. Multisensory engagement, involving sensory channels beyond vision, is considered a possible but not mandatory component of this multidimensional configuration. Its adoption depends on specific task objectives, procedural requirements and cognitive needs of the stakeholders, rather than being assumed as an intrinsic or universal requirement [30]. Likewise, claims regarding full-scale or 1:1 representation require careful qualification. While some immersive setups allow users to experience spatial relationships at a large-scale reference level, other XR setups, such as desktop-based environments, holographic tables, or shared displays, provide mediated or scaled representations. These configurations still support interpretation and decision making, but through different forms of spatial fidelity and interaction [12]. Consequently, the effectiveness of the representation should be assessed in relation to its intended use in administrative tasks, rather than in terms of technological immersion alone. Overall, the evolution of representation in the AEC sector reflects a gradual transition from technology-driven experimentation towards the need for methodologically grounded integration. The increasing complexity of information environments, combined with the demands of collaborative and administrative decision making, highlights the need for structured approaches that can align representation strategies with information management principles and procedural requirements. This evolution provides the foundation for the framework developed in the following chapters, which explicitly addresses how structured representation. can be systematically configured to support collaborative decision making in administrative procedures. 2.1.2 Integration of traditional and advanced representation methods into BIM workflows Over the past three decades, the AEC sector has experienced a substantial evolution in software tools and digital workflows dedicated to design, modeling, and coordination. This transformation has been strongly driven by the advent of Building Information Modeling and computational design, which have progressively streamlined information management and supported coordination and control across different phases of the project lifecycle [35]. Through the structured organization of data and models, BIM has contributed to addressing long-standing critical issues in the sector, including poor interdisciplinary coordination, inefficiencies in information exchange, and safety risks on construction sites [36]. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Academic research has played a significant role in supporting this transformation by investigating methods for improving interoperability, information consistency, and collaborative workflows. Among notable initiatives, the European H2020 BIM4EEB project, developed at the Polytechnic University of Milan, promotes semantic interoperability between tools and actors involved in building energy retrofitting processes, highlighting the importance of structured information exchange in complex multi-actor contexts [37]. These contributions reinforce the central role of BIM as an information backbone for coordinating heterogeneous data sources and stakeholders. In parallel, regulatory developments and international standards—such as ISO 19650, ISO 7817-1 (formerly UNI EN 17412-1), and ISO 16739-1:2018 (IFC)—have clarified many aspects related to information management, data exchange, and responsibility allocation within BIM-based processes. However, while these standards provide robust guidance on how information should be structured, shared, and managed, they do not explicitly address how information should be represented to support interpretation, interaction, and decision-making. As a result, representation within BIM workflows often remains confined to predominantly visual outputs, characterized by poorly measurable requirements and a weak methodological connection to information management and decision processes [7,15]. Consequently, the causal relationship between information structure, representation strategies, and decision-making outcomes remains largely implicit. The increasing availability of digital assets in the construction sector has led to information environments that are not only richer, but also significantly more complex and fragmented. As the volume, heterogeneity, and interdependence of digital information grow, these assets become progressively more difficult to interpret, particularly for stakeholders with heterogeneous professional backgrounds or limited digital literacy [38]. This condition highlights the need for process-oriented approaches capable of mediating between structured data and human understanding, transforming information into representations that are cognitively accessible and operationally meaningful within collaborative and decision-making contexts. Market analyses further confirm this trend. According to the IMARC Group report (2022–2027), the global BIM market reached a value of approximately USD 6.5 billion in 2021 and is expected to exceed USD 15 billion by 2027 [37]. Beyond indicating widespread adoption, this growth reflects a corresponding increase in the volume and complexity of information generated throughout the project lifecycle. The central challenge, therefore, is no longer limited to the availability of digital information, but increasingly concerns its structured representation and effective integration within collaborative and decision-making processes. In this context, several studies have explored the integration of BIM-based information with advanced and experiential representation approaches. Research by Saar et al. [39] demonstrates how augmented reality systems can support on-site information access and interpretation, while devices such as Microsoft HoloLens [40] and Trimble XR10 [41] have been investigated for their potential to facilitate intuitive interaction with BIM models. In line with the perspective introduced in the previous section, these approaches are not interpreted as technology- driven enhancements, but as alternative representational configurations aimed at improving information interpretation, spatial understanding, and decision-making effectiveness under specific task conditions. Cloud-based platforms, such as Autodesk Construction Cloud, further illustrate how traditional BIM workflows can be extended to support collaborative and distributed processes [42]. Experimental initiatives, including Autodesk Workshop XR (Oasis project), have demonstrated the possibility of interacting with a single digital model across both conventional and advanced representation environments, reducing content duplication and Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management enabling more fluid transitions between representational modalities. Similar experiments conducted at the Labora laboratory of the Polytechnic University of Milan explored shared visualization through holographic tables, enabling collective access to information parameters and fostering collaborative interpretation within controlled environments [43]. Despite these advancements, the integration of advanced representation methods into BIM workflows remains largely driven by technological opportunities rather than by structured methodological guidance. Emerging technologies such as Blockchain and Smart Contracts are occasionally proposed as complementary solutions for issues related to data ownership, traceability, and access control [44–46]; however, they remain outside the analytical scope of this research, as they do not directly address the core challenge of structuring representation to support understanding and decision-making within administrative and collaborative processes. A further unresolved issue concerns the definition and management of appropriate levels of detail (LOD) in experiential representation. Studies by Seo et al. [27] and Anifowose et al. [29] show that user perception, behavior, and interpretation vary significantly depending on scale, distance, and representational granularity. These aspects are still poorly integrated into BIM workflows, where LOD is primarily defined in relation to geometric and informational completeness rather than experiential and cognitive requirements. This limitation reinforces the need for representation requirements that explicitly address experiential and cognitive dimensions, beyond traditional notions of data completeness. In conclusion, while information management plays a fundamental role in structuring and governing data flows within BIM-based processes, it is not sufficient by itself to address the representational and experiential needs required to support collaborative understanding and decision-making. Current tools, although technically advanced, tend to lack structured references for describing interaction dynamics, user–object relationships, and experiential dimensions [7,15]. Addressing these limitations requires a broader methodological framework grounded in project management and information management principles, in which BIM operates as one component of an integrated system oriented toward structured experiential representation, rather than as a self- sufficient solution for supporting collaborative and administrative decision-making. 2.1.3  Collaboration and Decision-Making in Construction Collaborative decision-making processes play a critical role in the construction sector, particularly within administrative and institutional procedures that involve multiple stakeholders with heterogeneous roles, responsibilities, and technical backgrounds. In the Italian context, such processes are formally regulated through Law No. 241/1990 (Articles 14–14-quinquies) and subsequent amendments, including Legislative Decree 127/2016, which define the Conference of Services as a key mechanism for inter-institutional coordination and collective decision-making. Within this consolidated regulatory framework, the quality of decisions depends not only on procedural compliance, but also on the ability of stakeholders to access, interpret, and discuss complex project information in a shared and transparent manner. In this perspective, representation systems can assume a strategic role not merely as technological tools, but as mediators between structured information and collaborative understanding. When appropriately configured, experiential forms of representation support the interpretation of spatial, technical, and procedural information, enabling stakeholders to explore project alternatives prior to physical implementation. Rather than focusing on technological immersion per se, their contribution lies in facilitating shared interpretation, comparison of Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management alternatives, and informed deliberation within structured decision-making contexts. Several studies indicate that, when embedded within formalized processes, experiential representations have been shown to support discussion, evaluation, and traceability of design decisions [17,31]. Although interest in collaboration and decision-making within experiential environments has increased in recent years, the specific literature addressing these topics in the AEC sector remains fragmented. Existing studies often focus on isolated applications, specific project phases, or individual technologies, while limited attention is devoted to understanding how representation strategies can be systematically integrated into information flows, organizational procedures, and decision-making structures. This fragmentation highlights the need for research approaches that explicitly connect representation modalities with collaborative processes and procedural requirements, particularly in administrative contexts. Empirical evidence suggests that experiential representation systems influence not only technical aspects of representation, but also the social and cognitive dynamics of decision-making groups. Berg et al. [47] report increased stakeholder engagement and improved communication when collaborative tasks are conducted within shared digital environments, leading to more informed and negotiated decisions. Similarly, Sangiorgio et al. [48] demonstrate that interactive visualization of energy retrofit scenarios supports decision-making, especially when non-expert stakeholders are involved, by facilitating the comparison of complex alternatives and reducing interpretative asymmetries. Other studies further confirm the role of experiential representations in supporting collaborative evaluation and decision-making. Huang et al. [49] show that virtual prototypes enhance understanding of design consequences, while Berg et al. [50] highlight how interactive environments can be used for design review, enabling designers, clients, and institutional actors to navigate, comment on, and co-evaluate ongoing projects. These benefits are not inherently tied to a single technological configuration, but depend on how representation systems are aligned with the cognitive and procedural needs of the decision-making process. Different setups—ranging from immersive environments to mediated or shared displays—provide varying degrees of spatial fidelity and interaction, all of which can support decision-making when appropriately matched to task requirements. Despite these promising results, current research remains dispersed across a wide range of domains and application areas, including design [51], project coordination [52,53], clash detection [54], real estate [55], real- time simulations [56], healthcare [57,58], education [59], and sports [51]. While these studies provide valuable insights into the potential benefits of experiential representation, they often address domain-specific objectives and lack a unified methodological perspective. Contributions developed during the COVID-19 pandemic further demonstrated the potential of digital environments to support remote collaboration and decision-making [60], but also emphasized the absence of structured approaches capable of integrating these tools into formalized organizational and administrative processes. From a methodological standpoint, the literature shows that early studies on collaborative decision-making in virtual environments date back several decades [53]. However, only a limited number of recent contributions explicitly address the integration of information management principles with experiential representation strategies. While consolidated guidelines exist for the use of BIM within traditional digital workflows [61–63], comparable frameworks for structuring, managing, and validating representation within experiential environments are still largely missing in the AEC sector [23,64,65]. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management A further critical issue concerns the lack of a shared and consistent vocabulary. Concepts related to collaboration, interaction, decision support, and formal approval are often used interchangeably, making it difficult to distinguish between exploratory interaction, evaluative discussion, and binding decision-making processes. This ambiguity hampers the systematic integration of experiential representation within structured decision-making workflows, particularly in administrative contexts where traceability, accountability, and procedural clarity are essential. Addressing this gap requires the development of a structured and shared conceptual framework, capable of aligning representation strategies with information management principles and collaborative decision-making requirements in the construction sector. 2.1.3.1 Traditional Approaches to Administrative Decision-Making and Representation To assess the potential contribution of innovative and experiential representation approaches in administrative decision-making processes, it is first necessary to examine how such processes have traditionally been structured and supported from a representational and informational perspective. In the Italian context, administrative procedures in the construction sector are governed by a consolidated legal framework designed to ensure transparency, legality, and traceability of public action. These procedures are typically articulated into four main phases: the initiative phase, which defines the objectives and formal initiation of the procedure; the investigation phase, during which data, technical documentation, and institutional opinions are collected; the decision phase, which culminates in the issuance of a reasoned administrative act; and the phase of effectiveness, which ensures the implementation of the decision through formal checks, notifications, and enforcement measures (see Figure 3). Within this procedural structure, the investigation phase represents the core moment for coordination, evaluation, and negotiation among the involved actors. It is at this stage that the Conference of Services (Conferenza dei Servizi) assumes a central role as an institutional mechanism for inter-institutional coordination and collective decision-making. Traditionally, the process begins with the preparation and submission of extensive technical documentation, such as preliminary reports, feasibility studies, and sector-specific assessments, followed by the formal convocation of the competent authorities, including fire safety agencies, cultural heritage bodies, and environmental protection agencies (see Table 2 for the actors involved in the Conference of Services within the Municipality of Milan). During Conference sessions, the project proponent presents the proposal, and the participating institutions evaluate the submitted documentation. While this documentation was historically paper-based, it is now predominantly digital. However, despite this technological transition, information is still commonly exchanged through static and document-oriented formats, such as PDF files, two-dimensional drawings, and technical reports distributed via email or shared repositories. As a result, digitalization has primarily affected the medium of information exchange, rather than the underlying logic of representation and decision support. The outcomes of the Conference of Services are formalized in a final report that summarizes the positions expressed by the participating entities and establishes binding decisions. Although this model ensures procedural compliance and legal validity, several studies highlight its limitations with respect to information interpretation and representation. As noted by Caranta [8], digitalization has largely modernized operational modalities without substantially transforming the cognitive and organizational logic of administrative decision-making processes. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Representation remains predominantly anchored to a two-dimensional, descriptive paradigm, offering limited support for integrated interpretation and collective evaluation. Current practices, whether conducted through in-person meetings, videoconferences, or asynchronous document exchange, often constitute a direct digital transposition of analog workflows. Technical information is typically fragmented across multiple documents and disciplinary silos, making it difficult to reconstruct a coherent and integrated understanding of the project. This fragmentation generates interpretative ambiguities, hinders cross- disciplinary communication, and complicates the identification of spatial, functional, and procedural relationships among project elements. Therefore, decision-making processes may be affected by misunderstandings between institutions, contradictory requirements, and repeated review cycles [8,66]. From a decision-support perspective, the limitations of traditional approaches become particularly evident. The lack of structured and multidimensional representation mechanisms constrains stakeholders’ ability to perceive project implications in a holistic and shared manner. While the traditional administrative model remains formally effective from a regulatory standpoint, it exhibits weaknesses in terms of cognitive, communicative, and collaborative support. Representation primarily fulfills a documentary and descriptive function, rather than actively facilitating experiential understanding, interpretation, and collective evaluation of complex design and planning choices. This gap between procedural robustness and informational effectiveness provides the rationale for exploring alternative representation approaches capable of enhancing understanding, transparency, and decision quality within administrative procedures. Rather than questioning the legitimacy of established processes, such approaches aim to complement existing regulatory frameworks by introducing structured representational configurations that better support collaboration and informed decision-making in multi-stakeholder contexts. The analysis focuses on three interconnected areas that constantly emerge from the literature and regulatory sources: (i) BIM regulations and information management standards; (ii) standards relating to experiential and extended representation technologies; and (iii) legislative frameworks governing administrative and collaborative decision-making processes. The mapping presented in this section derives from a critical review of international standards, technical protocols and regulatory documents discussed in the literature [7,64]. Its purpose is not to introduce a research methodology, but to establish a coherent theoretical and normative baseline against which to evaluate the limitations of current Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management practices. The results of this synthesis are summarized in Table 3, which organizes the examined bibliographic references into analytical classes based on their primary scope and application domain. This comparative reading highlights a persistent asynchrony between technological innovation and regulatory or methodological consolidation. Although BIM has reached a relatively high level of maturity in terms of information management standards, the digitalisation of collaborative and decision-making processes, particularly around administrative procedures, remains limited and uneven [7]. This misalignment represents both a structural limitation and a strategic opportunity for the AEC industry, underscoring the need for approaches that go beyond data-centric models. From a technological point of view, standards such as ISO/IEC 23005 and IEEE 2888 represent important efforts towards the integration of physical and digital environments [1,3]. At the same time, initiatives promoted by organizations such as IEEE VR and Khronos Group, through protocols such as OpenXR and formats such as glTF, provide advanced technical solutions for the management of interactive and interoperable three-dimensional contents [2]. However, these standards were primarily developed with reference to industries such as gaming, entertainment and general simulation. As a result, they do not adequately address construction industry-specific requirements, particularly those related to information governance, process integration and decision support in professional and administrative contexts [12]. In contrast, information management standards such as ISO 19650 and UNI 11337 form the backbone of Information Management-based processes, focusing on data structuring, exchange and allocation of responsibilities [7]. While these standards recognize the potential role of digital simulations and advanced visualization, they do not provide operational guidance for defining, managing, or validating representation systems. Even recent developments such as ISO 7817-1 (formerly UNI EN 17412-1), which further formalize the levels of information need, remain focused on the completeness and reliability of data, without explicitly addressing the experiential, perceptual or interaction-related dimensions [15]. Consequently, the relationship between information structure, representation strategies and decision-making outcomes remains largely implicit [64]. In the context of administrative proceedings, article 3-bis of Law no. 241/1990 anticipates some central principles for contemporary digital transformation, including transparency, accessibility and electronic communication. If interpreted considering current technological capabilities, these principles can be considered enabling references for the integration of representation tools in collaborative administrative processes [8]. However, more than three decades after its introduction, a regulatory framework that defines explicit criteria, procedures and validation mechanisms for such tools is still missing, generating operational uncertainty and limiting systematic adoption [8]. Likewise, the New Public Contracts Code (Legislative Decree no. 36/2023) recognizes the strategic value of advanced digital technologies. Article 43 (Annex I.9, paragraph 12) introduces rewarding criteria for the adoption of innovative digital solutions, which represent a first recognition of the multidimensional representation of information within public procurement processes. However, standardized criteria for requesting, managing and evaluating representation services are still absent, limiting comparability and transparency. Within the literature and regulatory documents examined, it is therefore possible to distinguish two administrative contexts in which representation systems can be introduced: public procurement procedures and authorization or permissive processes. Although these contexts differ in procedural structure and decision-making logic, they share a common methodological limitation: the absence of structured and shared criteria for defining, requesting and evaluating structured representation solutions [7,12]. This limitation has concrete practical implications, as Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management heterogeneous and non-comparable proposals for representation systems may be submitted within administrative or procurement procedures without shared and objective evaluation parameters, thereby undermining transparency, consistency, and decision quality. Overall, the reviewed standards and regulations reveal a significant gap in the integration of representation systems within professional and institutional workflows. In particular, existing classifications of immersion levels, often grounded primarily in technological configurations and device typologies, offer limited support for assessing representation systems in relation to information interpretation, collaborative processes, and decision-making requirements [12]. Such classifications tend to emphasize hardware features, while overlooking contextual, informational, and procedural dimensions that are critical in administrative decision-making contexts. Addressing this gap requires a shift toward experiential information management approaches capable of extending established Information Management principles beyond data structuring alone, in order to explicitly account for representation, interaction, and decision-support requirements in complex administrative and multi-stakeholder processes [7].
