Within the AEC industry, these systems are progressively transforming the way information is represented, perceived, and interpreted, moving beyond traditional two-dimensional and monomedia representations [12]. Rather than functioning as simple visualization tools, representation systems reshape the relationship between information, users, and environments, influencing how spatial and technical data are understood and used within processes [60,71]. The relevance of representation systems emerges from their potential impact on operational and decision-making activities. By enabling interactive and experience-based forms of representation, these systems support deeper spatial understanding and facilitate the interpretation of complex project information. While representation can include multisensory components, the literature consistently indicates that its value in professional and administrative contexts is not tied to sensory stimulation per se, but to its ability to enhance cognitive engagement, awareness, and shared understanding among heterogeneous stakeholders [21,72,73]. To support conceptual clarity, representation approaches are often described in the literature through classifications that reflect increasing levels of interaction and integration between digital and physical environments (see Figure 4). Earlier forms of digital representation relied primarily on passive visualization, while newer systems enable active exploration, interaction, and contextual interpretation of information [10,74]. This evolution reflects a broader shift from representation as a static outcome to representation as an active component of decision support processes. In this context, the term immersive is commonly used to describe representation systems that extend or complement users' perception of the physical environment through digital content. While immersive experiences have historically been achievable through conventional screen-based systems, contemporary approaches increasingly rely on interactive three-dimensional environments supported by tracking and spatial computing technologies [29]. However, the degree of immersion achieved varies significantly depending on technological configurations, interaction paradigms, and intended use cases. The following factors are not introduced as a prescriptive or exhaustive framework, but as recurrent themes identified across heterogeneous strands of the literature, which inform the subsequent structuring of the research framework. Virtual reality (VR) is generally described in the literature as a fully digital experiential environment in which users can navigate and interact with spatial information, often associated with a strong sense of presence [74]. Augmented Reality (AR), (see Figure 5), on the contrary, superimposes digital content onto the physical environment, preserving the direct perception of real-world contexts and enriching them with contextual information. AR systems can rely on visual markers or operate through markerless approaches based on projection, geolocalization, or object recognition techniques [26,75,76]. Mixed Reality (MR) is commonly used to describe configurations in which digital and physical elements coexist and interact within a shared spatial context, supported by spatial mapping and tracking technologies [77]. Rather than representing a universally “more advanced” modality, MR can be understood as one of several approaches within a broader spectrum of hybrid representation configurations, the relevance of which depends on task requirements and contextual conditions. These modalities are often grouped under the umbrella term Extended Reality (XR), which Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management encompasses a continuum of representation approaches between purely physical and fully virtual environments (see Figure 6). In this research, XR is treated as a technological category rather than a conceptual lens. The analytical focus remains on how these technologies support representation in relation to information interpretation, collaboration, and decision-making, independent of specific devices or platforms. Emerging paradigms such as the Metaverse or web-based virtual environments are therefore considered potential technological contexts rather than primary drivers of the theoretical framework [46]. Within information management-based processes, representation systems create opportunities to integrate structured information with interactive and collaborative forms of understanding. When BIM is interpreted as a supporting framework for information management rather than exclusively as a modeling or visualization tool, it can provide semantically rich and coherent data capable of supporting advanced, causal, and not random representational environments. From this perspective, the technology does not replace BIM, but can extend its benefits. At the same time, the integration of BIM and advanced representation systems introduces challenges related to data compatibility, bidirectional information flows, system performance, scalability, and organizational adoption [12]. The literature highlights that, in the absence of structured guidelines and application standards, such integrations often remain fragmented and technology-driven. Addressing these challenges requires approaches that can align representation strategies with information management principles and process requirements. In the reviewed literature, several recurring factors emerge as critical for the effective integration of experiential representation systems into professional and administrative contexts. These include interoperability between platforms and data models, accessibility for heterogeneous user groups, learning curves associated with new interaction paradigms, and economic and environmental sustainability considerations. Rather than being introduced as predefined criteria, these factors represent synthesis elements derived from the literature and provide a conceptual basis for the methodological framework developed in the following chapters. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 6 Scheme of Immersive Reality: Virtual Reality, Augmented Reality, Mixed Reality, Extended Reality. 2.3 Systematic Review and Gap Analysis Despite the growing body of research on BIM, digital representation systems, and collaborative processes in the AEC industry, existing contributions remain highly fragmented. Much of the literature focuses on isolated technologies, specific applications, or limited phases of the project lifecycle, often without explicitly addressing the interdependencies between information management, representation strategies, and decision-making processes. As highlighted in the previous sections, these aspects are rarely examined in an integrated and systematic manner, particularly in relation to administrative and collaborative decision-making contexts. For this reason, a structured literature review was conducted to clarify the current state of the art, identify conceptual and methodological gaps, and establish a coherent theoretical foundation for the development of the proposed framework. The purpose of this review is not simply to provide a descriptive overview of existing studies, but to critically analyze how the relationships between information management, representation systems, and collaborative decision-making have been conceptualized and operationalized in the literature. In line with the research objectives and questions introduced in Chapter 1, three main thematic areas have been identified as the analytical backbone of the review: Building Information Modeling (BIM) and information management; Representation systems and experiential technologies; Decision-making and collaborative processes. Particular attention has been paid to the evolution of these fields and their intersections, with the aim of understanding how technological developments have been interpreted and applied in organizational, procedural, and administrative contexts. These three dimensions therefore define the scope of data collection and analysis and provide a structured lens through which to examine the literature. The review is explicitly oriented toward identifying limitations and discontinuities in existing research, rather than evaluating individual technologies or proposing immediate solutions. In this sense, the goal is to derive analytically grounded insights that can guide future research directions and support the formulation of a structured framework for structured representation in collaborative and decision-making contexts. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management To ensure transparency and rigor, the literature review process was structured into a sequence of analytical steps, summarized here and detailed in the following subsections. These steps do not constitute the study's research methodology, but rather describe the review protocol adopted to ensure consistency, traceability, and reproducibility of the literature analysis. The process includes: an initial exploration of the state of the art to identify key trends and recurring issues within the selected thematic areas; a structured data collection phase based on the definition and combination of relevant keywords and search strings; a quality assessment and screening phase, conducted using the PRISMA guidelines and complemented by a basic scientometric analysis to support transparency in inclusion and exclusion decisions; an interpretative synthesis of the reviewed contributions, aimed at identifying recurring strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, and limitations in current approaches; the derivation of research questions and analytical directions emerging from the identified gaps, which inform subsequent research phases. Taken together, these analytical steps establish a structured and focused review process, in which each choice is explicitly linked to the identification of research gaps highlighted in previous chapters. This approach provides the conceptual foundation necessary for developing a framework capable of supporting structured representation in collaborative and administrative decision-making processes. 2.3.1 Literature Collection and Analysis with the PRISMA Protocol Given the heterogeneity of existing studies, terminological overlap, and the lack of consolidated methodological frameworks explicitly addressing representation systems in administrative and collaborative decision-making contexts, it was necessary to adopt a transparent and reproducible review protocol (Figure 7). The goal of this phase was not only to gather relevant contributions but also to systematically examine their scope, focus, and limitations in relation to information management, representation strategies, and decision-making processes in the AEC sector. The systematic collection and analysis of the literature is a fundamental step in positioning this research within the existing body of knowledge. In this phase, the primary objective was to identify recurring themes, dominant approaches, and unresolved gaps regarding the use of digital and experiential representation systems to support decision-making and collaborative processes. Rather than evaluating individual technologies, the review sought to clarify how representation has been conceptualized, operationalized, and evaluated across different research streams. To structure this process, the review protocol was based on the approach proposed by Khan et al. [5], which provides a clear and replicable framework for conducting structured literature reviews. This framework was adopted to ensure transparency and consistency in source selection and evaluation. Furthermore, the work of Khan et al. inspired the subsequent interpretive synthesis through the use of the SWOT framework, which has been employed in previous studies to critically evaluate the strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, and limitations of immersive technologies in the AEC sector. In particular, Khan et al. have already highlighted the lack of shared standards and operational guidelines for collaborative virtual environments, offering an initial point of alignment with the research problem addressed in this study. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management In parallel, the work of Wang et al. [21] was used to support the identification of influential trends and methodological approaches in recent literature, particularly regarding collaborative and decision support applications. This contribution proved useful in clarifying terminological inconsistencies and supporting the definition of a more coherent research vocabulary. Preliminary analyses confirmed that similar concepts are often described using divergent or overlapping terms, reinforcing the need for semantic clarification and structured classification within the review process. Consequently, the literature review focused on contributions addressing collaborative and decision-making processes supported by digital, experiential, and virtual representation systems in the AEC industry. Studies related to BIM were also included, not as a primary focus, but as a necessary reference for understanding how structured information management supports representation and collaboration in digital workflows in the construction industry [23,67,79]. This inclusion reflects the role of BIM as an enabling information backbone rather than as a representation system per se. The results of this initial exploratory analysis highlighted a lack of comprehensive and systematic studies that explicitly address the integration of representation strategies, information management, and decision-making processes in a unified manner. Although numerous contributions examine individual technologies or applications, few adopt an integrated perspective capable of capturing organizational, procedural, and administrative dimensions. This fragmentation further justifies the need for a structured review to identify patterns, discontinuities, and unexplored areas. To organize the literature and ensure transparency in the selection process, the PRISMA (Preferred Reporting Items for Systematic Reviews and Meta-Analyses) protocol was adopted, in line with the recommendations of Khan et al. [5]. The protocol guided the literature review through three main phases: Identification, aimed at the initial collection of potentially relevant sources; Screening, conducted to exclude duplicates and contributions not aligned with the research scope; Inclusion, dedicated to the final selection and qualitative classification of the analyzed works. Fig. 7. Illustrates the PRISMA-based methodological flow adopted in this study, which structured the entire literature collection and analysis process and provides traceability for inclusion and exclusion decisions. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 2.3.1.1 Identification In the identification phase, an initial exploratory and intentionally broad search was conducted to delineate the conceptual boundaries of the research domain and to capture the diversity and fragmentation of existing contributions. This preliminary step was not aimed at exhaustively reviewing specific technologies, but at identifying recurring themes, terminological overlaps, and conceptual clusters related to representation systems, information management, and collaborative decision-making within and around the AEC sector. Relevant publications were identified across the AEC domain and closely related fields where experiential and digital representation systems have been applied. The following scientific databases and academic platforms were consulted to ensure comprehensive coverage: Google Scholar ACM Digital Library IEEE Xplore Scopus Web of Science ERIC ScienceDirect Semantic Scholar All retrieved references were archived in a dedicated Mendeley library, resulting in an initial corpus of 198 documents. These sources were provisionally classified into three thematic categories: (i) advanced and experiential representation systems; (ii) BIM and information management methodologies; and (iii) collaborative and decision-making processes. In addition to peer-reviewed journal articles, the exploratory phase included selected technical reports, standards-related documents, and academic multimedia resources when relevant to the research scope. This exploratory analysis served three main purposes: (i) identifying influential authors and research streams; (ii) highlighting preliminary thematic gaps; and (iii) supporting the definition of a coherent vocabulary for the subsequent structured search. To ensure terminological consistency, recurring keywords were grouped into three thematic areas (Table 4), integrating definitions from IEC Electropedia, an international technical reference dictionary [80], with terms emerging from the preliminary review. Following this exploratory phase, a structured search was conducted using Scopus, selected for its extensive coverage of peer-reviewed publications and advanced bibliographic metadata [21,78,81,82]. Scopus was employed as the primary database for the systematic review phase to ensure consistency, traceability, and replicability. The structured search was implemented using Boolean logic and the Scopus API [21], combining terms related to: the AEC sector, experiential and digital representation systems BIM and information management concepts collaborative and decision-making processes. The inclusion of broad technological terms (e.g., Metaverse, Blockchain, Artificial Intelligence, Internet of Things, Industry 4.0) in this phase was intentional and exploratory. These terms were not assumed to be directly Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management relevant to administrative decision-making, but were included to capture the breadth of the digital transformation discourse and to identify overlaps and ambiguities in how representation-related concepts are framed in the literature. Unlike the exploratory phase, which also considered domains such as healthcare, education, gaming, and sports, the structured search was explicitly limited to the AEC sector, to ensure relevance to the research objectives. The results were subsequently organized according to the three core thematic dimensions identified earlier. This process confirmed the limited presence of studies explicitly addressing structured decision-making and collaborative processes supported by structural representation systems in the AEC sector, thereby reinforcing the relevance of the identified research gap. Tab. 4 Parole chiave della ricerca strutturata. To ensure data quality and relevance, a multi-stage screening process was applied, combining automatic filtering and qualitative assessment. The following exclusion criteria were applied: Time filter: Only publications between 2010 and 2023 were included. This threshold was adopted to align the review with the consolidation of BIM standards, the emergence of interoperable information management frameworks, and the increasing digitalization of administrative and collaborative processes in the AEC industry. Previous studies, although conceptually relevant, were excluded from the systematic phase to ensure consistency with current regulatory and procedural contexts. Thematic filter: Only contributions explicitly related to the AEC industry were retained, excluding studies focusing on unrelated domains (e.g., entertainment, cultural heritage). Language filter: Only publications in English and Italian were included. • Duplicate removal: Automatic detection and removal of duplicate records in the databases. The screening process was supported by EndNote 20 software (trial version), which identified and removed 51 publications published before 2010 and 40 duplicate records (31 from Scopus and 9 from the exploratory search). After this initial data cleaning, the dataset was reduced to 353 records from the structured search and 183 from the exploratory phase. Subsequently, a qualitative relevance screening was conducted by analyzing the title, Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management abstract, and keywords. VOSviewer was used to verify semantic and thematic consistency among the remaining publications. Articles were excluded if: they addressed immersive or digital technologies without reference to collaborative or decision-making processes; they focused on visualization or simulation without explicitly considering information structures or process integration; they presented purely technical implementations without implications for organizational, procedural, or administrative contexts. At the end of this screening phase, 36 articles met the inclusion criteria defined by the PRISMA protocol and were selected for in-depth analysis. Tab. 5 Total documents analyzed through Scopus and grouped by area. 2.3.1.3 Inclusion The final corpus of 36 articles was subjected to an interpretative synthesis to support objective analysis and reduce researcher subjectivity. Rather than serving as a validation method, this phase aimed to structure the qualitative comparison of contributions and to identify recurring patterns and limitations within the literature. To this end, a SWOT analysis was applied to the selected articles, following approaches previously adopted in AEC-related reviews [5]. The SWOT framework was used as an analytical lens to compare how existing studies address representation systems in relation to collaboration, information management, and decision-making processes. This interpretative synthesis provided a structured basis for identifying conceptual gaps, methodological limitations, and underexplored dimensions in the literature. The outcomes of this phase informed the formulation of the research questions and directly supported the development of the reference framework presented in the subsequent chapters. 2.3.2 Scientometric Analysis of Research Trends on Representation Technologies and BIM in Collaborative and Decision-Making Processes Scientometric analysis was employed to support the exploration and interpretation of the scientific corpus identified through the PRISMA-based review process. This approach enables the mapping of publication trends, thematic concentrations, and relational patterns among concepts, authors, and keywords, complementing SCOPE QUERY Documents before screening Documents after screening AEC –  Industry advanced representation + (BIM) + Collaborative processes AEC OR "Architecture, Engineering and Construction" OR "Architecture" OR "Engineering" OR "Construction" AND "Immersive Technologies" OR "Immersive Realities" OR "Immersive Environments" OR "Virtual Environment" OR "Virtual Reality" OR "Augmented Reality" OR "Mixed Reality" OR "Extended Reality" OR "Metaverse" OR "Virtual model" AND "Building Information Modeling" OR "BIM" OR "Level of Detail (LOD)" OR " Level of Information Need (LOIN)" OR "Blockchain" OR "AI" OR "Gaming Engine" OR "IFC" OR "Facility Management" OR "Industry 4.0" OR "Internet of Things" AND " Decision conference " OR "Collaboration" OR "Decision Making" OR "Decision process" OR " Remote collaboration" OR "Service conference" OR "Design review" OR "Collaborative design". 429 24 Manual collection and cataloging (Mendeley) Not applicable (manual selection of relevant publications from reference lists, journals, or expert sources) 198 12 627 36 Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management qualitative analysis with quantitative indicators. Rather than replacing interpretative reading, scientometric methods provide an additional analytical lens for identifying dominant research streams and underexplored areas within complex and fragmented domains. Several tools can be used for this purpose, including VOSviewer, CiteSpace, and BibExcel, which are commonly adopted in AEC-related research to analyze topics such as construction management, interoperability, artificial intelligence, and BIM methodologies [83]. In this study, VOSviewer was selected for its suitability in keyword co-occurrence analysis and its widespread use in similar reviews. Following the screening phase, the resulting datasets—comprising 353 articles from the structured search and 183 from the exploratory search—were exported from EndNote 20 in .ris format and processed using VOSviewer. The analysis was configured with the following parameters: File type: Reference Manager files; Analysis type: Co-occurrence; Unit of analysis: Author keywords; Counting method: Full counting; Minimum occurrence threshold: 10. This threshold resulted in the identification of 35 keywords, which was considered sufficient to generate a readable and meaningful thematic map (Table 6). It should be noted that the selection of threshold values involves an element of arbitrariness, as no universally accepted criteria exist for defining minimum frequencies in keyword- based scientometric analyses [5]. The adopted configuration therefore reflects a balance between analytical clarity and representativeness. As illustrated in Figure 8, the data derived from the PRISMA protocol were used to generate a keyword co- occurrence network, enabling visualization of relationships among the main research topics. In the resulting diagram: node color represents the average publication period; node size reflects keyword frequency; distances between nodes indicate the relative strength of co-occurrence relationships. This representation supports the construction of a cognitive map of the research field, facilitating the identification of thematic clusters and potential gaps, rather than establishing causal relationships. The analysis reveals a dense network of interactions among key concepts related to BIM, representation technologies, and decision-making processes. Stronger co-occurrence relationships emerge between BIM and virtual reality (VR), as well as between BIM and decision-making, followed by notable connections between decision-making and AR/VR technologies. In contrast, weaker or less consistent linkages are observed between experiential representation environments and structured decision-making processes. This pattern suggests that, while representation technologies are frequently discussed alongside BIM, their integration into decision-making frameworks remains less explicitly articulated in the literature. This observation becomes particularly relevant when contrasted with the relatively mature standardization landscape surrounding BIM, including concepts such as Level of Development (LOD) and Level of Information Need (ISO 7817-1). The scientometric map suggests that comparable methodological and informational structures are less consistently associated with experiential representation technologies. Rather than indicating a lack of technological capability, this pattern reflects the absence of shared frameworks and evaluation Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management criteria capable of linking experiential representation to information governance and decision-making requirements. Another notable pattern concerns the proximity between BIM-related keywords and Mixed Reality (MR), compared to those associated with AR or VR. This proximity may indicate growing scholarly interest in hybrid representation environments that combine physical and digital elements. However, the relatively limited density of the MR cluster suggests that this research stream is still emerging and characterized by heterogeneous approaches, rather than by consolidated models or standards. These findings are consistent with observations by Bernal et al. [84], who emphasize ongoing concerns regarding the reliability and validation of results obtained in immersive and simulated environments. Similarly, Hammerschmid et al. [85] note that existing standards—such as IEEE 2888 and ISO/IEC 23005—primarily address information exchange within tools, while offering limited guidance on structural criteria for simulation and representation. To overcome this limitation and support a more interpretive reading of the selected studies, a SWOT analysis was adopted as a complementary analytical tool. Following the PRISMA-based selection and screening process, a corpus of 36 articles aligned with the objectives of this study was subjected to a SWOT synthesis. Rather than serving as an evaluative or prescriptive method, the SWOT analysis was used as a structured interpretive framework to compare how existing contributions address collaboration, representation, and decision-making processes in the AEC industry. The analysis was conducted on four key collaborative domains, synchronization, interaction, decision-making, and validation, identified in previous studies as critical dimensions of collaborative workflows in digital construction environments [86,87] (see Figure 9). Each article was analyzed using a standardized SWOT matrix, organized into predefined categories and subcategories. Furthermore, the objective of each contribution was classified as direct or indirect, depending on whether the study explicitly addressed collaborative or decision-making objectives. The aggregate results of this synthesis are summarized in Table 7, while Table 8 presents the consolidated SWOT matrix. Subsequent updates to the dataset did not produce substantial changes in the overall distribution of results, suggesting a stable pattern within the reviewed literature. The synthesis highlights several recurring trends. First, synchronization and interaction emerge as the most widely addressed domains, supported by established practices and a relatively mature set of tools and use cases. In contrast, decision-making, and particularly validation-related aspects, receive limited attention. The literature reveals a paucity of shared standards, metrics, and reproducible procedures capable of supporting structured evaluation and validation processes in advanced representation environments. A recurring theme in the studies reviewed is the lack of explicit information structures and formalized requirements capable of establishing a causal relationship between information content, representation strategies, Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management and decision outcomes. This gap impacts the clarity, timing, and transparency of collaborative decision-making processes, particularly in institutional and administrative contexts. Figure 9 illustrates this imbalance through a hierarchical representation of collaborative domains, highlighting the critical role of synchronization and interaction and the increasing complexity associated with decision-making and validation. Fig. 9 Hierarchical pyramid of domains according to collaborative processes for the AEC sector with ImT. The SWOT analysis-based synthesis suggests that, although the AEC industry has already pioneered credible solutions for coordination and interaction, the extension of representation systems to decision-making and validation remains conceptually and methodologically underdeveloped. Rather than indicating a lack of technological capability, this condition reflects the absence of structured criteria for defining requirements, integrating them with information management processes, and evaluating their contribution to decision quality. Several implications consistently emerge from the literature and can be interpreted as suggestions for further research rather than immediate operational prescriptions. These include the need to: explicitly correlate experiential aspects of representation to information requirements, supported by measurable indicators; improve interoperability and traceability between common data environments (CDEs), BIM models, and advanced representation environments; develop evaluation criteria and validation mechanisms that reduce subjectivity in experiential representations; explore approaches to integrate decision support and validation considerations into existing information management workflows. This could limit the effectiveness of technologies in the validation phase as it creates a disparity with the rest of the market, exposing one to a too narrow circle of providers who meet the requirements. 2.4 Summary The literature review and analyses presented in this chapter highlight a series of recurring limitations that currently limit the effective integration of information management and representation strategies into collaborative and Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management decision-making processes in the construction sector. These limitations are not primarily related to individual tools, such as BIM platforms or immersive technologies, but to the lack of structured connections between information, representation, and decision-making (see Table  9). A first critical issue concerns the lack of shared regulatory frameworks and standards for representation. Although BIM-based information management has reached a relatively mature stage, aspects related to representation remain poorly structured and difficult to evaluate through measurable criteria [23]. As observed by Berg et al. [47], this fragmentation limits the evaluation of the effectiveness of representation, particularly in decision- making contexts. A second limitation concerns interoperability between information systems. The translation of data into experiential environments is often manual and discontinuous, resulting in information loss and inefficiencies [39]. The persistence of proprietary formats and the limited adoption of open standards, such as USD or IFC [118], further hinder information continuity between data, models, and representation environments [97,119]. The third problem concerns the disconnect between data management and structured representation. Existing standards primarily address information exchange, neglecting the perceptual and cognitive dimensions of representation [58]. Consequently, digital models struggle to support interactive and causal representations capable of conveying decision-related information beyond mere geometry [42]. A fourth critical area concerns decision-making and validation processes. While synchronization and interaction are relatively well supported, decision-making and validation lack shared criteria and metrics. The absence of metrics for assessing the information quality of advanced and structured representations reduces their reliability as decision-support tools, particularly in administrative contexts [72,116]. Another recurring limitation is the conceptual ambiguity between information management systems and representation technologies. Tools are often treated as visual extensions of BIM, despite having distinct informational and experiential requirements [48]. Existing standards, such as IEEE 2888 and ISO/IEC 23005, focus on technical interoperability rather than structural representation criteria, leaving room for subjective interpretation. Finally, the literature highlights a lack of integrated skills that connect information management and representation. As observed by Berg et al. [47], current training programs rarely address these combined dimensions, resulting in operational difficulties and usability issues, including reduced cognitive interoperability and user discomfort [120]. The literature review highlights a set of recurring representational and experiential dimensions that, while discussed separately across different studies, lack a shared methodological structure. These gaps directly inform the research design and the development of the framework presented in Chapter 3. Tab. 9 synthesizes the logical and methodological transition between the outcomes of the systematic literature review and the research design adopted in this study, explicitly illustrating how identified gaps informed the definition of research questions and methodological choices within the Design Science Research framework. It describes the adopted methodological approach (Figure 10), which follows the principles of Design Science research methodology and develops progressively from a systematic literature review to empirical validation and critical discussion of the findings. The chapter presents the integrated protocol for data collection and analysis, which combines quantitative and qualitative methods, and clarifies how these tools are aligned with the research questions and objectives. While some operational and implementation aspects of the methodology are further detailed in the chapters dedicated to the framework description and case studies (Chapters 4, 5, and 6), this chapter establishes the overall research design, validation strategy, and methodological coherence of the study. Finally, the chapter concludes with a critical reflection on validity, reliability, limitations, and generalizability, providing a structured basis for evaluating the robustness and transferability of the proposed approach. Fig. 10 illustrates the Design Science Research framework adopted in this study, highlighting the iterative relationship between conceptual grounding, framework development, and empirical validation. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 3.2  Research Approach and Paradigm The overall research design follows a mixed-methods approach, combining quantitative and qualitative tools to comprehensively address the three research questions [121,122]. The methodological approach is guided by the principles of DSRM, which integrates theoretical development with empirical validation through the iterative design, implementation, and evaluation of a research artifact, as illustrated in Figure 10. The adoption of DSRM was motivated by the nature of the research problem addressed in this study. The research does not aim solely to describe or explain existing phenomena, but to design, develop, and validate a structured methodological framework capable of improving collaborative and decision-making processes in real administrative contexts. Alternative research approaches focused exclusively on empirical observation or hypothesis testing would not have been sufficient to address this objective, as the study requires the intentional construction and evaluation of an artefact, namely, a structured framework integrating information management and representation. Design Science Research provides a suitable methodological foundation for this purpose, as it explicitly supports the iterative linkage between problem identification, artefact design, and empirical validation. Moreover, DSRM is particularly appropriate for socio-technical research contexts characterized by complex interactions between information structures, technologies, and organizational actors, such as administrative decision-making processes in the AEC sector. By combining conceptual grounding with practical instantiation and evaluation, the methodology enables both scientific rigor and practical relevance, aligning with the dual objective of this research to generate transferable knowledge and actionable methodological guidance. Within this paradigm, the proposed framework is conceived as the primary research artifact resulting from the methodological process, rather than as a standalone technological or procedural solution. The DSRM framework provides the overarching logic through which the framework is developed, tested, and refined in different contexts. The research was structured into three main phases, each corresponding to a specific research question (RQ) and a distinct phase of the DSRM cycle: Commissioning Phase (RQ1): Identifying the prerequisites and information requirements needed to consistently integrate structured Representation Ecosystems into collaborative and decision-making processes; Utilization Phase (RQ2): Applying and testing the framework in controlled, real-world contexts, with the aim of measuring its impact on collaborative processes, effective decision-making, and stakeholder experience; Post-Use Phase (RQ3): Analyzing the factors influencing the acceptance, perception, and professional adoption of structured experiential solutions, with particular attention to differences between user categories and technological tools. The research questions stem from a systematic literature review conducted using the PRISMA protocol, complemented by scientometric analyses and a SWOT assessment. This phase identified key gaps in both the scientific literature and operational practice, particularly the lack of structured prerequisites and information requirements for representation in administrative and collaborative contexts. Based on these findings, the process was divided into subsequent phases: Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management an initial exploratory phase, based on questionnaires administered to professionals and academics, aimed at assessing awareness, expectations, and the perceived value of experiential representations in administrative procedures; a subsequent application phase, in which the framework was instantiated and validated through simulated and real-world case studies. Data collection followed an integrated strategy: qualitatively, through open-ended feedback and Plus/Delta analyses, to support the identification of strengths, weaknesses, and cultural or perceptual barriers. quantitatively, through Likert-scale questionnaires and statistical analyses (t-tests, ANOVA, regression models, and correlation analyses) targeting variables such as presence, immersion, engagement, usability, and project understanding; The case studies were selected based on three main criteria: relevance to administrative procedures, availability of federated digital models, and institutional and operational accessibility to data and stakeholders. The analysis of the results followed a structured sequence that included data coding and organization, statistical processing of quantitative data, and critical interpretation through triangulation of quantitative and qualitative evidence. Through this methodological approach, the research systematically explores how information management principles, structured experiential representation, and collaborative decision-making processes can be consistently aligned within a single methodological framework grounded in project and information management principles. 3.3 Linking Review to Research Objectives The literature review conducted in Chapter 2 highlighted how the integration of structured Representation Ecosystems into collaborative and decision-making processes in the AEC industry remains fragmented and largely disconnected from a coherent information management framework. Despite the regulatory and methodological consolidation of BIM within national and international standards (e.g., ISO 19650, ISO 7817, UNI 11337), academic research has paid little attention to the dynamic and experiential dimension of representation and its systematic relationship with information management. To address this gap, a systematic literature review was conducted following the PRISMA protocol, supported by scientometric analyses and a SWOT assessment, and based on authoritative academic sources (Scopus, Web of Science, IEEE Xplore, ACM Digital Library, Semantic Scholar, and Google Scholar). This process identified recurring conceptual patterns, critical gaps, and underexplored relationships between information structures, representation systems, and collaborative decision-making contexts. The analysis highlighted three main critical issues hindering the structured adoption of representations in the AEC industry: the lack of standardized information frameworks capable of supporting experiences. the absence of shared metrics for evaluating the effectiveness and impact of representations in supporting decisions, particularly in terms of information comprehension, quality of collaboration, and decision- making outcomes. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management the insufficient technical and methodological knowledge and preparation of professionals in the use and management of technologies. Rather than directly producing a predefined set of experiential dimensions, the systematic literature review provided a structured conceptual framework within which it was possible to identify recurring themes, partial constructs, and fragmented evaluation criteria related to experiential representation and decision support. The analysis highlighted how concepts such as presence, immersion, engagement, usability, and cognitive effects are frequently cited in the literature, albeit inconsistently and non-operationalized ways, often disconnected from explicit information management principles. These concepts were therefore not adopted as pre-packaged dimensions but were critically selected and subsequently conceptualized within the Design Science Research process. Through this iterative reasoning, the identified concepts were progressively reformulated as analytical constructs and aligned with the research objectives, forming the theoretical basis for the development of the proposed framework. Their operationalization into measurable variables and evaluation criteria was carried out in the subsequent methodological and empirical phases of the research. Based on this evidence, the overall research objective (Table 10) was defined as the development of a structured methodological framework for the management and implementation of representation systems within collaborative and decision-making processes, with reference to administrative procedures. The framework was conceived as a means of operationalizing the identified dimensions, translating them into explicit information requirements, experiential prerequisites, and measurable variables. This objective is addressed through the following overarching research question: What are the effects of a structured configuration on the integration of representation systems in collaborative and decision-making processes, particularly within administrative procedures? This question is divided into three interconnected sub-questions: RQ1 – Framework Development: How can information requirements and prerequisites be organized, according to information management principles, to enable the construction of coherent and functional representation ecosystems for collaborative and decision-making processes? RQ2 – Implementation Effects: What measurable effects does the application of the framework have on the effectiveness of the decision-making process, collaboration, and the overall quality of the stakeholder experience? RQ3 – Professional Adoption: What factors influence the acceptance, effectiveness, and perception of structured experiential solutions among the various professional categories involved in administrative procedures? In line with these research questions, the study pursues the following objectives: Identify the critical determinants that enable the effective implementation of experiential systems in administrative and collaborative decision-making processes; Develop a replicable and step-by-step framework to guide the definition, management, and validation of information and experiential requirements; Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Evaluate, through comparative case studies, the positive effects and potential limitations associated with the application of the proposed framework; Formulate operational and scientific recommendations aimed at improving methodological harmonization and supporting future developments in information management and experiential representation practices in the AEC sector.
