Within this paradigm, the framework is conceived as a research artifact resulting from an iterative and reflective process of problem identification, analysis, design, implementation, and evaluation. Rather than proposing a predefined technological solution or a prescriptive user experience model, the framework aims to structure how representation can be systematically integrated into administrative and information management processes. The initial phase of the framework's development was guided by a systematic literature review and gap analysis. This phase highlighted several recurring critical issues: the lack of shared methodological approaches for representation technologies, the lack of explicit links between information structuring and experiential outcomes, the limited evaluability of immersive solutions in decision-making contexts, and significant organizational and professional barriers to adoption. It is important to note that the literature has revealed that experiential qualities are often considered intrinsic properties of technologies, rather than the results of structured informational and interactional configurations. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Based on these findings, the framework was not developed through a direct deductive definition of experiential dimensions. Instead, it followed an inverse analytical process. Empirical observations from collaborative simulations and case studies were initially used to identify recurring experiential effects relevant to administrative and decision-making contexts, such as presence, engagement, immersion, etc. These experiential effects were treated as observable outcomes that emerge during interaction with digital representations, rather than as predefined design objectives. These outcomes were subsequently decomposed analytically to identify the underlying sensory, interactional, and informational mechanisms that contributed to their emergence. This reverse analytical process allowed us to trace experiential effects back to controllable and designable input conditions, including participation modes, levels of interactivity, perceptual channels, interaction modes, and strategies for representing information and structuring content. From this perspective, experiential dimensions are interpreted as system outputs, while prerequisites and requirements constitute inputs. Within the framework, experiential dimensions such as presence, immersion, engagement, usability, and cognitive impact do not form a closed or fixed taxonomy. Instead, they operate at different analytical and functional levels. Presence and immersion are interpreted as enabling perceptual conditions that support the user's ability to meaningfully interact with digital representations, but they are not considered objectives in themselves. Usability serves as a transversal operational prerequisite, ensuring accessibility, inclusiveness, and effective interaction among the diverse professional profiles involved in administrative procedures. Engagement plays a mediating role, supporting sustained attention and active participation during collaborative evaluation processes. Cognitive impact represents a higher-level outcome related to shared understanding, interpretation of alternatives, and informed decision-making. During the empirical phases of the research, additional dimensions such as emotions and skills emerged as context- dependent variables. These dimensions reflect individual differences, professional background, learning effects, and familiarity with digital tools. They are therefore considered emergent and situational rather than constitutive elements of the main framework. The selective focus on presence, immersion, and usability in the discussion phase reflects their stability as experiential outputs that consistently influence the effectiveness of structured representation in different scenarios and administrative contexts. To ensure conceptual clarity, the framework explicitly distinguishes three interconnected yet distinct levels. The methodological framework refers to the general research and implementation logic based on DSRM. The configuration of structured information concerns data organization, information requirements, and information flows according to information management principles. Structured representation refers to the representative and interactive level through which structured information is accessed, perceived, and collectively discussed by stakeholders. The framework also adopts an ecosystem-oriented perspective that expands the traditional object-centric view of BIM. Representation is conceptualized as a relational system based on the interaction between three main components: the user, the digital model, and the virtual environment. The user represents the cognitive and decision-making agent, characterized by specific roles, skills, and interpretative needs. The digital model serves as an information vector, structured according to explicit information requirements. The environment serves as a mediating container through which sensory stimulation, interaction, and interpretation occur. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Meaning and decision support do not reside in any single component of this triad, but emerge from their dynamic interaction. Variations in the information structure, representational configuration, or interaction mode directly influence how information is perceived, interpreted, and discussed. This relational logic underpins the ecosystem paradigm adopted in research, shifting attention from isolated representational outputs to integrated systems in which users, information, and contexts co-evolve. The resulting framework integrates technical, geometric, informational, and documentation requirements with explicitly defined experiential prerequisites. Case studies and simulations conducted during the research provided empirical support for this approach, highlighting both its potential advantages and current limitations. These findings guided the iterative refinement of the framework and support its applicability to collaborative and administrative decision-making processes, while also identifying directions for future research and further methodological consolidation. 3.5 From Research Questions to Case Study The complexity of the research questions, which focused on the effects of a structured configuration and the factors influencing its adoption, required an investigation strategy capable of capturing both organizational dynamics and the effects within real-world decision-making contexts. For this reason, the case study approach was adopted as the primary empirical strategy, in line with its suitability for the in-depth investigation of contemporary phenomena in their real-world context and the integration of heterogeneous data sources [121]. Within the Design Science research methodology adopted in this study, case studies play a specific methodological role, supporting the empirical instantiation, verification, and validation of the proposed framework as a research artifact. The case study approach can serve exploratory, descriptive, or explanatory purposes. This research adopted a combined exploratory-descriptive approach: exploratory in identifying the conditions and factors influencing the implementation of structured representation technologies, and descriptive in observing and interpreting existing collaborative and decision-making practices in the AEC sector. In line with the methodological considerations discussed by Yazan [123], the choice of this approach was based on three conditions: the nature of the research questions, which require explanations of the "how" and "why"; the limited degree of control over the variables involved; and the focus on contemporary sociotechnical phenomena rather than historical events. These elements make the case study strategy particularly appropriate for investigating collaborative decision-making processes characterized by complex interactions between information, technology, and organizational actors. From a temporal perspective, data collection followed a cross-sectional design. This choice reflects the constraints of the doctoral research context, while being consistent with the exploratory and evaluative objectives of the study, acknowledging the limitations inherent in the lack of a longitudinal analysis [124]. Regarding the number and configuration of cases, the research adopted a deliberate replication logic. Two complementary case studies were designed to meet different methodological purposes within the framework's validation process. The first case study was conducted in a controlled, simulated environment and was primarily intended to support literal replication. This context allowed the framework to be applied under predefined conditions, facilitating verification of its internal consistency and the reproducibility of its main mechanisms. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management The second case study was conducted in a real-world administrative setting and aimed to achieve theoretical replication. In this case, the framework was applied under different organizational, procedural, and contextual conditions to assess its robustness, adaptability, and validity beyond the simulated environment. The combined use of simulated and real-world contexts therefore reflects a deliberate methodological strategy, aligned with the established principles of case study research. This replication logic strengthens the overall validity of the empirical investigation and supports the assessment of the framework's transferability to different decision- making contexts. 3.5.1 Criteria for selecting case studies The selection of case studies was guided by the primary objective of the research: to develop an approach capable technical materials and the participation of a qualified sample of professionals and institutional representatives. The selection of case studies was guided by the primary objective of the research: to develop and validate a methodological approach capable of improving collaborative and decision-making processes in administrative procedures through the integration of information management and structured experiential representation. Accordingly, case selection was not driven by convenience, but by theoretically and methodologically defined criteria aligned with the research questions and with the validation logic of the Design Science Research Methodology. The Conference of Services was identified as the most representative reference context, as it constitutes a paradigmatic multi-stakeholder public decision-making process characterized by high information complexity, heterogeneous professional actors, and the need for coordination and consensus-building. These features make it particularly suitable for testing the capacity of the proposed framework to support structured information representation and shared understanding within administrative procedures. In line with the DSRM approach and with the replication logic outlined in Section 3.5, the case studies were intentionally selected to represent realistic and progressively complex scenarios, consistent with the transferability and validation objectives of the framework. The selection was therefore based not only on data availability and stakeholder access, but primarily on their representativeness with respect to the collaborative and decision-making dynamics typical of the Italian Public Administration. The first case study (Borgo Pirelli) was adopted as a controlled experimental setting, specifically designed to support the preliminary validation of the framework and to analyze internal collaboration mechanisms under predefined conditions. This context enabled the verification of the internal coherence of the framework and the functionality of its core components. The second case study (Milano MIND) was conducted in a real-world administrative and regulatory environment and aimed at testing the application of the framework under operational conditions. This case allowed the assessment of the framework’s robustness, adaptability, and effectiveness in a complex context involving multiple institutional actors and formal decision-making constraints. Both case studies satisfied a common set of selection criteria, defined ex ante to ensure methodological consistency and validity of the results: Relevance to administrative procedures: the possibility of applying the framework within a real or simulated administrative process involving multiple stakeholders with diverse expertise. The Conference of Services was therefore identified as the ideal testing ground, as it simultaneously encompasses coordination, evaluation, and decision-making activities. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Use of a BIM-based information structure: although BIM is not yet systematically adopted in administrative practice, it was employed in both case studies as an information backbone to test the central research hypothesis, namely the feasibility of connecting structured data management with experiential representation. In this sense, BIM is adopted not as a technology per se, but as a methodological instrument for organizing and structuring information. The implications of this role are further discussed in the analysis of results. Diversity and progressive complexity of scenarios: the two case studies were selected to represent increasing levels of organizational, procedural, and informational complexity, supporting a gradual and systematic validation of the framework. Case 1 – Borgo Pirelli: an urban redevelopment project subject to landscape constraints, simulated in a controlled environment to evaluate collaborative dynamics and the effectiveness of the framework during the testing phase. Case 2 – Milano MIND: a real-world infrastructure project developed in collaboration with RFI, enabling the evaluation of the framework within a concrete operational context characterized by regulatory constraints and multiple institutional actors. Accessibility to data and stakeholders: access to federated digital models, technical documentation, and qualified participants was ensured through collaborations with ALER Milano (Borgo Pirelli) and RFI (Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata), guaranteeing both data reliability and stakeholder involvement. Together, these criteria ensured a balance between scientific rigor and practical relevance, allowing the framework to be validated under controlled conditions and subsequently tested in a realistic administrative environment. 3.5.2 Structured approach for information representation in administrative proceedings The Conferenza dei Servizi, established by Law 241/1990, represents a particularly suitable context for testing a methodological approach based on structured information representation. By its nature, it constitutes a collaborative administrative and decision-making process involving multiple public bodies with heterogeneous expertise, such as Fire Departments, Superintendencies, ARPA (Regional Environmental Protection Agency), an Local Health Authorities (ASL), engaged in the joint evaluation of complex projects. Although the formal structure of the procedure is articulated into four phases (initiative, investigation, decision, and effectiveness integration), in practice the investigation phase, in which the Conference takes place, is often characterized by superficial forms of digitization. The conversion of paper-based documentation into digital formats (e.g., PDFs) and their circulation via email or basic digital platforms has improved procedural speed, but has not significantly enhanced the quality, coherence, or interpretability of the information process. This situation results in fragmented content, communication difficulties, and a partial understanding of spatial, functional, and regulatory relationships within the project. Such limitations negatively affect the efficiency, transparency, and traceability of decision-making processes, often leading to inconsistencies between requirements, extended approval times, and reduced accountability. At the same time, the Italian regulatory framework is undergoing a progressive transition toward structured digital management of public works. Ministerial Decree 312/2021 introduced the mandatory adoption of BIM methodologies in public procurement, subsequently reinforced by Legislative Decree 36/2023 (the new Public Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Contracts Code). In particular, Articles 19 and 43 establish digital information management as a structural component of public works governance, rather than an optional technological enhancement. Despite this regulatory evolution, the Conferenza dei Servizi remains largely peripheral to the digital transformation process, continuing to rely on fragmented and document-centric information practices. This misalignment between regulatory intent and operational practice creates a critical space for methodological intervention. This research positions itself within this gap, proposing an approach that integrates BIM-based information management with structured experiential representation as components of a unified information ecosystem. Within this framework, technology is not conceived as an end in itself, but as a means to overcome cognitive and communicative barriers among stakeholders, improving project readability, shared understanding, and the quality of decisions. From a methodological perspective, the Conferenza dei Servizi functions not merely as an application context, but as a paradigmatic stress test for the proposed framework. Its high information complexity, multi-actor structure, and formal decision-making requirements amplify the cognitive and representational challenges that the framework is designed to address. As such, it provides a particularly suitable environment for observing the causal role of structured information representation in supporting informed, transparent, and participatory decision-making processes. 3.6 Data Collection and Analysis Approach The data collection and analysis process was articulated into four sequential phases, each defined by specific methodological objectives and dedicated analytical tools. This structure was designed to ensure coherence between the conceptual framework, the operationalization of analytical constructs, and the empirical validation process. The first phase focused on instrument design and data collection. Data collection tools were developed following a multidimensional approach, aimed at capturing the complexity of representation and decision-making from different perspectives. To preserve the continuity and immediacy of the experience, direct interviews were deliberately avoided, and structured questionnaires were adopted and differentiated according to the research context. Questionnaire Q1 (GEBA – General Experiential Building Assessment) was conceived as a large-scale quantitative survey and administered to 59 participants. Its purpose was to map the level of technological adoption, awareness, and familiarity with experiential and digital representation tools within the AEC sector. Distribution took place during industry events and professional exhibitions, ensuring the involvement of participants with diverse roles and expertise. Questionnaires Q2 (FE – Framework Evaluation) and ISES (Stakeholder Experience Survey) were designed as mixed-method instruments and administered to 12 and 7 participants, respectively. These questionnaires were specifically tailored to assess user experience quality, interaction effectiveness, and perceived value of the framework within its application contexts. To ensure experiential continuity and perceptual immediacy, all questionnaires were completed immediately after the simulation sessions, either through conventional digital interfaces or via QR codes accessible directly from the virtual environments. This approach facilitated the collection of spontaneous and experience-consistent feedback. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management The second phase involved data coding and structuring. Qualitative data derived from open-ended responses and Plus/Delta analyses were processed using an open coding approach, allowing the identification of recurring themes, strengths, and critical issues. Quantitative data were coded a priori by treating Likert-scale responses as numerical variables and organizing them into structured datasets. The selection of variables was guided by the analytical constructs defined during the framework development phase. Constructs such as presence, immersion, engagement, usability, and cognitive impact, together with complementary experiential factors related to emotional response and perceived skills, were operationalized through specific questionnaire items. These variables formed the basis for the construction of data matrices used in subsequent statistical analyses. The third phase consisted of applying statistical analysis techniques, including t-tests, ANOVA, regression models, and correlation analyses, to identify significant relationships and differences between variables across different conditions, user groups, and technological configurations. Finally, in the fourth phase, quantitative and qualitative findings were integrated through a data triangulation process. This integration enabled the interpretation of observed phenomena from multiple perspectives, reduced the risk of interpretative bias, and strengthened the internal consistency and robustness of the empirical results. 3.7 Approach to the Discussion of Results To systematically address the research questions and ensure coherence between methodological design and result interpretation, a structured discussion model was developed as an integral part of the research framework. Rather than serving solely as an ex post organizational scheme, this model was designed to support the analytical interpretation of empirical findings in direct relation to the research questions, the defined constructs, and the underlying information management principles. Starting from a synthesis of the literature and empirical data, the model identifies the main factors influencing administrative procedures and the effectiveness of collaborative decision-making processes. Inspired by the multilevel analytical approach proposed by Kim et al. [116], the discussion framework is articulated across phases, focus areas, evaluation criteria, and metrics, as illustrated in Figure 11. This structure ensured methodological continuity between the formulation of the research questions, the operationalization of analytical constructs, data collection and analysis, and the subsequent critical interpretation of results. Each empirical finding is therefore discussed within a predefined analytical context, allowing for traceable and verifiable links between observed effects and the framework’s underlying assumptions. The discussion chapter is organized into three main phases, each corresponding to one of the research questions and to a specific stage of the framework: Commissioning (RQ1): This phase addresses the definition of prerequisites and information requirements derived from information management principles and integrated with experiential constructs. The objective is to understand how data and information can be structured to support more effective collaborative and decision-making processes. The analysis focuses on criteria such as compatibility and coherence, assessed through information robustness metrics. Usage (RQ2): This phase examines the effects of the framework during its application. The focus areas include experiential dimensions (presence, immersion, engagement, emotion, usability), collaboration Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management mechanisms (multi-user interaction, real-time communication, shared modifications), and decision- making activities (inspection, interpretation, and information evaluation). Metrics related to capacity, efficiency, and effectiveness are used to quantify the influence of structured information on process quality. Post-Usage (RQ3): The final phase focuses on framework acceptance and professional perception, analyzing differences across user categories and technological configurations. The discussion addresses practical effects, physical perceptions, and cognitive barriers, with the aim of identifying improvement strategies and more informed criteria for future adoption. By adopting this structured discussion model, the research moves beyond a purely descriptive interpretation of results. Instead, empirical evidence is situated within a coherent analytical architecture that supports a causal reading of the relationships between information management, structured experiential representation, and collaborative decision-making processes. Fig. 11 Framework for data discussion. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 3.8 Validity, Reliability, and Generalizability The methodological rigor of this research is based in a systematic and explicit consideration of validity, reliability, limitations, and generalizability. These dimensions are essential to ensure both the scientific soundness of the study and its practical relevance for the evolution of digital administrative processes supported by structured Representation Ecosystems. 3.8.1 Validity The overall validity of the study was addressed from a multidimensional perspective, integrating internal validity, construct validity, and considerations of transferability. Internal validity was ensured through the consistent alignment of research questions, data collection instruments, and analysis methods. This alignment was achieved through an iterative research design, in which methodological choices were continuously verified in relation to one another to maintain coherence across all phases of the study. The adoption of a mixed-methods approach enabled the integration of quantitative and qualitative perspectives, reducing the risk of interpretative bias and supporting data triangulation, which strengthened the robustness of the empirical findings. Construct validity represented a particularly critical aspect, given the exploratory and design-oriented nature of the research. The main analytical constructs—such as presence, immersion, and usability—were selected based on metrics previously validated in the scientific literature and subsequently adapted to the specific context of administrative and collaborative decision-making processes [125]. This adaptation was conducted through a participatory validation process involving industry experts during the framework definition phase. This iterative comparison supported the refinement of measurement instruments and ensured that theoretical concepts were translated into operational variables consistent with the research objectives. As illustrated in Tables 24, 29–35, 37, and 56, the convergence between quantitative indicators and qualitative analyses confirms the internal coherence and construct robustness of the methodological approach. External validity was addressed in terms of transferability rather than universal applicability. The framework’s potential transferability is supported by its alignment with established regulatory and methodological references, such as ISO 19650, UNI 11337, and Legislative Decree 36/2023, which ensure compatibility with organizational contexts characterized by structured information management and collaborative practices based on BIM and CDE environments. The framework demonstrates its greatest effectiveness in complex public administrative procedures, where experiential representation functions as a cognitive enabler for shared understanding and consensus-building. In more production-oriented or private-sector contexts, where operational efficiency prevails over collaborative negotiation, its contribution may be less pronounced. This does not undermine the validity of the framework, but rather delineates its intended scope of application. 3.8.2 Reliability The reliability of the study was ensured through a systematic approach to documenting, structuring, and replicating research procedures. A detailed protocol was developed for the case studies, specifying participant selection criteria, data collection procedures, analytical methods, and interpretation guidelines. This protocol Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management functioned both as an operational reference and as a methodological control mechanism, supporting consistency and transparency throughout the research process. Reliability was further reinforced by the convergence of results obtained across the two case studies. Despite their different levels of complexity and contextual conditions, both the simulated and real-world cases exhibited comparable trends and improvements, indicating the framework’s capacity to produce consistent effects under varying circumstances. 3.8.3 Limitations Notwithstanding the robustness of the methodological design and the integration of quantitative and qualitative evidence, several limitations must be acknowledged. The relatively small sample size (19 participants) and potential deviations from normal data distributions constrained the application of more advanced inferential statistical techniques. The use of parametric tests was therefore oriented toward descriptive and diagnostic purposes, consistent with the exploratory nature of the research. Future studies may address this limitation by extending the empirical base and incorporating non-parametric tests, such as Mann–Whitney or Kruskal–Wallis, to further assess the stability of the observed effects. An additional limitation is related to the cross-sectional design of the research, based on two distinct case studies conducted within contexts characterized by relatively high levels of digital maturity and collaborative culture. Applying the framework in environments with lower degrees of digitalization would require methodological adjustments, particularly in defining experiential requirements and managing information flows [126]. The thematic specificity of the selected cases, focused on urban redevelopment and infrastructure projects, also limits the immediate extension of findings to other administrative domains, such as private construction or heritage management. Nevertheless, the methodological coherence demonstrated through the convergence of quantitative and qualitative results provides a solid foundation for future comparative and longitudinal investigations. 3.8.4 Generalizability The assessment of generalizability was conducted through a comparative analysis of the two case studies, which represented complementary validation environments. The Borgo Pirelli case, developed in a controlled and simulated context, supported the verification of the framework’s internal logic and operational sequencing. The Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata case, by contrast, tested the framework under real-world regulatory and organizational conditions, characterized by formal administrative procedures and multi-institutional decision- making. This comparison highlights two distinct levels of transferability. The first concerns contexts that share structural characteristics with the analyzed cases, including BIM- and CDE-based information management, medium-to- high digital maturity, and collaborative procedural frameworks. In such contexts, the framework can be applied with minimal adaptation, offering tangible benefits in terms of communication clarity, project understanding, and decision-making quality. The second level αφορά contexts that require contextual calibration. In environments governed by different regulatory frameworks, heterogeneous technological infrastructures, or limited familiarity with structured information management, the framework must be adapted to local conditions. This adaptation may involve Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management adjustments in the depth of information structuring, the pace of tool integration, or alignment with existing information governance practices. From this perspective, generalizability should be understood not as direct replication, but as a guided adaptation process in which the core principles of the framework are preserved while its implementation is modulated according to organizational, procedural, and technological constraints. The modular architecture of the framework supports this adaptive application, ensuring methodological continuity across different institutional settings. Importantly, the framework is not tied to specific tools or technologies, but accommodates a range of implementation strategies, from conventional solutions to more advanced and immersive configurations [4]. While empirical validation in this study primarily addresses public-sector administrative procedures, the underlying principles of information management and collaborative decision-making are also applicable to private-sector contexts characterized by comparable organizational dynamics. 3.9 Summary The research process was articulated into a set of closely interconnected phases, designed to systematically address the research questions and to investigate the effects of a structured approach on collaborative and decision-making processes. The initial phase focused on the definition of the conceptual and methodological foundations of the study, informed by a systematic literature review that identified key gaps, recurring challenges, and the theoretical space within which the research was positioned. Building on this conceptual grounding, a structured methodological framework was progressively designed and developed within the Design Science Research paradigm. The framework was subsequently instantiated, applied, and validated through case studies characterized by increasing levels of organizational, procedural, and informational complexity. This approach enabled an integrated assessment of the framework’s effectiveness, experiential impact, robustness, and transferability across different contexts. The final phase involved the systematic analysis and triangulation of quantitative and qualitative data, followed by a structured discussion of the results and their critical interpretation in relation to the initial research objectives and assumptions. This process ensured traceable links between empirical evidence, analytical constructs, and the proposed methodological framework. The adoption of a mixed-methods research strategy, combining interpretative and design-oriented reasoning with quantitative analytical tools, proved essential for achieving a deeper and measurable understanding of the investigated phenomena. Overall, this methodological configuration ensured coherence between theoretical foundations, empirical validation, and critical reflection, providing a robust basis for evaluating the contribution and implications of the proposed approach. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 4 A FRAMEWORK FOR COLLABORATIVE DECISION-MAKING PROCESSES Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 4.1 Overview of the Theoretical Framework Given the conceptual density and multilayered nature of the proposed framework, this introductory section aims to clarify its overall structure, logic, and internal components before addressing each element in detail. The goal is to provide the reader with a navigational reference that supports a progressive and coherent understanding of the framework as an integrated whole, rather than as a collection of isolated concepts. The framework is conceived as an operational and methodological framework that guides the configuration, implementation, and evaluation of representation systems in regulated and collaborative contexts. Rather than proposing a technological solution or promoting specific representational features, it defines a structured process that connects information requirements, representation strategies, and decision-making needs through a coherent and traceable logic. In this perspective, representation is treated to support interpretation and evaluation, rather than as an end in itself. From a methodological standpoint, the framework represents the outcome of the third phase of the Design Science Research Methodology, corresponding to the design and development of the research artifact. Its structure reflects an iterative and adaptive approach, in which theoretical principles derived from the literature are translated into operational components and subsequently validated through empirical application. This process-oriented logic ensures that representational choices are always grounded in contextual objectives, constraints, and evaluative needs. As illustrated in Figure 12, the framework is organized into five interconnected structural components, each addressing a specific aspect of the information and representation process: Context and Case Definition, which establishes the scope of application, decision-making objectives, stakeholders involved, and organizational or procedural constraints; Prerequisites and Requirements, which define the conditions and specifications necessary to enable structured experiential representation, extending the concept of Level of Information Need to experiential and decision-oriented contexts; Information Exchange Processes, which govern how information is requested, structured, transmitted, and managed between systems, including the extension of traditional Information Exchange Requirements (EIR) to experiential parameters; Procedural Integration, which connects the framework to procurement, contractual, and administrative workflows, ensuring consistency between information specifications, representation strategies, and formal decision-making procedures; Iteration and Validation, which introduces cycles of verification and refinement based on empirical feedback, in line with the principles of DSRM. The interaction between these components enables the progressive transformation of raw data into structured information and, subsequently, into experiential representations capable of supporting evaluation, interpretation, and collective decision-making. Importantly, this transformation is not conceived as a linear enhancement of experiential richness, but as a controlled and purpose-driven alignment between informational structure and representational effectiveness. A key feature of the framework lies in its ability to bridge two traditionally separate domains: information management and representation. On the one hand, it draws on established standards and Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management methodologies for structuring and exchanging information, such as BIM-based information management and ISO standards. On the other, it introduces experiential dimensions as explicit and configurable elements, whose activation depends on contextual relevance and decision-making objectives. In this way, representation strategies are aligned with informational and procedural needs, avoiding the assumption that increased sensory complexity or immersion necessarily leads to better outcomes. The following sections examine each component of the framework in detail, explaining its theoretical foundations, methodological implications, and operational relevance. Chapter 4 therefore constitutes the core explanatory chapter of the research, in which the internal logic of the framework and its constituent elements are articulated and operationalized, building upon the methodological foundations introduced in Chapter 3. Fig. 12 Structure of the chapter 4.2, 4.3 and 4.4. 4.2 Structuring Information: From Data to Sensory and Cognitive Engagement While grounded in the theoretical foundations discussed in Chapter 2, this section does not aim to further extend the literature review. Instead, it operationalizes selected theoretical constructs into design principles that directly inform the development of the research artifact, in accordance with the Design Science Research methodology. Following the identification of gaps in the literature and the definition of the research objectives, this section addresses the third activity of the DSRM, namely the design and development of the framework. Here, the framework is conceived as the core research artifact, providing an operational and methodological structure that guides the subsequent phases of the study and supports the implementation and evaluation of the proposed model. The purpose of this section is not to promote specific representational features, but to clarify how information can be progressively structured to support different levels of cognitive and experiential engagement in collaborative and decision-oriented contexts. 4.2.1 Experiential Engagement: From the Information Level to the Immersive Experience Level In the AEC industry, a solid foundation of technical knowledge and relevant information is essential to successfully design, construct, and operate buildings and infrastructure. This includes understanding standards, codes, regulations, safety requirements, design techniques, and construction materials. Information needs therefore represent the set of data and knowledge necessary to make informed decisions within the AEC domain Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management [63]. This process involves researching and analyzing technical information, project documentation, guidelines, and industry standards, as well as critically evaluating existing solutions. Alongside this technical dimension, a sensorial and perceptual component plays a complementary role. This component is based on the selective use of perceptual channels, primarily sight, hearing, and spatial perception, which support the cognitive processing and interpretation of information by different stakeholders. In this research, sensorial engagement is not treated as an intrinsic value or as an objective, but as a functional support to understanding, evaluation, and decision-making outcomes, particularly in collaborative contexts characterized by heterogeneous technical, institutional, and organizational expertise. Although the relationship between sensory stimulation and perception has been extensively investigated in Virtual Reality research [74], its relevance in the AEC domain lies primarily in improving information comprehension and shared interpretation, rather than in maximizing experiential richness. In regulated and administrative contexts, excessive or poorly calibrated sensory stimulation may introduce cognitive overload or distraction, potentially reducing rather than enhancing decision quality. For this reason, the framework adopts a selective and purpose-driven approach to experiential engagement. Once the necessary information and technical knowledge have been acquired, it is possible to move to a higher level of engagement oriented toward evaluation and decision support. This level involves the guided exploration of design or project scenarios for assessment purposes, requiring a degree of cognitive and perceptual engagement sufficient to support understanding, comparison, and discussion, rather than immersive experience per se. In administrative contexts, this experiential dimension is explicitly not intended to support creative design activities, but to facilitate informed evaluation and collective decision-making among heterogeneous stakeholders. However, this form of experiential information is not yet systematically supported within conventional BIM methodologies. Elements such as the static or dynamic behavior of models, the interaction between objects and users, and the representational configuration of sensory cues are rarely defined a priori. This lack of definition makes it difficult to establish coherent information flows, compare alternative representation strategies, and identify appropriate tools and devices for specific decision-making scenarios. Data constitute the essential basis for any knowledge process, as they represent the source from which higher levels of processing derive. In the context of this research, data are not limited to information extracted from BIM models. They include multiple and complementary sources, such as technical and geometric data from digital models, regulatory and procedural data provided by administrative bodies, and contextual information related to authorization, approval, and procurement processes. In administrative contexts, including Conferences of Services and tendering procedures, public authorities supply critical data in the form of planning constraints, regulatory requirements, environmental assessments, formal opinions, and evaluation criteria. These data interact with project-based information produced by designers and contractors, forming a heterogeneous but structured informational substrate. From data, information is derived, and from information, knowledge emerges, representing the highest level achievable by machines (for example, through advanced data processing or neural networks) [127]. At a further level, awareness arises, a characteristic exclusive to human agents [128]. Through this process, humans interpret and contextualize information, determining the degree of experiential engagement required based on perceptual and cognitive mechanisms, as schematically represented in Figure 13. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 13 illustrates the progressive relationship between information need and experiential engagement for both human and machine actors. 4.2.2 Information level and experiential dimensions in information management The information level concerns the quantity, quality, and structure of data needed to understand or address a given situation, with the aim of satisfying the cognitive needs of users or machines [129]. For example, the information need may be low when a general overview is sufficient, or high when detailed and in-depth content is required to support evaluation or verification. Experiential dimensions, by contrast, refer to how information is perceived, processed, and related to the user. They describe the degree of cognitive and perceptual engagement that an information environment is capable of supporting. In this research, experiential engagement is not associated with entertainment-oriented or immersive experiences, but with interactive and scenario-based environments designed to support evaluation, comparison, and informed decision-making. In administrative and collaborative assessment contexts, experiential engagement increases when information is presented through coherent interactions, clear spatial relationships, and interfaces that support active interpretation rather than passive consultation. Importantly, this does not imply that higher engagement is always preferable. Depending on the complexity of the task and the decision-making objectives, reduced or simplified experiential configurations may be more effective than highly immersive or multisensory ones. While information management traditionally focuses on the structuring and organization of data, experiential dimensions concern the relationship between content and users, the representational strategies adopted, and the extent to which these strategies support understanding and informed judgment. This perspective helps address the AEC industry’s resistance to adopting advanced representation systems, not by emphasizing immersion for its own sake, but by improving the accessibility, interpretability, and usability of complex information in collaborative processes. Within an information management perspective, the experiential dimension expresses a model’s ability to provide not only structured data, but also interactive and decision-oriented representations. Such representations typically rely on selected perceptual cues, primarily visual and spatial, that are functionally relevant to the task at hand, rather than on full multisensory stimulation. Representation systems thus enable users to explore and interact with information at different levels of depth, according to their role, expertise, and decision-making responsibilities. Accordingly, information requirements should consider not only data structure, type, and quality, but also the representational strategies and experiential effectiveness necessary to communicate information meaningfully to Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management different categories of users, particularly in regulated administrative and decision-making contexts. In this sense, experiential dimensions are treated as contextual and configurable variables, activated only when they provide demonstrable informational value. 4.3 Framework Structure: Prerequisites, Requirements, and Information Exchange The construction industry increasingly requires practical applications and documented case studies that clearly describe implementations, results, and limitations of advanced digital solutions. While other domains, such as the gaming industry, have already defined standards, requirements, and protocols for developing experiential content and managing different levels of representation [130], the AEC sector still faces a significant methodological gap when it comes to integrating experiential representation within structured information processes. Despite the growing adoption of representation systems in the AEC field, standardized criteria and processes for their systematic integration remain underdeveloped [4]. Keeping pace with technological advances in information modeling and interactive data visualization is essential [129]; however, a substantial gap persists in information management, particularly in defining structured requirements that connect data organization with advanced representation strategies. This gap does not concern technology availability, but rather the lack of methodological guidance on how representation choices should be aligned with information needs and decision-making objectives. This issue is particularly relevant because no consolidated standard currently exists for structuring projects that employ advanced or immersive representations. Existing standards primarily address data organization, object relationships, and exchange formats, but rarely define representational parameters in relation to experiential effectiveness or decision-making relevance. As a result, representation strategies are often selected ad hoc, leading to divergent interpretations across platforms and stakeholders, as well as interoperability and consistency issues [131]. Although some approaches, such as the automatic verification of geometric requirements described by Angelo et al. [1], address specific functional constraints, they do not explicitly consider perceptual, interactional, or experiential dimensions. Open data models such as Industry Foundation Classes (IFC) provide a robust basis for information exchange between BIM platforms, describing three-dimensional objects, properties, and spatial relationships. Recent extensions, including IFC4x3, introduce features for managing dynamic and interactive scenarios. However, these developments only partially address the needs of advanced representation contexts, as they do not define how experiential aspects should be specified, constrained, or evaluated within information exchange processes. Tomczak et al. [132] emphasize the crucial role of information requirements specification in digitalized AEC processes, noting that no single approach comprehensively addresses all dimensions of complex operational contexts. In line with this perspective, the present research does not aim to prescribe a universal representational solution, but to provide a methodological framework that supports context-aware selection and configuration of representation strategies. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Based on these considerations, this research proposes a conceptual framework designed to address the identified gaps by defining structured prerequisites and requirements for the implementation of Representative Ecosystems in the AEC sector. The proposed structure builds upon the ISO 7817-1 standard (Figure 14), extending its application beyond traditional digital contexts to include experiential and decision-oriented considerations. For each information dimension, Why, When, Who, What, and Where, the framework evaluates not only conventional data configurations, but also the implications of different representation strategies in relation to decision-making objectives. Importantly, these additional prerequisites do not imply that richer or multisensory representations are inherently preferable. Instead, they establish a structured basis for deciding when specific representational or sensory configurations are relevant, necessary, or potentially counterproductive. By explicitly linking representation strategies to information purposes and procedural contexts, the framework mitigates the risk of cognitive overload and diminishing returns associated with indiscriminate multisensory stimulation. Fig. 14 illustrates the relationship between information requirement levels according to ISO 7817-1 and their extension to experiential contexts. 4.3.1 Conceptual Architecture of the Framework for Extended Representation applications Taking the prerequisites and requirements defined by ISO 7817-1 as a reference, the proposed framework introduces an extended information exchange flow that adapts traditional digital paradigms to experiential and decision-oriented representation contexts. While conventional information management approaches are effective for structuring and exchanging data, they prove insufficient when applied to Representative Ecosystems, where interaction dynamics, perceptual mediation, and contextual interpretation play a critical role  [7,132]. Rather than replacing existing standards, the framework extends their application by introducing additional layers of specification related to representation and interaction. This extension  (see Figure 15)  enables a more precise definition of objectives, information content, and representational constraints in advanced contexts, ensuring that Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management experiential configurations remain aligned with information management principles and decision-making needs [4]. The conceptual framework is organized into five structural components, each supporting the progressive transformation of raw data into structured and context-aware information outputs. These components are intentionally designed to support evaluability and traceability, rather than generic notions of experiential “quality.” Iterative verification and refinement cycles are incorporated to ensure internal coherence and alignment with predefined objectives. The framework phases include: Case Study or Intervention Area, defining the application context, decision-making scope, and operational constraints; Definition of Prerequisites and Requirements, identifying key information parameters, roles, and intended uses; Processes for the Exchange of Information, governing information consistency and interoperability; Standardized Procedural Steps, supporting validation, control, and traceability in regulated contexts; Iteration, enabling refinement based on empirical feedback. The outcome of this process is a structured information representation plan that can adapt to different levels of experiential engagement, calibrated according to the objectives and constraints defined during the prerequisite and requirement phases. The framework does not aim to optimize experiential richness, but to support clearer information exchange and alignment between stakeholders, particularly in administrative and procurement contexts. Fig. 15 Framework for Effective Integration of Experiential Requirement in the BIM and AEC Sector (Attachment xx). 4.3.2 Scope of Application and Definition of the Intervention The framework is applicable across all phases of the project life cycle, from feasibility studies to operation and maintenance. Defining the nature of the intervention is a crucial step, as it directly influences the selection of Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management tools, stakeholders, and representational strategies. This contextual definition ensures that experiential configurations are proportionate to the decision-making task, avoiding unnecessary complexity. 4.3.3 Definition of Key Prerequisites and Requirements Referring to the ISO 7817-1:2024 standard, which introduces and regulates the concept of Level of Information Need, this research defines a structured system of prerequisites and requirements aimed at integrating BIM-based information management with advanced representation systems. While ISO 7817-1 provides a robust foundation for specifying information content, granularity, and exchange, its application to experiential and interactive contexts requires further methodological extension. Within this framework, a clear distinction is established between prerequisites and requirements. Prerequisites are understood as contextual, organizational, and procedural conditions that must be defined prior to implementation, whereas requirements specify the informational, representational, and interactional configurations that the system must satisfy in order to support structured experiential representation. This distinction is essential to ensure interoperability, traceability, and proportionality, particularly in regulated and administrative decision-making contexts. 4.3.3.1 Prerequisites The prerequisites were developed according to a structured logic based on the five fundamental dimensions of the Level of Information Need: Why, When, Who, What, and Where. In the context of advanced representation systems, these dimensions are expanded to address information gaps related to interaction, collaboration, and experiential mediation (Table 11). The first prerequisite concerns the information purpose (Why?), namely the reason for which information is requested and used. This dimension is directly linked to the intended use cases, such as visualization, coordination, evaluation, or compliance verification. In different project phases and administrative procedures, the information purpose varies significantly, influencing both the type and the level of detail of the required data. The second prerequisite relates to information timing (When?), identifying when information must be provided, updated, or validated. In BIM-based contexts, this includes both the temporal definition of activities and milestones, as well as the sequencing of operations. In experiential contexts, timing also affects how representations are accessed and interpreted during collaborative sessions or formal evaluations. The definition of the actors involved (Who?) is equally critical. In collaborative and administrative environments, multiple stakeholders with different roles, responsibilities, and skill levels interact with the same information. Explicitly defining actors and responsibilities supports transparency, reduces ambiguity, and enables the configuration of representation strategies appropriate to heterogeneous user profiles. The information object (What?) assumes a broader meaning in experiential contexts than in traditional object- centric BIM approaches. It does not refer solely to building or infrastructure components, but to the entire digital experience, encompassing digital models, users, and interaction dynamics. In this sense, information objects include sensorial, perceptual, and behavioral aspects insofar as they are relevant to interpretation and decision- making. Finally, the spatial and access dimension (Where?) defines how and where information activities take place. Information may be accessed in person or remotely, through shared digital platforms or hybrid environments. This Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management dimension influences tool selection, system configuration, and the degree of integration between physical, digital, and representative spaces. Importantly, the definition of prerequisites does not imply the automatic activation of all available representational or sensory options. Rather, prerequisites establish the boundary conditions within which representation strategies may be selectively configured. This principle is particularly relevant in administrative and regulated contexts, where clarity, interpretability, and proportionality are prioritized over experiential richness. In person Remotely 4.3.3.2 Beyond the Object-Centric View: The Immersive Ecosystem as an "Extended BIM Environment" The integration of structured Representation Ecosystems into information management processes requires a shift beyond traditional object-centric BIM paradigms. The integration of structured Representation Ecosystems into information management processes requires a shift beyond traditional object-centric BIM paradigms. In this study, the term “object-centric” does not refer to the viewpoint or navigation paradigm commonly used in immersive technologies (e.g., third-person or allocentric views). Instead, it denotes an information-centered representation approach in which the digital object, typically the BIM model and its associated data structures, constitutes the primary unit of organization, interpretation, and interaction. While object-based approaches are effective for describing discrete elements and their attributes, they become limiting in experiential contexts, where perception, interaction, and contextual interpretation play a significant role. In Representative Ecosystems, information management evolves from a static repository of geometric, alphanumeric, and documentary data into a dynamic network of entities, relationships, and behaviors. These ecosystems can therefore be understood as extended BIM environments, in which model information is translated into interactive and context-aware representations. Within such environments, information flows generate experiential outputs, such as presence, engagement, or immersion, through interaction between three fundamental entities: the user, the object, and the environment. These entities are considered ontologically equivalent components of the system, each characterized by specific attributes and behaviors. The user acts as the cognitive and interpretative agent; the object represents the digital element carrying structured information; and the environment constitutes the contextual space that mediates perception and interaction. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management The multisensory potential of these environments should not be interpreted as a requirement for comprehensive sensory stimulation. Instead, it defines a range of representational possibilities that may be activated selectively. The framework explicitly avoids assuming that increased sensory richness leads to better outcomes. Rather, it emphasizes controlled and task-oriented configurations that support interpretation, evaluation, and collective understanding. The concept of the Functional Informed Entity (FIE) bridges the ecosystem  (see Figure 16) dichotomy by identifying informational units that may represent single components or interconnected sets of elements. The functional dimension describes the operational or cognitive purpose of the entity, while the informed dimension specifies its data content and relationships. This abstraction supports both traditional BIM applications and experiential simulations within a unified information management logic. Fig. 16 Compositional scheme of the Experience Ecosystem. 4.3.3.3 Requirements The ISO 7817-1 classification of information into geometric, alphanumeric, and documentary categories provides a solid basis for information exchange. However, this tripartite division proves insufficient when applied to advanced representation contexts, where experiential mediation and interpretation play a central role. To address this limitation, the framework introduces experiential requirements  (see Table  12) as configurable input conditions, rather than as prescriptive experiential goals. These requirements define how information may be accessed, interacted with, and perceived, while the resulting experiential dimensions, such as Presence, Immersion, Engagement, or Usability, are treated as emergent and evaluative outputs of the system. The definition of experiential requirements follows an input–output logic. Desired experiential outcomes are first identified at a conceptual level, and then traced back to controllable informational, representational, and interactional configurations. In this way, experience is not treated as an intrinsic property of the technology, but because of structured design decisions. As illustrated in Figure 17, the process begins with the identification of physical and perceptual stimuli that activate human perception, such as visual, auditory, or haptic cues. These stimuli are then translated into technical configurations through the framework’s prerequisites, including participation mode, level of interactivity, perceptual channels, interaction mode, representation strategies, and information content. Each perceptual configuration is associated with specific mediation tools or technologies, such as visual rendering, sound generation, or tactile interfaces, that transform digital information into experiential perception. Once Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management processed by human cognition, these perceptions give rise to experiential dimensions, including presence, engagement, immersion, usability, and comprehension. Crucially, the framework does not assume that activating multiple sensory channels inherently improves experiential effectiveness. Research on multisensory simulations indicates that beyond certain thresholds, additional sensory inputs may lead to cognitive overload or diminishing returns. This risk is particularly relevant in administrative and collaborative decision-making contexts, where excessive stimulation may distract users from evaluative tasks. Accordingly, sensory channels are treated as optional and task-dependent variables. Visual and spatial cues typically constitute the primary channels for information interpretation, while auditory, haptic, or olfactory stimuli are considered only when they provide clear and demonstrable informational value related to the specific decision- making objective. The framework therefore offers methodological guidance on when and why certain channels may be included, rather than promoting their indiscriminate use. Experiential requirements are not intended as objective or legally binding measures, but as evaluative indicators used to assess the coherence and effectiveness of representation strategies within a controlled and predefined context. In administrative settings, experiential data are limited to non-sensitive, role-based indicators related to interaction modes, representation effectiveness, and information comprehension. This ensures transparency, proportionality, and methodological consistency, while avoiding legal, ethical, or procedural ambiguities. Fig. 17 Representational Systems: Visual, Auditory, Kinesthetic. Sensory preferences for processing information: Sight, Hearing, Smell/Taste/Touch/Emotions (Kinesthetic). The requirements are structured as follows: Participation mode: defines how users are represented and participate in the experience (e.g., static presence, dynamic avatar); Level of interactivity: specifies the degree of action allowed (passive observation, interactive, collaborative) Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Perceptual channels (selective activation): defines whether mono- or multisensory configurations are adopted, based on task relevance; Interaction mode: specifies communication and control methods (manual, gestural, vocal, AI-assisted); Information representation: identifies the sensory channels used to convey information (visual, auditory, tactile); Information content: defines the information conveyed (geometric, alphanumeric, documentary), extending ISO classifications to include visual and perceptual attributes when relevant. The activation of these requirements produces experiential dimensions as outputs, including presence, engagement, immersion, usability, and comprehension. Through this structured approach, experiential representation becomes an integrated component of the information management process, aligned with decision- making objectives and contextual constraints, rather than an implicit or uncontrolled by-product of technology adoption.
