Within BIM-based processes, the Exchange Information Requirements (EIR) represent a Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management fundamental instrument for formalizing how information is specified, exchanged, and managed across organizational, asset-related, and project-specific contexts. In recent years, the role of the EIR has evolved from a mechanism for document collection into a structured information ecosystem supporting coordination, traceability, and decision-making (Figure 18). Traditionally, the EIR architecture is articulated across three complementary dimensions: Organizational Information Requirements (OIR), which define information needs at a strategic and organizational level, translating business objectives and governance principles into structured information requirements; Asset Information Requirements (AIR), which introduce an operational dimension focused on the management, maintenance, and lifecycle monitoring of physical and digital assets; Project Information Requirements (PIR), which specify information needs within the concrete constraints of a given project, operationalizing OIR and AIR according to scope, phase, and contractual conditions. While this structure has proven effective for managing geometric, alphanumeric, and documentary information, it reveals intrinsic limitations when confronted with advanced representation and experiential contexts. Existing EIR models do not provide a standardized way to specify how information should be accessed, interacted with, or interpreted within interactive or immersive environments. As a result, experiential aspects, such as interaction modes, levels of engagement, or representational configurations, are often handled implicitly or informally, leading to inconsistencies across platforms and stakeholders. Rather than proposing a radical replacement of the EIR, this research reinterprets its role by extending it into a methodological framework capable of accommodating experiential parameters in a controlled and selective manner. This evolution does not aim to formalize subjective experience itself, but to specify the conditions under which experiential representation is configured and evaluated within information exchange processes. In this perspective, the evolution of the EIR involves three main directions: Extension of classification schemes, to include experiential parameters defined at the level of prerequisites and requirements, such as interaction modalities, representational strategies, and selectively activated perceptual channels; Definition of interoperability principles, ensuring that experiential configurations and representational constraints remain interpretable and consistent when information flows between traditional digital environments and advanced representation systems; Introduction of structured evaluative indicators, not intended to measure individual perception or emotional response, but to assess the coherence, adequacy, and effectiveness of representation strategies with respect to predefined information and decision-making objectives. Crucially, the framework does not assume that richer or more immersive representations necessarily improve information exchange. On the contrary, the extension of the EIR explicitly supports proportionality and task- dependence, allowing experiential parameters to be specified only when they provide demonstrable informational value. This approach mitigates the risk of cognitive overload and diminishing returns associated with indiscriminate multisensory stimulation, particularly in administrative and collaborative decision-making contexts. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Operational continuity between conventional digital environments and advanced representation spaces is therefore achieved not by introducing parallel information flows, but by extending existing protocols with clearly defined experiential prerequisites and requirements. In this way, representation systems remain embedded within established information management practices, while gaining the flexibility needed to support interaction, interpretation, and evaluation in complex scenarios. The evolution of the EIR proposed in this research represents a strategic opportunity to rethink representation, exchange, collaboration, and coordination processes in increasingly multidimensional project environments. By framing experiential representation as a configurable and evaluable component of information exchange—rather than as an implicit or purely technological feature—the framework strengthens methodological coherence and supports informed decision-making across heterogeneous stakeholders. Fig. 18  Integration of Information Requirements (EIR) and Level of Need in BIM Environments. 4.3.5 Connecting information representation paradigms in procurement processes In public procurement procedures, the proposed framework requires the development of a technical information offering that is consistent with the Information Specifications prepared by the contracting authority. In line with established procurement practices, this process is articulated across two main phases: the pre-contractual phase, in which guidelines, objectives, and information requirements are defined by the client; the post-award phase, in which a detailed information plan is developed by the contractor, integrating structured BIM data with any experiential components explicitly required or justified by the scope of the intervention (Figures 19-20). Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management The pre-contractual phase establishes the foundational information architecture necessary for project execution. Traditional BIM-oriented procurement methodologies have developed consolidated standards for this phase, including clear parameters for geometric accuracy, information density, document hierarchy, and information exchange protocols. These parameters provide a robust basis for data consistency, traceability, and contractual clarity. However, when representation technologies are introduced to support collaborative evaluation, coordination, or decision-making processes, these established protocols reveal significant limitations. The simple conversion of geometric models into immersive or interactive environments may be sufficient for preliminary visualization or individual spatial understanding, but it does not address core procurement-related issues such as structured information exchange, role-based interaction, evaluation criteria, or the traceability of decisions. These aspects are particularly critical in regulated administrative and procurement contexts, where formal accountability and procedural transparency are required. Following contract award, it therefore becomes necessary to define an integrated information plan capable of combining structured BIM data with selectively activated experiential components, resulting in a hybrid information ecosystem. At present, such configurations are rarely formalized within tender documents and are often treated as ancillary or demonstrative outputs. This limits the systematic adoption of advanced representation tools, such as AR and VR, beyond illustrative purposes, and hinders their effective integration into decision- making and validation workflows.The absence of standardized parameters for advanced representation does not constitute merely a technical limitation but introduces concrete contractual and procedural ambiguities. When experiential deliverables are not explicitly specified, evaluated, and bounded, expectations regarding scope, responsibilities, and performance criteria remain unclear. For example, development teams may prioritize visual fidelity over informational coherence or interaction logic, not as a design choice but because of insufficient guidance within information specifications. Current regulatory frameworks, while increasingly acknowledging the relevance of digital representations, do not yet provide explicit mechanisms for framing experiential configurations within formal information exchange and validation processes. The proposed framework addresses these challenges by introducing dedicated technical specifications that explicitly define when and how experiential dimensions may be activated within procurement processes. These specifications do not impose experiential requirements by default; rather, they enable contracting authorities to request or justify experiential components when they are functionally relevant to collaborative evaluation, verification, or decision-making objectives. In this way, experiential representation becomes an optional but governable extension of traditional information specifications, rather than an informal or ambiguous add-on. To ensure transparency, comparability, and procedural fairness, such technical parameters should be explicitly included in tender documentation and post-award information plans whenever experiential deliverables are envisaged. This allows proposals to be evaluated against a clearly defined level of need that encompasses both informational and experiential requirements, without conflating technological sophistication with value or performance. As representation technologies continue to evolve, procurement frameworks will need to adapt accordingly. By aligning information management principles with controlled and context-aware representation strategies, the proposed framework supports a more consistent convergence between procurement processes, information exchange, and collaborative decision-making, particularly within public-sector and regulated environments. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 19 Standard procurement procedure. 4.3.6 Iteration and Framework Optimization The implementation of an iterative verification process represents one of the most relevant and distinctive aspects The implementation of an iterative verification process represents one of the most relevant and distinctive features of the proposed framework (Figure 20). Consistent with the principles of Design Science Research Methodology, iteration is conceived not as a post hoc adjustment mechanism, but as an integral component of the framework’s development logic. Through iterative cycles of application, feedback, and refinement, the framework is progressively aligned with real-world operational and administrative contexts. When addressing the integration of traditional digital paradigms with structured experiential representation, iteration becomes indispensable. Representation technologies, exchange protocols, and interaction paradigms evolve rapidly, creating conditions in which static or prescriptive models are unable to remain effective over time. The approach adopted in this research explicitly acknowledges this dynamic landscape, embedding a structured feedback loop that allows operational evidence to inform methodological refinement. The iterative process is grounded in empirical validation across multiple application contexts. Rather than relying on controlled laboratory conditions, the framework was tested through case studies and experimental applications situated in realistic collaborative and administrative scenarios. This multi-context validation strategy made it possible to identify limitations and opportunities that would not emerge through purely theoretical analysis, including: context-dependent requirements that become evident only during practical implementation; compatibility and interoperability issues between existing information systems and advanced representation environments; accessibility and interpretability challenges related to representation strategies, interfaces, and interaction configurations. Importantly, feedback collected during these iterations does not concern subjective experiential satisfaction or individual perception in a psychological sense. Instead, it focuses on the coherence between predefined prerequisites and requirements, the effectiveness of information exchange, and the suitability of representation strategies in supporting interpretation, evaluation, and decision-making tasks. Each implementation context therefore functions both as a validation environment and as a source of evolutionary input, contributing to the progressive optimization of the framework. In line with the research objectives, this study strategically focused on the early stages of the framework’s lifecycle, including the definition of prerequisites, the structuring of requirements, and initial empirical testing. This choice reflects the intention to Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management establish a robust conceptual and methodological foundation before addressing more advanced or large-scale implementation phases. Iterations play a critical role in verifying that prerequisites and requirements remain proportionate, context-aware, and aligned with operational needs. Through successive refinement cycles, the framework increases its internal consistency, applicability, and methodological robustness, without converging toward a fixed or closed solution. From this perspective, the framework is not conceived as a static or definitive artifact, but as a dynamic methodological tool capable of evolving alongside changes in technology, organizational practices, and regulatory environments. This adaptive orientation is particularly relevant in the domain of representation technologies, where methodological flexibility and controlled evolution are essential to ensure long-term relevance and applicability. The research project implemented the initial phases of this iterative cycle, establishing the conditions for future extensions and refinements. Subsequent iterations may further expand the scope of empirical validation and address additional layers of implementation complexity, while remaining anchored to the same information management and decision-oriented principles defined in this study. Fig. 20 Improved Level of Need Iteration in the BIM Information Management Process. 4.4 Representative Ecosystem for Administrative Procedures The introduction of advanced tools grounded in project management principles and structured representation systems represents not merely a form of process optimization, but an opportunity to rethink and evolve administrative procedural flows. In this perspective, the objective is not to replace existing regulatory structures, but to enhance their effectiveness by transitioning from document-centric exchanges toward a unified and traceable information ecosystem. Within the proposed model, BIM constitutes the structured, queryable, and verifiable data backbone of the process, while representation technologies operate as advanced interfaces that support the interpretation, evaluation, and discussion of complex information (Figure 21). Representation is therefore not conceived as an autonomous experiential layer, but as a controlled mechanism that facilitates access to and understanding of structured information in collaborative and administrative contexts. The Representative Ecosystem described in this research is proposed as a reference and methodological model, rather than as a fully consolidated or standardized procedural practice. Its purpose is to demonstrate how Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management information management and experiential representation can be integrated within administrative workflows, without assuming universal applicability or immediate regulatory adoption. The model reorganizes the procedural experience into three interconnected macro-phases that synergistically combine information structuring, representation, and decision-making. The process begins with the submission of natively digital documentation. In this configuration, project proponents no longer deliver static document sets, but provide a structured information model through a Common Data Environment. The CDE is interpreted as an evolvable digital infrastructure, configured according to explicitly defined prerequisites and requirements. While current CDE platforms already support document management and data exchange, the proposed framework extends their role conceptually, enabling rule-based data classification, procedural routing, and traceability aligned with administrative workflows. This configuration represents a methodological objective of the framework, rather than a description of an already standardized solution. The administrative evaluation phase then evolves through one or more collaborative sessions conducted within structured representation environments. Instead of relying exclusively on two-dimensional documents, stakeholders interact with the three-dimensional information model in a shared space. This interaction supports spatial understanding and interpretative clarity, allowing participants to explore the project at different representation scales. Importantly, this interaction is designed to support cognitive and informational comprehension, not immersive experience for its own sake. The experiential dimension serves as a catalyst for shared understanding and negotiation, rather than as a source of subjective or unstructured data. During these collaborative sessions, observations, requirements, and change requests are formulated and digitally recorded. These inputs are explicitly linked to information entities or model elements, ensuring full traceability of discussions and decisions. This approach transforms the evaluation process into a structured information exchange, in which each contribution can be contextualized, verified, and revisited over time. The final phase involves the consolidation of decisions. Validated requirements and agreed modifications are integrated directly into the information model or associated shared environments, resulting in an enriched information ecosystem that becomes part of the formal authorization process. Conference outcomes are documented through semi-automated procedures that generate structured reports linking decisions to specific information entities, model elements, and recorded collaborative sessions. In this way, BIM functions as the authoritative container of decisions, while experiential representation supports their emergence and negotiation. Fig. 21 Illustrates the anatomy of the proposed administrative procedure. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management This approach aims to accelerate decision-making processes while improving transparency and reducing interpretative ambiguity. Crucially, this is achieved not by capturing or quantifying subjective experiences, but by structuring and documenting how information is accessed, explored, and discussed within shared representational environments. Experiential aspects are therefore recorded indirectly, through predefined indicators related to interaction modes, representation effectiveness, and decision-making outcomes, rather than through personal, biometric, or psychological data. To translate this vision into operational practice, a shared methodological tool is required that enables stakeholders, including project proponents, contracting authorities, and authorizing bodies, to configure representative ecosystems based on explicit prerequisites and requirements. This research addresses this need by proposing a framework that guides the initial configuration, use, and validation of such ecosystems. Within this framework, experiential aspects are documented and evaluated through structured information artifacts, predefined evaluation criteria, and iterative verification cycles, ensuring consistency and reusability across different stages of the administrative procedure. Table 13 and Figure 22 summarize the qualitative gap between traditional administrative procedures and the proposed model, highlighting improvements in terms of information sharing, interpretability, interaction, problem identification, and decision-making clarity, while maintaining alignment with regulatory principles and accountability requirements. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 5 FIRST CASE STUDY: BORGO PIRELLI – MILAN Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 5.1 Introduction This first case study analyzes a simulated service conference involving industry professionals engaged in administrative procedures. The objective is to evaluate the effects of applying a structured framework of prerequisites and information requirements, compared to an unstructured configuration, within collaborative and decision-making processes. The proposed framework extends existing information management standards (ISO 7817-1) by introducing explicit experiential dimensions aimed at supporting interpretation, interaction, and collective evaluation during administrative procedures. To this end, an urban redevelopment scenario was developed for the Borgo Pirelli neighborhood in Milan. This context was selected as a representative case characterized by the involvement of multiple stakeholders, regulatory constraints, and complex information and spatial requirements typical of public administration decision-making processes. The scenario was intentionally designed to reflect the conditions commonly encountered during a service conference, including the need to evaluate spatial configurations, technical implications, and procedural constraints through shared representations. The experimental design followed a within-subject comparison approach, in which the same group of participants (n=12) was involved in two sequential collaborative sessions simulating a service conference. The two sessions were based on the same project scenario, digital models, and basic information content, differing only in how the informational and experiential aspects were structured and configured. The first session adopted an unstructured configuration, reflecting common practice. In this setting, the simulations were developed and executed without prior stakeholder involvement in defining prerequisites, information requirements, or experiential configurations. Information and representations were therefore organized according to generic or predefined hypotheses, without a systematic process for capturing and formalizing the specific needs, roles, and decision-making tasks of the participants involved in the administrative procedure. In contrast, the second session applied the proposed structured framework, which was used as a design and configuration tool to explicitly capture stakeholder needs prior to the simulation. In this condition, the simulations were structured by systematically eliciting and formalizing user and stakeholder requirements through the framework. These inputs were translated into prerequisites and requirements, with the aim of aligning the representative ecosystem with the roles, objectives, and decision-making responsibilities of the participants within the simulated administrative process.The experimental process was organized into five sequential phases: Design and Modeling: development of the architectural proposal and 3D models of the buildings and the surrounding urban context; Information Structuring: definition of prerequisites and information requirements, implicitly (unstructured condition) or explicitly through the framework (structured condition), in relation to stakeholder roles and procedural needs; Ecosystem Construction: implementation of a shared digital environment that enables collaborative access to project information through advanced representation systems; Procedural Simulation: Execution of two consecutive collaborative sessions simulating a service conference, conducted under unstructured and structured conditions, respectively; Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Data Collection and Analysis: Systematic collection of qualitative and quantitative data regarding user experience, perceived communication effectiveness, collaboration quality, and perceived decision- making support capacity at the end of each session. This experimental design allows for a controlled comparison between unstructured and structured configurations, enabling us to evaluate how explicitly defining requirements influences collaborative dynamics, stakeholder perceptions, and the perceived effectiveness of decision-making processes within administrative procedures. 5.2 Identification of the Historical and Territorial Context Borgo Pirelli è un significativo esempio di edilizia popolare di inizio Novecento, situato nella zona nord di Milano, in prossimità del quartiere Bicocca. Costruito tra il 1920 e il 1923 per ospitare i dipendenti dello stabilimento Pirelli, il complesso residenziale fu concepito secondo i principi urbanistici del modello inglese della città giardino, che combinava unità residenziali con spazi verdi condivisi e uno sviluppo a bassa densità. Questo approccio rappresentò un esperimento urbano e sociale innovativo per l'epoca [133]. Progettato dagli ingegneri Giacomo Loria e Pietro Allodi, il complesso è costituito da unità abitative bifamiliari a due piani organizzate in cinque distinte configurazioni tipologiche. Questa diversificazione rifletteva un'esplicita strategia di stratificazione sociale, in base alla quale l'assegnazione degli alloggi era legata al ruolo professionale e alla posizione gerarchica degli assegnatari all'interno dell'organizzazione Pirelli. Nonostante la sua rilevanza storica e architettonica, Borgo Pirelli ha subito limitate trasformazioni nel corso dei decenni e ha ampiamente conservato la sua configurazione morfologica e spaziale originale, il che lo rende un caso prezioso per lo studio dell'interazione tra vincoli storici, rigenerazione urbana e processi decisionali amministrativi contemporanei. Attualmente, il complesso è affetto da un significativo degrado strutturale, funzionale e conservativo, che ne compromette sia la fruibilità che il valore storico (Fig. 23). La gestione amministrativa del sito è attualmente affidata all'Azienda Lombarda Edilizia Residenziale (ALER), che opera su mandato del Comune di Milano. È stato recentemente formalizzato un partenariato pubblico-privato a supporto della riqualificazione complessiva dell'area, che coinvolge molteplici attori istituzionali e professionali e richiede complesse valutazioni amministrative. Nell'ambito di questa ricerca, Borgo Pirelli è stato selezionato come caso di studio pilota proprio per questa combinazione di valore storico, complessità fisica e rilevanza amministrativa. La riqualificazione del sito richiede un processo decisionale coordinato tra attori eterogenei, la valutazione di molteplici alternative progettuali e l'interpretazione di informazioni spaziali, normative e tecniche all'interno di procedure amministrative formali. Queste caratteristiche rendono Borgo Pirelli particolarmente adatto a testare il framework proposto in un contesto che rispecchia fedelmente le conferenze di servizi del mondo reale. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 23 Photo showing the current state of the housing complexes in the neighborhood. 5.2.1 Survey and Modeling Campaign Prior to the implementation of the experimental simulations, a structured workflow was defined encompassing surveying, modeling, and virtualization activities. This phase aimed to establish a robust and coherent digital information base capable of supporting both unstructured and structured simulation conditions. The workflow was conceptually aligned with the logic of an Exchange Information Requirements (EIR) document, simulating the definition of information needs typically required before the submission of design documentation in administrative procedures. The initial phase involved an advanced survey campaign based on laser scanning technologies, selected to ensure geometric accuracy and spatial consistency across the digital models. Two complementary systems were employed: Leica RTC 360, a high-resolution static laser scanning system, used for the metric acquisition of external spaces and building volumes; Leica BLK2GO, a portable laser scanner based on SLAM (Simultaneous Localization and Mapping) technology, used for the digitization of internal environments and confined spaces. Five buildings were surveyed, selected as representative of the main architectural typologies present within the Borgo Pirelli complex. The acquired point clouds were initially aligned on-site using the Cyclone FIELD application and subsequently processed and registered using Register 360 software. The resulting datasets were Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management exported in RCP (Reality Capture Project) format and imported into Autodesk ReCap to support parametric modeling within the Autodesk Revit environment. To ensure spatial coherence at the urban scale, the digital models were georeferenced through integration with a three-dimensional master plan generated using the Cesium plugin [134]. This step enabled accurate positioning of the buildings within their territorial context and facilitated the development of urban-scale simulations relevant to administrative evaluation processes. The primary objective of this methodological phase was not solely to achieve geometric accuracy, but to produce digital models suitable for use in collaborative and decision-support simulations. The resulting models constituted a structured digital information base, enabling the development of redevelopment scenarios and the subsequent implementation of experiential simulations under both unstructured and framework-based configurations. This ensured that differences observed during the experimental sessions could be attributed to the configuration of information and experiential requirements, rather than to inconsistencies in the underlying digital representations. Fig. 24 3D view of Borgo Pirelli model during a weather simulation. 5.3 Identification of the Application Context The application context of this demonstration case study focuses on a simulated administrative process for drafting an Urban Regeneration Plan (PRU) for the Borgo Pirelli neighborhood, initiated by a private application. The process is coordinated by the Single Procedure Manager (RUP), who is responsible for informing and coordinating all public and private stakeholders involved, in accordance with the applicable regulatory framework. Given the intrinsic complexity of this type of administrative process, the experimental simulation focuses specifically on the preliminary investigation phase, with particular reference to a simultaneous preliminary Services Conference. This phase represents a critical moment in the administrative decision-making process, as it aims to gather technical opinions, multidisciplinary observations, and preliminary indications that will guide the Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management subsequent procedural steps. During this phase, stakeholders are required to interpret spatial, regulatory, and technical information in a shared context, often under conditions of information uncertainty and heterogeneous expertise. The case study involves the drafting of a Urban Redevelopment Plan (PRU) for the Borgo Pirelli neighborhood, aimed at transforming the area into a university campus through a change of use. This planning tool enables the definition of coordinated urban redevelopment and redevelopment projects, operating within significant regulatory constraints. Specifically, the existing residential units are subject to landscape restrictions imposed by the Superintendency, which limits direct interventions on the buildings and shifts the assessment focus toward functional transformation, reuse strategies, and the redevelopment of common and open spaces. The proposed design scenario therefore envisions the preservation of the existing building heritage, a change of use, and the integrated redevelopment of common spaces. These interventions will be evaluated by a diverse group of institutional bodies, including ALER, the Provincial Fire Brigade Command, the Superintendency of Archaeology, Fine Arts, and Landscape, ARPA, the Local Health Authority (ASL), and the Municipal Administration. In practice, this evaluation occurs through the Services Conference, which provides a formalized mechanism for discussion and collective, simultaneous decision-making (Fig. 25). Fig. 25 Italian Administrative procedure chosen for our case study. In this research, the administrative process is not reproduced in its entirety, but rather simulated to focus on the phases and activities most relevant to collaborative evaluation and decision-making. The simulation framework maintains the same procedural structure, the same actors, and the same project content across all experimental conditions, while varying the way information and experiential aspects are configured and managed. This approach allows the study to isolate the effects of structured versus unstructured representation configurations without altering the administrative scenario itself. For simulation purposes, the Services Conference is divided into the following phases: Documentation preparation: development of preliminary documentation, feasibility studies, and supporting analyses required for the administrative evaluation; Conference convening: formal notification and convocation of all competent bodies, including information on methods, timing, and participation; Conference Procedure: Presentation and discussion of the project. In the experimental setting, this phase is supported by representational ecosystems rather than traditional document-based presentations, enabling shared exploration and discussion of the project; Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Gathering Observations and Opinions: Collection of technical feedback, concerns, and recommendations that emerged during the discussion, including potential requests for modifications or additional analysis; Conclusions and Next Steps: Summarizing the findings and identifying subsequent procedural actions, recorded in the conference minutes. The 12 stakeholders involved in the simulated administrative process represent a diverse set of professional and institutional profiles, reflecting the multidisciplinary nature of a real Services Conference (see Table  14). In this research, the administrative process is intentionally structured through the implementation of representational ecosystems defined by explicit prerequisites and requirements. Rather than focusing on technological novelty, the simulation aims to evaluate how different information configurations and experiential structures influence participants' understanding, interaction, and perceived effectiveness of the collaborative decision-making process within a controlled administrative scenario.. The experimental design intentionally incorporated a baseline condition in which advanced representation systems were employed without the application of the proposed framework. In this unstructured configuration, simulations were developed and executed without the prior elicitation of stakeholder needs and without the formal definition of experiential prerequisites, information requirements, or collaborative roles. As a result, access to information, interaction modalities, and navigation strategies were not explicitly structured, reflecting common professional practice in exploratory or technology-driven uses of advanced representation systems. This unstructured condition served as a reference baseline against which the effects of the framework-based structured configuration could be assessed. In the structured condition, the same project scenario, digital models, and technological setup were retained, while the simulations were configured using the proposed framework to explicitly capture and formalize user roles, objectives, and decision-making needs. Within this experimental context, three distinct information requests were formulated, corresponding to three simulation scenarios (A, B, and C). These scenarios represent different types of informational and evaluative needs typically encountered during administrative procedures, and were intentionally designed to reflect increasing levels of complexity and analytical focus. Each scenario was derived from the conceptual framework and configured to reproduce a formalized information exchange between a client (or public authority) and a technology or design service provider. Importantly, the same three scenarios (A, B, and C) were implemented under both unstructured and structured conditions. This approach ensured that observed differences between conditions could be attributed to the presence or absence of the framework, rather than to variations in scenario content, project information, or technological tools. The experimental design followed a within-subject approach, whereby the same group of AEC industry professionals participated in all simulation sessions. Participants were therefore exposed to both unstructured and structured configurations, allowing for direct comparison of perceived differences in information clarity, collaborative interaction, and experiential quality. Data collection was conducted through structured questionnaires administered after each simulation. The instruments were designed to capture both qualitative and quantitative self-reported measures, focusing on perceived aspects rather than objective performance metrics. Specifically, the questionnaires investigated dimensions such as perceived quality of experience, social presence, level of engagement, clarity and coherence of information, and perceived effectiveness of communication and collaboration. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management This methodological choice aligns with the exploratory and evaluative nature of the research, which aims to assess how different configurations of information and experiential structures influence participants’ perceptions and collaborative dynamics during administrative decision-making processes. The collected data provided empirical feedback for the comparative analysis of the simulations and supported the iterative refinement and calibration of the proposed framework. Fig. 26: procedure for situations with frameworks where the structure is defined by very specific input from the stakeholders involved. 5.4.2 Simulation A: Interactive Collaboration via VR Headsets Simulation A represents the baseline (unstructured) condition of the experimental setup. In this configuration, advanced representation technologies were employed without applying the proposed framework as a design and configuration tool. While a virtual environment and interactive visualization were available, no systematic process was used to elicit stakeholder needs or to formally define experiential prerequisites, information requirements, or collaborative roles prior to the simulation. As a result, information access, interaction modalities, and navigation strategies were not explicitly structured, but emerged implicitly during the session based on participants’ individual experience, professional background, and ad-hoc decisions. This condition reflects common professional practice in exploratory or technology-driven uses of virtual reality for design review and technical discussion. The primary objective of Simulation A was to support a detailed visual and spatial analysis of the proposed redevelopment project. The operational context replicated an informal technical working meeting, in which architects, engineers, and project managers collaboratively examined the Borgo Pirelli model in order to formulate technical observations and preliminary assessments, without the support of a structured information and experiential framework. 5.4.2.1 Definition of Prerequisites and Requirements Although Simulation A is defined as unstructured (see Table  15, 16), it is important to clarify that a minimal and implicit configuration was nonetheless required to enable the simulation to take place. In this sense, the terms Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management “prerequisites” and “requirements” are used here in a descriptive and analytical manner, rather than as prescriptive elements derived from the framework. The implicit prerequisites for Simulation A concerned the basic conditions necessary to conduct a collaborative VR session, including the presence of professional participants, a shared physical location, and access to a virtual representation of the project. These elements were not formalized through stakeholder engagement or structured information elicitation, but rather reflected generic assumptions typical of technology-centered demonstrations. From an experiential perspective, the interaction was limited to visual perception and manual navigation, without the integration of additional sensory channels such as audio content embedded in the virtual model, haptic feedback, or environmental effects. Consequently, the experience is defined as monosensory, based exclusively on visual information. Social interaction among participants occurred externally to the virtual model, through face-to-face verbal discussion and observation of gestures, and is therefore interpreted as social presence, rather than as a multisensory feature of the representation itself. The Representative Ecosystem in Simulation A was characterized by a static virtual environment, with no animations applied to either the content or the surrounding space. Interaction with the model was active and collaborative, mediated through handheld controllers, enabling participants to navigate the model, point at elements, and discuss design aspects in real time. The digital content consisted of a three-dimensional model developed at a 1:1 scale, including essential architectural elements such as building volumes, openings, frames, and gutters. The geometry was positioned using absolute coordinates, ensuring spatial coherence within the virtual environment. Visual appearance was intentionally kept simple, with basic textures and static natural lighting, in order to avoid introducing perceptual or aesthetic variables not relevant to the objectives of the baseline simulation. No textual, alphanumeric, or documentary information was embedded in the model, as the focus of this condition was limited to spatial inspection and visual analysis. Tab. 15 Scenario A Prerequisites. A virtual reality-based ecosystem was therefore considered appropriate for enabling immersive spatial inspection and synchronous interaction among participants. The hardware platform selected was the Meta Quest 3 headset, chosen for its standalone operation, integrated controllers, and high-resolution display, which together provide sufficient performance for collaborative design review sessions without requiring external tracking systems. For the software environment, Unreal Engine was adopted using the Collab Viewer Template, due to its capability to support multi-user sessions, real-time rendering, and stable networking performance. The template provided a predefined collaborative structure, allowing participants to share the same virtual space while minimizing custom development effort. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management The data pipeline originated from the BIM model developed in Autodesk Revit, which was imported into Unreal Engine using the Datasmith plugin. This workflow was selected to ensure geometric fidelity and consistency between the BIM authoring environment and the real-time visualization platform, while maintaining a streamlined and reproducible process. 5.4.2.3 Experimental Protocol The experimental protocol followed a predefined sequence of steps. First, the BIM model was exported from Autodesk Revit and imported into Unreal Engine using the Datasmith plugin. The collaborative session was then configured through the Collab Viewer Template and deployed on a local network. The simulation took place in person at the Labora laboratory. Participants wore Meta Quest 3 headsets and accessed the shared virtual environment simultaneously, where they navigated the model, discussed spatial and technical aspects, and formulated preliminary observations. At the conclusion of the session, participants were asked to complete structured questionnaires designed to collect self-reported data related to their experience. These questionnaires focused on perceived clarity of information, quality of interaction, social presence, and overall usefulness of the simulation for collaborative evaluation, providing baseline data for comparison with the structured simulations. Fig. 27 Photos and screenshots of the simulations during the collaboration phase with the viewers. 5.4.3 Simulation B: Spatial Navigation in a Virtual Theater Simulation B shifts the focus from direct interaction with the digital model to collective spatial perception and contextual understanding at the urban scale. While maintaining the same project scenario and informational content adopted in the other simulations, this scenario was designed to support shared observation and discussion of spatial relationships, proportions, and the overall impact of the proposed redevelopment within its surrounding environment. The primary objective of Simulation B was to allow participants to experience the project together, within a shared physical and virtual space, in order to evaluate spatial configurations, relationships between Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management buildings and open areas, and the perceived integration of the intervention within the existing urban fabric. Rather than emphasizing individual interaction with the model, this scenario prioritizes collective exploration, discussion, and interpretation. 5.4.3.1 Definition of Prerequisites and Requirements In Simulation B, the prerequisites were reconfigured to reflect a different experiential focus compared to Simulation A (see Table  17, 18). While the purpose of the activity remained visual and spatial analysis, the object of the experience shifted from interactive model manipulation to collaborative spatial exploration. As a result, the required technical skills were reduced, enabling participation without prior experience with immersive devices. As in the previous simulation, no explicit milestones or activation sequences were defined, as the activity represents a hypothetical and exploratory administrative scenario. The stakeholders involved remained consistent across all simulations and included architects, engineers, the procedure manager, and researchers, ensuring comparability between experimental conditions. From an experiential standpoint, Simulation B was structured as a fully collaborative experience, deliberately eliminating direct interaction with the digital model. Participants did not individually control navigation or manipulate objects; instead, spatial exploration was collectively guided to support shared observation and discussion. The Representative Ecosystem was conceived as a unified environment, in which the project model was embedded within its broader urban context. User movement within the virtual environment did not rely on continuous locomotion. Instead, spatial exploration was organized through controlled transitions between predefined observation points, designed to support collective attention and discussion while maintaining experimental control and usability. This approach was adopted following preliminary tests, which showed that more complex locomotion techniques could introduce cognitive overload and reduce the effectiveness of group discussion. Importantly, although participants were able to physically move within the available space of the virtual theater, the experience remained monosensory from a representational standpoint, relying exclusively on visual information. No audio content, haptic feedback, or additional sensory stimuli were embedded in the virtual representation. Physical movement and face-to-face verbal communication among participants are therefore interpreted as elements of embodied interaction and social presence, rather than as multisensory features of the representation itself. To support contextual understanding, selected critical locations within the project such as intersections, public spaces, and collective facilities were represented through a sequence of 360° immersive visualizations. These views allowed participants to experience specific urban situations in a controlled manner, simulating key moments of an on-site visit while preserving visual consistency across sessions. The information behavior of the model was kept static, without animations or dynamic changes. Visual characteristics, lighting conditions, and representation scale were optimized to ensure clarity, spatial coherence, and intuitive orientation. This configuration ensured that perceptual differences observed during the simulation could be attributed to the experiential setup, rather than to variations in model behavior or visual effects. Therefore, the Labora laboratory's Virtual Theater, an immersive 360° CAVE with a diameter of 7 meters, was used. This solution proved ideal for allowing multiple users to share the same physical and virtual space, while maintaining the ability to move freely and communicate directly. These conditions fostered collective spatial perception and real-time discussion, key aspects of the experimental objective. From a technical standpoint, the system uses a proprietary Virtual Theater software platform, designed for managing panoramic content. The content production pipeline was optimized by switching from Revit to Twinmotion, leveraging the native integration between the two environments to generate high-quality 360° images. This solution proved more efficient than creating a real-time interactive 3D model, as the experiment required a shared, but non-interactive, experience. 5.4.3.3 Experimental Protocol The protocol involved exporting panoramic images and videos from the model developed in Revit using Twinmotion, which served as the rendering engine. The generated content was uploaded to the Virtual Theater management software for exploration sessions. During these sessions, the professionals participated in shared navigation experiences within the CAVE, followed by completing structured questionnaires to gather feedback to answer the research questions. Fig. 28 Photo portraying the user while walking inside the virtual theatre. 5.4.4 Simulation C: Information Analysis on a Holographic Table Simulation C introduces a further level of analytical complexity by shifting the focus from predominantly spatial perception to the informational interrogation of the BIM model. While maintaining the same project scenario adopted in the previous simulations, this condition was designed to support a combined analysis of geometric representation and alphanumeric information, enabling participants to collaboratively explore and discuss model parameters relevant to administrative evaluation. The primary objective of Simulation C was to assess how the structured access to BIM-related information influences participants’ perceived understanding, interpretative Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management clarity, and collaborative discussion during decision-making processes. Rather than emphasizing immersive spatial experience, this scenario prioritizes information transparency, data accessibility, and collective interpretation. 5.4.4.1 Definition of Prerequisites and Requirements In Simulation C, the prerequisites and requirements were refined to explicitly address the informational dimension of the model, in addition to its geometric representation (see Table  19, 20). The purpose of the activity remained visual analysis, complemented by the ability to access and visualize alphanumeric parameters associated with building components. As in the previous simulations, no explicit timeline or activation sequence was defined, as the activity represents a hypothetical and exploratory administrative scenario. The stakeholders involved remained consistent across all experimental conditions and included architects, engineers, the procedure manager, and researchers, ensuring comparability of participants and roles. From an experiential perspective, the simulation was configured as interactive and collaborative, enabling multiple users to jointly explore and interrogate the model. However, from a representational standpoint, the experience remained monosensory, relying exclusively on visual information. No audio content, haptic feedback, or additional sensory stimuli were embedded in the representation. Verbal communication and physical co- presence around the holographic model are therefore interpreted as elements of social presence and collaborative interaction, rather than multisensory features of the system. The Representative Ecosystem was intentionally constrained to a single-building focus, excluding the broader urban environment. This design choice was made to reduce cognitive load and allow participants to concentrate on the interpretation of building-level information, parameters, and attributes relevant to administrative assessment. The digital model was static, without animations or dynamic behavior. Visual characteristics such as textures, lighting, and depth of field were configured to support clarity and legibility of the holographic representation. While geometric information was fully available, parametric behavior was not activated, as the objective was to support interpretation rather than simulation of performance. Alphanumeric information associated with building elements such as identification attributes and parameter values was accessible through direct selection of components. This configuration enabled participants to interrogate the informational content of the BIM model in a shared and intuitive manner, supporting collaborative discussion and sense-making. Dimensionary 3D - Location Absolute position - Appearance Realistic with texture - Parametric behavior Not required - Texture (gradual scale) Realistic with texture - Lighting Manual settings - Depth of field Infinite - Alphanumerical Information Identification Building type - Infromation content Not required - Documentation Set of documents Not required - 5.4.4.2 Selection and Justification of the Technological Solution To support collaborative information interrogation in a shared physical setting, a holographic table was selected as the primary technological solution for Simulation C. The holographic table available at the Labora laboratory enables the projection of a three-dimensional model as a shared visual artifact, around which participants can physically gather and interact. This configuration transforms the digital model into a common reference object, facilitating joint attention, discussion, and interpretation of both geometric and informational content. The hardware platform was paired with the Euclideon Holotable Toolkits, which provide native support for interactive visualization and parameter inspection. The data pipeline adopted for this scenario was intentionally more complex than in the previous simulations, reflecting the need to preserve the informational richness of the BIM model. The workflow followed the sequence: Revit → NVIDIA Omniverse → USD → Euclideon Toolkit. NVIDIA Omniverse was used as an intermediate platform to convert the BIM model into the Universal Scene Description (USD) format. This format was selected because, unlike geometry-oriented exchange standards, USD is designed to preserve not only geometric and material data, but also hierarchical structures and metadata. This capability was essential for maintaining access to the alphanumeric information that constitutes the core informational value of the BIM model and the primary focus of Simulation C. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 5.4.4.3 Experimental Protocol The experimental protocol began with exporting the BIM model from Autodesk Revit to USD format using the NVIDIA Omniverse connector. The resulting USD file, including both geometry and associated parameters, was then imported into the Euclideon Holotable Toolkits to develop the interactive holographic application. Simulation sessions were conducted in person at the Labora laboratory. Participants gathered around the holographic table and collaboratively explored the model. Using a controller, they selected individual building components to visualize associated parameters, which were displayed in real time and served as a basis for discussion and evaluation. At the conclusion of each session, participants completed structured questionnaires designed to collect self- reported data related to perceived clarity of information, ease of interpretation, collaborative effectiveness, and overall usefulness of the experience for administrative decision-making. These data contribute to the comparative analysis of the three simulation scenarios and support the empirical validation of the proposed framework. Fig. 29 Photos and screenshots during the collaborative phase of viewing and interrogating the model with the hologram table. 5.5 Data Collection and Sampling The adoption of structured representation ecosystems in the construction industry represents a significant methodological and organizational evolution. However, the effectiveness of such ecosystems cannot be assessed solely in terms of technical performance or computational efficiency. Their integration into professional practice is inherently socio-technical and depends on factors such as user perception, interpretative clarity, communication dynamics, and perceived usefulness within collaborative decision-making processes. For this reason, the research adopts a hybrid methodological approach based on the use of multidimensional survey tools. This approach is explicitly designed to capture perceived and experiential dimensions, rather than objective performance metrics, in line with the exploratory and evaluative nature of the proposed framework. The objective Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management is not to measure the efficiency or accuracy of activities, but to investigate how different configurations of information and experiential structures are perceived and interpreted by users operating in simulated administrative contexts. Given the structural complexity of the simulations and the need to address both contextual and experiential aspects, a dual-questionnaire strategy was implemented. This strategy allows for data collection at different scales and levels of depth, combining a broader contextual assessment with an in-depth simulation- based assessment of the framework's application. The following survey tools were used: Questionnaire Q1 - General Evaluation of Experiential BIM (GEBA): A quantitative survey aimed at mapping the current state of adoption of structured representation ecosystems and experiential BIM practices in the AEC industry. This questionnaire focuses on professional background, level of experience, familiarity with immersive and experiential tools, and self-reported perceptions regarding their use in professional contexts. Questionnaire Q2 - Structured Framework Evaluation (SFE): A mixed-method survey administered exclusively to participants involved in the simulation sessions. This tool is designed to collect self- reported and subjective assessments related to the application of the proposed framework, including perceived clarity of information, quality of interaction, social media presence, and perceived support for collaborative decision-making. While Q1 allows for the collection of standardized and comparable data from a relatively large and diverse sample of professionals, Q2 provides a more detailed and contextualized understanding of participants' experiences within the simulated scenarios. The combination of the two tools supports the identification of emerging large-scale patterns and their in-depth interpretation within a controlled experimental context. The primary goal of the data collection process is to answer the research questions defined in Chapter 2 by triangulating contextual information with experiential feedback from the simulations. Accordingly, this chapter is structured into two main methodological components: Development of the survey instruments, which describes the design rationale for the questionnaires, with particular attention to variables such as demographic profile, professional role, level of experience with BIM and immersive technologies, and perceived usability and usefulness; Sampling and data collection process, detailing participant selection criteria, recruitment strategies, and administration procedures across diverse professional and academic contexts, as well as the distinction between the larger Q1 sample and the simulation-based Q2 participant group. This approach ensures methodological transparency and supports consistent interpretation of the results, explicitly acknowledging the subjective and perceptual nature of the data collected while maintaining analytical rigor. 5.5.1 Questionnaire Development Questionnaire Q1 (Table 21) was structured into two main sections. The first section collected demographic information and professional background data, including age, professional role, level of experience with immersive technologies (AR, VR, MR), familiarity with structured Representation Ecosystems, and frequency of their use in professional practice. The second section focused on participants’ self-reported perceptions of experiential representation systems and their potential role within the construction industry. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Participants were asked to express their assessments of various aspects, including perceived usefulness of experiential systems in supporting design and decision-making processes, perceived ease of use, and perceived potential to reduce errors and misunderstandings in professional communication. It is important to note that these items explicitly capture subjective perceptions and attitudes, rather than objective performance improvements. The data collected through Q1 provided an overview of current professional perceptions, expectations, and adoption tendencies related to structured Representation Ecosystems. This contextual information supports the interpretation of the results obtained from Questionnaire Q2, which was administered to a smaller and more controlled sample due to the operational complexity of the simulation-based experimental setup. The analysis of Q1 responses was conducted using a quantitative descriptive approach, based on the percentage distribution of Likert-scale responses to identify recurring patterns and emerging trends. Where applicable, qualitative insights derived from open-ended responses were integrated through thematic analysis, with the aim of capturing interpretative nuances and underlying motivations expressed by participants. (1-5) Questionnaire Q2 (Table 23) was specifically designed to evaluate participants’ perceived experience and interpretative responses during the simulation sessions in which the proposed framework was applied. In addition to demographic and background information, Q2 incorporated open-ended questions to explore participants’ experiences within structured Representation Ecosystems in greater depth. The design of Questionnaire Q2 was informed by established evaluation methodologies, including Plus/Delta Analysis and prior studies on immersive and experiential systems (e.g., Tcha-Tokey et al. [125]; Kim et al. [135]). These references provided a structured basis for investigating experiential strengths, limitations, and areas for improvement. The questionnaire structure followed four main phases: Collection of perceived strengths and weaknesses of the experience through open-ended questions; Acquisition of background information regarding participants’ professional profiles and prior experience; Assessment of perceived spatial understanding and representational clarity; Evaluation of perceived interaction quality and collaborative dynamics during the simulation. Participants took part in two simulation sessions: an initial session configured without the application of the framework, serving as a baseline condition, and a subsequent session in which the framework was applied. Questionnaire Q2 was administered after the framework-based session and was explicitly structured to elicit Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management comparative reflections between the unstructured and structured configurations, based on participants’ subjective experience. The structured classification of feedback enabled the systematic extraction of key experiential dimensions and supported a comparative interpretation of perceived differences between the two conditions. For the qualitative components, Plus/Delta Analysis was employed to identify recurring positive and negative aspects. For the quantitative components, comparative statistical analyses (including regression analysis and independent-sample t-tests) were applied to self-reported measures, with the objective of identifying statistically meaningful patterns within the limits of the sample and the exploratory nature of the study. The results derived from Questionnaire Q2 provide indicative and exploratory evidence of how the proposed framework influences perceived clarity, interpretative support, and collaborative interaction within experiential simulations. Rather than claiming objective performance improvements, these findings contribute to understanding the perceptual and cognitive dynamics associated with the structured integration of experiential systems in design and administrative decision-making contexts. Participation was voluntary and respondents were not preselected, resulting in a naturally heterogeneous sample. This approach supports an exploratory assessment of current perceptions, familiarity, and adoption trends related to experiential and representation ecosystems within the AEC sector (Fig. 30). Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 30 Questionnaires Workflow for quantitative data collection. Questionnaire Q2  (see Figure 31) was administered to a purposively selected sample, defined to replicate the multi-stakeholder structure of a Conferenza dei Servizi. Participants were selected based on their professional role and relevance to the administrative evaluation process and included architects, engineers, BIM managers, representatives of public administrations, environmental agencies, student representatives, and municipal technicians involved in the Borgo Pirelli redevelopment scenario. The experimental activity followed a within-subject design. The same group of participants took part in two consecutive simulation sessions based on the same project scenario and information content. In the first session, the simulation was conducted without the application of the proposed framework, representing an unstructured reference condition. Prior to the second session, participants were introduced to the framework’s prerequisites and requirements, which were designed to structure information access and experiential configuration in line with the dynamics of a Conferenza dei Servizi. The simulation was then repeated under the structured condition. Following the simulations, Questionnaire Q2 was administered. The instrument combined qualitative and quantitative components. Qualitative data were collected through Plus/Delta analysis to capture perceived strengths and weaknesses of the experience. Quantitative data were collected using Likert-scale items addressing perceived spatial understanding, clarity of information, and overall experience during the simulation. The quantitative section of the questionnaire was administered twice, enabling a direct comparison of participants’ perceptions between the unstructured and structured conditions. Data analysis followed a mixed-method approach. Qualitative responses were processed through thematic analysis to identify recurring perceptions and interpretative patterns. Quantitative data were analyzed using statistical tests applied to self-reported measures, with the aim of identifying comparative differences between the two conditions. The analysis does not assess objective task performance, but rather examines perceived effects of the framework on understanding, interaction, and collaborative evaluation within the simulated administrative context. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 31 Questionnaires Q2 - Workflow for qualitative and quantitative data collection. 5.6 Data Analysis and Interpretation of Results 5.6.1 Introduction The data analysis adopts a multi-method approach (Fig. 32 and Table 24), combining a quantitative survey (Q1, n = 59) with a mixed qualitative–quantitative analysis conducted on a smaller, purposively selected sample (Q2, n = 12). This structure supports data triangulation and enables the interpretation of broad adoption trends alongside in-depth experiential insights derived from the simulation-based study. The two samples represent different segments of the AEC community. Q1 includes a heterogeneous group of professionals and researchers, while Q2 focuses on participants directly involved in the experimental simulations. The demographic profiles reflect this distinction: the Q1 sample shows a higher mean age (52.4 years, range 24– 70), whereas the Q2 sample presents a lower mean age (37.4 years, range 21–68). This differentiation allows for the exploration of generational patterns in perceived adoption and use of experiential systems, without implying statistical generalization beyond the respective samples. Fig. 32 Schema of the Q1-Q2 Questionnaire. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Tab. 24 Schema of the Q1-Q2 Questionnaire. As summarized in Table 25, Q1 supports a contextual and descriptive analysis of adoption trends and perceived barriers within the AEC sector. Q2, instead, enables a comparative and exploratory evaluation of perceived experiential effects associated with the application of the proposed framework. The analytical methods applied to Q2 include Plus/Delta Analysis, comparative perception analysis by professional background and age group, correlation analysis between perceptual dimensions, and descriptive statistical testing of perceived differences between unstructured and structured simulation conditions. The empirical results highlight consistent patterns across the two instruments. Participants in both samples reported a low self-assessed level of operational familiarity with immersive and experiential technologies (mean value 1.95 on a five-point scale), indicating limited integration into routine professional practice. At the same time, respondents expressed a high perceived potential of these technologies, with mean values of 3.91 (Q1) and 3.90 (Q2). This contrast suggests a gap between perceived opportunity and actual implementation. Perceived implementation difficulty shows comparable values in both samples (mean 3.36 for Q1 and 3.35 for Q2), indicating that barriers are recognized as relevant but not prohibitive. Reliability analysis confirms acceptable internal consistency for Q1 (Cronbach’s α = 0.63) and high internal consistency for Q2 (Cronbach’s α = 0.94), supporting the use of Q2 for in-depth analysis of self-reported experiential dimensions. Overall, the analysis focuses on perceived understanding, interaction quality, and experiential clarity, rather than on objective performance metrics. Statistical analyses are therefore interpreted as indicative of perceptual trends and comparative patterns within the study context, providing structured empirical support for the interpretation of the framework’s role in shaping experiential and collaborative dynamics. The data, processed with Power BI, provided clear and illustrative graphs of the current state, allowing for a descriptive analysis of the information obtained. Results related to the current use of XR systems in professional practice (Fig. 33) show that XR technologies are used infrequently by most respondents. While 59% report having never used them, an additional 32% indicate only occasional use. Regular use is limited to 9% of the sample. These findings suggest that, despite increasing visibility, experiential systems remain marginal in routine operational activities.At the same time, expectations regarding future adoption are markedly positive: 92% of respondents believe that XR- based systems will become widely adopted in the coming years. A minority of participants expressed skepticism, mainly associated with perceived complexity, limited awareness, and implementation costs. The contrast between low current usage and high expected diffusion reflects a gap between perceived potential and present operational constraints. In this study, this pattern is interpreted as an anticipatory technological dissonance, understood as an interpretative construct describing the divergence between expectations and current practice, rather than as a validated theoretical model Perceived difficulty of use emerges as a relevant factor: approximately 37% of respondents rated the difficulty of using these systems at the higher end of the scale (values 4–5, Table 26). This perception may be associated with the rapid evolution of technologies and the consequent challenges in consolidating stable professional practices. Regarding perceived benefits, 83% of respondents indicated that Representation Ecosystems have the potential to reduce errors and misunderstandings, reflecting a positive attitude toward their role in supporting communication and shared understanding. It is important to note that this result refers to self-reported perceptions, not to objectively measured reductions in error rates. As shown in Table 26, 44.07% of respondents assigned the maximum score to the relevance of these systems within the construction sector, while only 8.47% expressed minimal relevance. A similar trend is observed in perceptions of the contribution of VR/AR technologies to innovation, with responses concentrated in the upper Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management range of the scale. Among the perceived barriers to adoption, the lack of standards and guidelines was consistently identified as critical, with more than half of respondents assigning high relevance to this issue, confirming the role of regulatory uncertainty as a limiting factor for large-scale implementation. Analysis by professional role (Fig. 34) reveals differentiated perception patterns. Researchers, engineers, and BIM coordinators tend to report higher awareness of the potential of experiential systems, while architects show more heterogeneous responses. Professionals operating in public-sector contexts appear more cautious, likely reflecting the absence of consolidated regulatory frameworks and shared operational references. Overall, the observed heterogeneity does not indicate inconsistency, but rather reflects the non-uniform and gradual nature of technological adoption across interconnected professional ecosystems. The Q1 results therefore provide contextual evidence supporting the need for structured frameworks capable of reducing uncertainty and supporting more consistent integration of experiential systems in practice.
