The objective is not to assess objective performance outcomes, but to explore how participants experienced and interpreted the simulations, comparing an unstructured configuration with a framework-based structured condition. The analysis is based on descriptive and comparative statistics applied to self-reported measures, complemented by qualitative feedback. This approach enables an in-depth examination of experiential, perceptual, and interpretative dimensions emerging during the simulations, while maintaining consistency with the exploratory nature of the study. The section is structured into a set of analytical components, each addressing a specific aspect of the experience: Plus/Delta Analysis, used to identify perceived strengths and weaknesses of framework-structured simulations in comparison with the unstructured baseline; Correlation analysis between key experiential dimensions, aimed at exploring relationships among perceptual variables (e.g., clarity, involvement, perceived usefulness); Comparative analysis by professional background, to examine how participants’ roles and experience influence interaction with structured representation ecosystems; Assessment of perceived spatial and design understanding, focusing on participants’ subjective evaluation of their ability to interpret spatial and project-related information; Analysis of perceptual impact by age and judgment, investigating how age-related factors influence experiential perception under different configurations; Intergenerational perceptual trends, highlighting variations in interaction patterns with representation ecosystems across age groups; 1,00 1,00 1,00 1,83 2,00 2,40 2,50 4,00 5,00 0 1 2 3 4 5 Program Manager Public employee Surveyor Architect Lighting designer Engineer BIM Experts Computer scientist Researcher Average of Immersive Experience Pr of es si on al R ol e Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Dynamics of representation ecosystems, including descriptive analyses and statistical testing of self- reported measures, aimed at identifying comparative differences between unstructured and structured conditions. Throughout this section, statistical analyses are interpreted as indicative of comparative perceptual trends within the study sample, rather than as evidence of causal or performance-based effects. This framing ensures coherence with the methodological assumptions outlined in the previous sections and supports a rigorous interpretation of the framework’s role in shaping experiential and collaborative dynamics. 5.6.3.1 Plus -Delta Analisis The Plus/Delta analysis provides qualitative insights into participants’ perceived strengths and limitations of the different experiential configurations adopted in the simulations. Rather than indicating intrinsic technical superiority, the results highlight how the perceived effectiveness of experiential systems is strongly dependent on their alignment with the specific context of use. Participants consistently reported a situational preference between individual and collective systems. Individual experiential devices (e.g., head-mounted displays) were perceived as more suitable for detailed inspection and focused interaction, whereas collective environments (such as virtual theaters) were associated with higher perceived support for discussion and collaboration, despite acknowledged technical constraints. This observation is coherent with the Contextual Technology Acceptance Model [135], which emphasizes the role of contextual factors in shaping user acceptance and perceived usefulness. An experiential paradox emerged from the feedback: features perceived as advantageous in one configuration were often perceived as limiting in another (Tables 27, 28, 29). For example, the absence of direct interaction with the model in the virtual theater intentionally designed to support collective presentation was perceived as a constraint in sessions where participants expected a more active and exploratory role. Similar dynamics have been reported in the literature, where intentional interaction constraints can evolve into perceived operational barriers [106]. Another recurring theme concerns perceived visual quality. Participants reported a higher tolerance for lower visual resolution in collective settings compared to individual experiences. This suggests that social and collaborative factors may mitigate the perceived impact of technical limitations, pointing to an interaction between social presence and perceptual thresholds that remains underexplored in current research. The analysis also highlights the role of the information structure based on predefined prerequisites and requirements. While this structure was generally perceived as helpful in guiding the simulations, participants reported the emergence of additional, unforeseen needs linked to specific professional roles. This phenomenon aligns with the concept of “emergence of ongoing requirements” [136,137], suggesting that representation ecosystems should be conceived as adaptive systems capable of evolving during use rather than as static configurations. Differences in perception were also observed across professional profiles. Participants’ feedback confirms a relationship between professional specialization and sensitivity to technological limitations, consistent with findings by Okoro et al. [138]. Role-specific needs such as the integration of temporal information, regulatory references, or domain-specific data were frequently mentioned, reinforcing the idea that perceived usefulness is strongly influenced by users’ expertise and responsibilities. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Finally, participants identified a range of potential application domains extending beyond the original scope of the simulations, including architectural restoration, construction site management, fire prevention, interdisciplinary validation, and accessibility assessment. These indications do not constitute evidence of effectiveness in these domains but rather reflect perceived opportunities for broader application. Overall, the Plus/Delta analysis confirms that participants’ expectations tend to evolve during the experience, a phenomenon described as feature expectation evolution [139]. Even in the presence of predefined requirements, users developed new expectations not anticipated during the design phase, reinforcing the need for a flexible and incremental approach to the design of experiential and representation ecosystems. Integration of avatars with motor limitations to more effectively evaluate solutions for overcoming architectural barriers, promoting inclusivity. 5.6.3.2 Correlation Matrix between Key Experience Dimensions This analysis explores the relationships between key self-reported experiential dimensions—including presence, involvement, immersion, emotional response, perceived skills, usability, and overall judgment—in order to examine how these dimensions interact under unstructured and framework-based configurations. The analysis is based on correlation matrices [140] computed separately for simulations conducted without the framework and those conducted with the framework. The objective is not to infer causal effects, but to identify patterns of association that characterize experiential dynamics under different configuration conditions. Correlation Matrix  Framework-Free Simulations In the absence of a framework (Fig. 36), presence shows a strong positive correlation with the final judgment (r = 0.83), indicating that the perceived sense of “being there” is closely associated with the overall evaluation of the experience in unstructured conditions. Involvement also correlates with the final judgment (r = 0.67), suggesting that active participation is related to perceived experiential quality, although with moderate strength. Emotional response and immersion exhibit similarly high correlations with the final judgment (r = 0.80 and r = 0.69 respectively), highlighting the relevance of affective and perceptual components in shaping overall impressions in unguided configurations. The strongest association is observed between perceived technical competence and final judgment (r = 0.89). This pattern suggests that, in framework-free simulations, participants’ evaluations are strongly associated with their self-assessed ability to interpret and manage the system, indicating a higher dependence on individual skills. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management In contrast, usability shows weak correlations across most dimensions, suggesting that, in the absence of a reference structure, interaction is perceived as less coherent and less intuitive, which may limit the accessibility of the experience. Correlation matrix for simulations with framework With the introduction of the structured framework (Figure 35), interdimensional relationships are significantly strengthened. The correlation between presence and final judgment reaches very high values (r = 0.96), indicating that the framework contributes to a more stable and coherent perception of the experiential environment. With the introduction of the framework (Figure 36), the correlation structure appears more consolidated. The association between presence and final judgment increases (r = 0.96), indicating a stronger alignment between the sense of presence and the overall evaluation of the experience under structured conditions. Involvement shows a stronger relationship with presence (r = 0.89), suggesting that structured configurations are associated with more coherent links between participation and perceived experiential quality. Similarly, correlations between emotional response, immersion, and final judgment increase (r = 0.91 and r = 0.79 respectively), pointing to a tighter coupling between affective perception and overall evaluation. The correlation between perceived technical competence and final judgment slightly decreases (r = 0.86). While still high, this reduction suggests that the framework may be associated with a lower reliance on individual expertise, potentially supporting more uniform experiential evaluations across participants. Finally, usability shows a general increase in correlation values across dimensions, although its relationship with immersion remains moderate. This indicates that, while the framework contributes to improved perceived coherence and interpretability, technological mediation continues to influence the experiential balance between usability and immersive perception.Engagement also shows a significant increase in correlation with presence (r = 0.89), suggesting that structured implementations foster more fluid and participatory modes of interaction. Similarly, emotional response and immersion improve their relationship with final judgment (r = 0.91 and 0.79), indicating a strengthening of the link between affective experience and perceived quality. The correlation of technical competence decreases slightly (r = 0.86), suggesting that the framework can reduce the dependence on individual skills, making the experience more accessible and inclusive. Finally, usability shows a general increase in all dimensions, while remaining moderate in relation to immersion, an aspect that indicates how technological mediation continues to play a decisive role in the overall quality of the experience. Fig. 36 Correlation matrix on experimental dimensions for simulations with framework. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 5.6.3.3 Descriptive and comparative statistical analysis of virtual ecosystem perceptions by profession This section examines differences in self-reported perceptions of the Representative Ecosystems across professional groups, with the aim of exploring how background and professional role relate to experiential evaluation. The analysis considers the following perceptual dimensions: presence, immersion, engagement, emotional response, usability, perceived skills, and overall judgment. Simulations Without Framework In the unstructured condition (Figure 37), perceptual evaluations vary across professional categories, reflecting differentiated approaches to the virtual ecosystem. Researchers report the highest scores across most dimensions, including presence (4.34), emotional response (4.44), perceived skills (4.89), and overall judgment (3.70), indicating a generally positive and exploratory attitude toward the simulation. BIM managers show high presence (4.27) and a positive overall judgment (3.46), but lower immersion (2.67), suggesting a more analytical and tool- oriented interaction. Engineers present a more balanced profile, with moderate presence (3.28) and immersion (2.41), and an overall judgment of 3.15, reflecting a pragmatic evaluation focused on functional aspects. Students report relatively high immersion (3.06), engagement (3.62), and emotional response (3.52), but a more cautious overall judgment (3.38), potentially reflecting limited professional experience and evolving expectations. Architects report lower scores in immersion (2.63) and emotional response (2.84), but a comparatively higher perception of usability (3.04). Their overall judgment (2.48) is the lowest among the groups, suggesting a weaker alignment between the unstructured ecosystem and their professional practices. Simulations With Framework BIM Managers achieve high results in presence (4.27) and a positive overall rating (3.46), but with lower immersion (2.67). This suggests a more rational and technical approach, linked to their familiarity with complex digital tools. Engineers show a more balanced profile, with moderately low presence (3.28) and immersion (2.41), but an overall rating of 3.15 and perceived consequences of 3.71, indicating a pragmatic and functional use of the experiential ecosystem (Figure 38). Students report high levels of immersion (3.06), engagement (3.62) and emotion (3.52), but are more critical in the overall evaluation (3.38), perhaps due to their lesser professional experience and still developing expectations. Architects adopt a more pragmatic approach, with lower scores in immersion (2.63) and emotion (2.84), but a better perception of usability (3.04). However, their overall evaluation (2.48) is the lowest, indicating the need for more customizable and contextual systems to their operational environment. Comparative Discussion Across professional categories, the introduction of the framework is associated with consistently higher self- reported evaluations of the experience. Differences between groups remain evident, reflecting distinct professional expectations and interaction styles. Perceived skills show the largest variation across professional roles, indicating a strong association between professional background and confidence in interacting with the ecosystem. Under the structured configuration, these differences appear slightly reduced, suggesting a potential leveling effect of predefined prerequisites and information structures. Presence remains relatively stable across professional categories, while dimensions such as immersion and engagement show moderate and consistent increases. These variations should be interpreted as perceptual trends within the study sample, influenced by both professional role and individual factors, rather than as statistically generalizable effects. 5.6.3.4 Impact of a Structured and Unstructured Virtual Ecosystem on Perceptual and Experiential Dimensions This section examines how self-reported spatial and design understanding relates to other perceptual dimensions of the experience, comparing simulations conducted without and with the application of the proposed framework. The analysis aims to explore how the presence of a structured information and experiential configuration is associated with variations in perceived presence, engagement, immersion, emotional response, skills, usability, and overall evaluation. The analysis is based on simple linear regressions, in which perceived spatial and design understanding is treated as the independent variable and the remaining experiential dimensions as dependent variables. The results are interpreted as associative patterns within a self-reported dataset, without implying causal relationships. Simulations Without Framework In the unstructured condition, perceived spatial and design understanding shows its strongest associations with immersion (R² = 0.57, p = 0.0075) and emotional response (R² = 0.53, p = 0.0110) (Table 30). These results suggest that, in the absence of a guiding structure, participants’ understanding of spatial and design aspects is closely related to how immersive and emotionally engaging the experience is perceived to be. Moderate associations are observed with presence (R² = 0.42, p = 0.0304) and perceived skills (R² = 0.43, p = 0.0279), indicating that spatial understanding contributes to these dimensions, while remaining influenced by additional factors such as prior experience and technological familiarity. Engagement shows a weaker relationship (R² = 0.32, p = 0.0688), suggesting that active participation is less directly associated with spatial understanding alone and may depend on interaction design and task structure. Usability appears largely independent of spatial understanding, confirming that ease of use is primarily related to interface and interaction characteristics rather than to perceptual comprehension. Overall, these patterns indicate that, in unstructured simulations, perceived spatial understanding is associated with selected experiential dimensions, but the experiential configuration remains fragmented, with limited integration between perceptual, interactive, and cognitive aspects. Simulations With Framework With the introduction of the framework, the association patterns become more consolidated (Table 31). Strong relationships emerge between perceived spatial and design understanding and presence (R² = 0.77, p < 0.001) and engagement (R² = 0.73, p < 0.001), indicating a closer alignment between comprehension, participation, and the overall sense of involvement (Figure 39). Associations with immersion (R² = 0.68, p = 0.002) and emotional response (R² = 0.74, p < 0.001) remain high, suggesting that structured configurations are associated with a more coherent relationship between understanding and affective-perceptual experience. The relationship with perceived skills remains moderate (R² = 0.43, p = 0.028), indicating that confidence in interacting with the ecosystem continues to depend partly on individual background. Usability shows a modest increase in association (R² = 0.36, p = 0.0498), while remaining one of the least directly related dimensions, highlighting the persistent role of interface design. The overall evaluation of the experience also shows a strong Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management association with perceived spatial and design understanding (R² = 0.77, p < 0.001), indicating that, under structured conditions, comprehension and overall judgment tend to align more consistently. Comparison of the Obtained Data Comparing the two conditions, the framework-based simulations exhibit a more integrated pattern of associations between perceived spatial understanding and other experiential dimensions. In the unstructured configuration, understanding primarily relates to immersion and emotional response, whereas in the structured configuration it is associated with a broader set of dimensions, including engagement and overall evaluation (Figure 40). These results suggest that the framework is associated with a greater coherence of perceptual relationships, rather than with isolated experiential effects. Dimensions such as perceived skills and usability show more limited variation, indicating areas where further refinement of interaction design may be required. Importantly, these findings refer to self-reported perceptions within a controlled experimental setting and should be interpreted as exploratory indications of how structured information and experiential configurations relate to perceived understanding and experience, rather than as evidence of objective performance improvements. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 5.6.3.5 Effects of Framework Implementation on Spatial and Design Understanding This section examines how self-reported spatial and design understanding relates to selected individual characteristics namely age and prior experience with BIM and experiential technologies under unstructured and framework-based simulation conditions. The analysis is based on linear regression models and is intended to explore associative patterns, rather than to identify causal effects. Simulations without the framework In the unstructured condition (Table 32), the regression models do not reveal statistically significant relationships between perceived spatial and design understanding and the individual variables considered. Prior experience with experiential technologies shows no association with spatial understanding (R² = 0.001, p = 0.925), while experience with BIM tools similarly exhibits a negligible relationship (R² = 0.001, p = 0.932). Age shows a weak negative association (β = −0.021), with younger participants tending to report slightly higher levels of perceived spatial understanding (R² = 0.147, p = 0.219), although this relationship is not statistically significant (Figure 41). Overall, these results suggest that, in the absence of a structured framework, perceived spatial and design understanding varies heterogeneously across participants, without clear associations with technological experience or demographic variables Simulations with the framework With the introduction of the framework (Table 33), the regression models remain statistically non-significant; however, changes in the direction and distribution of associations can be observed. The negative relationship between age and perceived spatial understanding remains consistent (R² = 0.149, p = 0.241; Fig. 42), indicating a stable age-related pattern across both conditions(Figure 42). Experience with BIM tools shows a slight shift from a neutral to a weakly positive association (β = 0.014), although without statistical significance (R² = 0.001, p = 0.932). Experience with experiential technologies (AR/VR) remains essentially neutral (β = 0.016, R² = 0.001, p = 0.925). These patterns suggest that, while the framework does not produce statistically detectable effects at the individual-variable level, it may be associated with a reconfiguration of how participants relate their prior experience to the simulation. Comparative interpretation Comparing the two conditions, the results indicate subtle differences in the way individual characteristics relate to perceived spatial and design understanding. While age-related patterns remain stable across configurations, the framework-based condition shows indications of a more integrated use of prior BIM experience, albeit at an exploratory level. These findings suggest that spatial and design understanding in experiential simulations is not strongly determined by technological expertise alone, but is influenced by a combination of individual characteristics and the overall structure of the representation ecosystem. From this perspective, adaptive and user-aware configuration strategies appear more relevant than rigid, expertise-driven assumptions. Given the limited sample size, these results should be interpreted as exploratory indications. Future studies involving larger and more differentiated samples could further investigate how user profile, technological background, and structured information environments interact in shaping perceived spatial understanding. Within Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management the scope of this research, the findings highlight the importance of carefully defining user-related prerequisites during the framework configuration phase, in order to support more coherent and accessible experiential simulations. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 42 Linear Regression Plots for the Analysis of the Impact of Age and Spatial and Design Understanding on the Ecosystem (with framework). 5.6.3.6 Impact of Age on Virtual Ecosystem Perception: A Comparative Framework Analysis This section explores the relationship between age and self-reported perceptual dimensions of the virtual ecosystem, comparing simulations conducted with and without the proposed framework. Linear regression models were used to examine associative patterns between age and six experiential dimensions: presence, engagement, immersion, emotional response, perceived skills, and usability. The analysis is exploratory and does not aim to establish causal effects. Simulations without the framework In the unstructured condition (Figure 43, Table 34), relationships between age and perceptual variables are weak and statistically non-significant, although some consistent trends can be observed. Presence shows a negative association (β = −0.03, p = 0.214), suggesting that younger participants tend to report a slightly stronger sense of “being there.” A similar trend is observed for engagement (β = −0.04, p = 0.174). Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Immersion appears largely independent of age (β = −0.002, p = 0.976), while emotional response shows a weak negative association (β = −0.02, p = 0.509). Perceived skills exhibit the strongest age-related trend (β = −0.05, p = 0.051), indicating that younger participants tend to report higher confidence in interacting with the ecosystem, although this relationship does not reach statistical significance. Usability shows no meaningful association with age (β = 0.01, p = 0.874). Overall, in the absence of a framework, age-related differences remain limited and the experiential evaluation appears primarily influenced by individual factors rather than demographic characteristics. Simulations with the framework With the introduction of the framework (Figure 44, table 35), age-related patterns become more consistent across selected dimensions. The association between age and presence reaches statistical significance (β = −0.03, p = 0.045), indicating a stronger alignment between age and perceived presence under structured conditions. A statistically significant relationship also emerges between age and perceived skills (β = −0.04, p = 0.022), suggesting that the framework-based configuration is associated with reduced variability in self-reported competence across age groups. Other dimensions show limited changes. Emotional response approaches statistical significance (β = −0.03, p = 0.082), while engagement (β = −0.02, p = 0.263) and immersion (β = −0.01, p = 0.473) remain weakly associated with age. Usability continues to show no relationship with age (β ≈ 0, p = 0.995), indicating stable perceived accessibility across generations. Comparative interpretation Comparing the two configurations, the framework-based simulations are associated with more structured and consistent age-related perceptual patterns, particularly for presence and perceived skills. While these findings do not imply causal effects, they suggest that a clear methodological structure may be associated with a more homogeneous experiential perception across age groups. Usability remains independent of age in both conditions, indicating that interface accessibility is perceived as broadly stable. The emergence of statistically significant associations for presence and skills under the structured configuration should be interpreted cautiously, given the exploratory nature of the analysis and the limited sample size. Overall, the results indicate that age plays a secondary but stable role in shaping experiential perception, and that structured representation ecosystems may support a more balanced distribution of perceived presence and competence across generations. These findings point toward the relevance of adaptive and user-aware configuration strategies in the design of inclusive experiential simulations. The variables considered are Emotion, Engagement, Immersion, Presence, Skills, and Usability, analyzed as a function of participant age. In the framework-free condition, the results show greater variability across age groups. Perceptual dimensions such as Skills and Presence exhibit higher values among younger participants, followed by a gradual decline in older age groups. Usability remains relatively stable across ages, while Immersion displays a more irregular pattern, with lower values generally reported by older participants. Overall, younger participants tend to report more intense and differentiated experiences, particularly in terms of Engagement and Emotional response, whereas older participants report more attenuated perceptions, especially for Presence and Immersion. In the framework-based condition, perceptual trends appear more stable and consistent across age groups. Differences between age ranges are reduced, with Presence, Engagement, and Skills showing higher and more Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management uniform values. Usability remains consistently high across all ages, suggesting that perceived ease of interaction is largely independent of age under structured conditions. Immersion also presents a more balanced trend, with reduced dispersion compared to the unstructured simulations. From a comparative perspective, the framework-based configuration is associated with a reduction in score dispersion across age groups and with more homogeneous perceptual profiles. The most pronounced differences between conditions are observed for Skills and Engagement, which show greater variability and age dependence in the absence of a framework, and more stable patterns when the framework is applied. Finally, the comparison suggests that age-related declines observed in the unstructured simulations particularly for Presence, Engagement, and Immersion are less pronounced under the structured configuration. These results should be interpreted as descriptive perceptual trends within the study sample, indicating that a defined information and experiential structure is associated with more consistent self-reported experiences across age groups. Fig. 45 Trends in User Experience as a Function of Age (without framework). Fig. 46 Trends in User Experience as a Function of Age (with framework). 5.6.3.8 Descriptive Analysis and Statistical Significance Testing The results indicate significant changes across several key experiential parameters, confirming the framework’s influence on user perception and interaction (Figure 47 and Table 36). A substantial reduction in average simulation duration was observed between the unstructured and structured configurations, decreasing from 17.25 to 12.00 minutes (−30.43%). This variation is interpreted as an Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management observational indicator of procedural streamlining rather than a direct measure of operational efficiency. The reduction is likely associated with clearer task framing, well-defined objectives, and more coherent information organization, which minimized redundant actions and enabled participants to focus on core activities. A comparable trend is evident in the number of support requests, defined as explicit requests for assistance related to navigation, information access, model interpretation, or procedural clarification during the simulation. The 50% decrease in support requests suggests that participants perceived the structured configuration as more intelligible and easier to manage, resulting in reduced reliance on facilitators. This metric reflects perceived autonomy and interaction clarity rather than objective usability performance. From a perceptual standpoint, all analyzed dimensions show higher mean values under the framework-based configuration. Perceived spatial and design understanding shows an increase of 14.71%, indicating that participants experienced greater clarity in interpreting the digital model. Presence (+12.07%) and Immersion (+19.40%) also increase, suggesting a more coherent experiential perception of the Representative Ecosystem. Similarly, Engagement (+19.40%) and Emotional response (+14.53%) exhibit higher values, reflecting deeper involvement as reported by participants. Perceived skills increase more moderately (+6.79%), indicating that while the framework supports interpretability and confidence, it does not substantially alter self-assessed competence. Usability (+9.50%) also increases, suggesting improved perceived ease of interaction, particularly for users with limited prior technological experience. Inferential statistical testing (independent-sample t-tests) was applied to these self-reported measures to assess whether observed differences between configurations exceed random variation within the study sample. All tested dimensions yielded p-values below the 0.05 threshold (Table 37), indicating statistically significant differences within the context of this exploratory dataset. These results support the identification of consistent comparative trends, but do not constitute evidence of objective performance improvement or generalizable effects. These findings should be interpreted as evidence of how structured prerequisites and information requirements relate to users’ perceived understanding, interaction, and experiential clarity, rather than as confirmation of causal or efficiency-based outcomes. Questionnaire Subchapter Analysis Main Findings and Research Question Alignment Q1: General Experiential BIM Assessment (GEBA) Adoption and Usage Trends of Experiential Representation Systems in the AEC Sector The survey (n = 59) highlights a marked discrepancy between current use and expected future adoption of experiential representation systems. While the majority of respondents report limited or no direct experience, a large proportion anticipates widespread professional adoption. Key perceived barriers include the absence of shared guidelines, implementation costs, and limited training opportunities. Perceptions vary across professional roles, with BIM-related profiles reporting higher openness and public-sector professionals expressing greater caution. Alignment with RQ1 & RQ3: These findings provide contextual support for RQ1 by identifying the lack of structured references as a relevant sectoral issue and inform RQ3 by showing how professional background is associated with differing levels of perceived readiness and acceptance. Q2: Framework Evaluation (FE) Plus/Delta Analysis: Strengths and Weaknesses of Structured Experiential Simulations Qualitative feedback identifies collaboration support and perceived clarity of information as recurrent strengths, while physical discomfort and hardware-related constraints emerge as limitations. Participants emphasize that perceived usefulness varies depending on the application context. Alignment with RQ2: The results indicate that the framework is associated with perceived experiential benefits, while reinforcing the importance of contextual adaptation rather than universal configurations. Correlation Matrix: Interdependencies between Key Perceptual Variables (with vs. without framework) The correlation analysis shows more consolidated and coherent associations among perceptual dimensions under the framework-based configuration. Higher correlations between presence, engagement, and overall judgment indicate a reorganization of perceptual relationships rather than isolated metric changes. Alignment with RQ2: These patterns support an interpretative reading of the framework as contributing to greater coherence among experiential dimensions, based on self-reported measures. Comparative Analysis of Representative Ecosystem Perception by Professional Category Perceptual evaluations differ across professional groups. Researchers and BIM-related profiles report higher perceived presence and skills, while architects and public-sector professionals express more critical evaluations. Alignment with RQ3: The findings highlight that perceived value and acceptance are strongly associated with professional role, suggesting that adaptability and role-aware configuration are central to cross-disciplinary adoption. Perceived Spatial and Design Understanding under Framework-Based Configuration Participants report higher levels of perceived spatial and design understanding under the structured configuration. The observed increase reflects subjective clarity in model interpretation rather than objective performance measures. Alignment with RQ1 & RQ2: These results support RQ1 by reinforcing the relevance of an entity-based framework structure and inform RQ2 by illustrating perceived experiential differences associated with framework application. Comparative Analysis of Age-Related Perceptual Trends In the absence of a framework, perceptual dimensions such as presence and engagement show greater variability across age groups. Under the structured configuration, these variations are reduced, and perceptual evaluations appear more homogeneous. Alignment with RQ3: The findings suggest that structured configurations are associated with more consistent experiential perceptions across age groups, supporting considerations of inclusivity and accessibility. Dynamics of Representative Ecosystems: Descriptive Analysis and Statistical Testing Comparative analyses show consistent differences between unstructured and structured configurations across multiple self-reported experiential dimensions. Reductions in simulation duration and support requests are interpreted as observational indicators of perceived clarity and procedural streamlining. Statistical testing indicates that observed differences exceed random variation within the study sample. Alignment with RQ2: These results support the identification of comparative perceptual trends associated with the framework, without implying objective performance gains or causal effects. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 6 SECOND CASE STUDY: MILANO MIND - CASCINA MERLATA Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 6.1 Introduction The second case study examines a real-world application of the Structured Experiential Framework within the administrative procedure for the new Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata railway station. The primary objective of the case study was to collect empirical evidence on the framework’s effectiveness in supporting the integration of representation systems into public-sector decision-making and collaborative processes, with specific reference to administrative evaluation activities. The experimental process was articulated into five sequential phases, each addressing a distinct level of the framework, from information configuration to experience assessment: Sharing and preparation: acquisition and optimization of 3D models of the building and surrounding urban context, provided by RFI, aimed at ensuring geometric coherence and informational consistency of the initial dataset. Information structuring: definition of prerequisites and requirements aligned with the specific needs of institutional stakeholders involved in the administrative procedure and with the objectives of project evaluation. Construction of the Representative Ecosystem: implementation of a shared representation environment configured to support collaborative access, spatial navigation, and multi-perspective project assessment. Procedural simulation: execution of collaborative sessions within an experiential Conferenza dei Servizi, involving institutional representatives and technical professionals. Data collection and analysis: systematic collection of data related to user experience, perceived communication effectiveness, and interaction dynamics among participants. This case study represents a key step in validating the framework under real operational conditions, assessing its capacity to support project understanding, enhance transparency in decision-making processes, and facilitate interinstitutional collaboration within an established administrative context. 6.2 Identification of the Historical and Territorial Context The Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata railway station (Figure 48) is located in the north-western area of Milan and is embedded within a complex, multi-scale territorial and infrastructural context involving both the regional railway network and the metropolitan urban system. The project, with an estimated investment of €33.5 million, is situated along the Milan–Varese railway line between Rho Fiera and Milano Certosa and is designed to serve as a strategic access node to the Milano Innovation District (MIND) and the surrounding residential areas. The study area is characterized by a significant historical and regulatory stratification. Its evolution spans from the ancient Bosco della Merlata, lost in the nineteenth century, through the seventeenth-century Cascina Merlata— restored during Expo 2015—to the recent redevelopment of the former exhibition site into the MIND innovation district. Throughout the twentieth century, industrial expansion and subsequent decommissioning processes Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management triggered progressive urban transformations, culminating in the large-scale territorial reconfiguration initiated for Expo 2015, which acted as a catalyst for the current infrastructural and mobility enhancement strategies. This evolution has resulted in what can be defined as a layered constraint configuration, in which multiple regulatory and technical frameworks coexist. These include landscape protection constraints managed by the Superintendency, railway safety regulations established by ANSFISA, environmental requirements monitored by ARPA, metropolitan urban planning regulations, and the technical specifications defined by RFI for railway infrastructure. From a functional standpoint, the area is subject to significant urban and infrastructural growth. Current estimates indicate approximately 10,000 daily users, with a projected increase driven by the new Galeazzi Hospital (600 beds), the University of Milan campus (approximately 23,000 users by 2027), the Human Technopole, and new residential developments expected to accommodate around 15,000 inhabitants. As a result, the future railway station is designed to serve an estimated demand of approximately 45,000 daily users. Within this context, the collaboration established with RFI enabled the use of the Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata project as a case study for the implementation and validation of the Structured Experiential Framework applied to administrative procedures. The case study was specifically conceived to assess how structured Representative Ecosystems can support decision-making activities, enhance communication among institutional stakeholders, and facilitate a shared and coherent understanding of complex infrastructural projects under real operational conditions. Fig. 48 Rappresentazione 3D della nuova fermata con fabbricato viaggiatori, passerella e pista ciclabile. 6.3 Identification of the Application Context The project for the new Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata railway station, promoted by Rete Ferroviaria Italiana (RFI) (CUP J52C21001910001), was selected as an appropriate case study for this exploratory investigation due to a combination of procedural, temporal, and informational factors. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management First, the administrative process is characterized by a high level of procedural complexity, involving multiple competent authorities operating at different institutional levels (Municipality of Milan, Lombardy Region, ANSFISA, among others), each responsible for specific regulatory evaluations. Second, the project was under approval at the time of the study, allowing the observation of decision-making dynamics within an active administrative procedure rather than a retrospective or simulated scenario. Third, the availability of structured technical documentation and federated BIM models provided a reliable information base for configuring representative simulations aligned with real procedural requirements. Within the overall intervention, the Passenger Building was selected as the focal element of the experiment due to its architectural relevance, its functional role within the station system, and its direct impact on the surrounding urban context. The case study focuses on the administrative procedure for the approval of the final design, initiated under the MIT–RFI Investment Programme Contract 2022–2026. The process is coordinated by the RFI Sole Procedure Manager (RUP) and conducted through a decision-making Conference of Services in a simplified and asynchronous format. Given the infrastructural nature of the project and the complexity of the territorial framework, the procedure involves expertise distributed across multiple institutional domains. Table 39 summarizes the main stakeholders involved, their areas of expertise, and the evaluation aspects addressed through the Structured Experiential Framework. Tab. 39 Framework Stakeholder Map: Domains of Expertise and Evaluation Aspects. In conventional administrative procedures, these elements are Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management typically assessed through separate and sequential authorization processes, often resulting in fragmented evaluations, communication redundancies, and limited cross-domain integration. The application of the Structured Experiential Framework aimed to address these limitations by enabling a unified and process-oriented representation of the project, structured according to administrative evaluation needs rather than technological features. The experimental implementation was articulated into the following operational phases: Acquisition and preparation of digital models: federated models provided by RFI and developed in collaboration with CREW – Cremonesi Workshop (in partnership with Italferr) were acquired and optimized. The dataset included the passenger building, platforms, vertical circulation systems, bicycle station, and pedestrian connections, ensuring consistency with the approved design documentation. Definition of framework prerequisites and requirements: the theoretical framework developed in the research was applied to define prerequisites and requirements for the simulations. Requirement levels were calibrated according to stakeholder roles and the specific evaluation functions of the administrative procedure. Construction of the Representative Ecosystem: a shared representation environment was configured by integrating spatial, informational, and interaction components. Environmental impact, landscape integration, and mobility aspects were included, balancing visual clarity and technical performance to ensure accessibility across different devices. Technological setup and stakeholder preparation: participating institutions were provided with access credentials and operational guidelines. Compatibility tests and familiarization sessions were conducted to ensure effective interaction with the environment, while traditional administrative documentation was maintained in parallel to preserve legal validity. Execution of the Conferenza dei Servizi: the Conference was conducted using a hybrid approach, combining conventional administrative procedures with interaction within the structured Representative Ecosystem, in line with the traditional CDS model described in Chapter 4. Data collection and documentation: stakeholder observations were collected through voice annotations and recordings directly associated with spatial elements of the model. User experience data were gathered through structured questionnaires and semi-structured interviews. Data analysis: quantitative and qualitative analyses were conducted to evaluate the perceived effectiveness of the framework in supporting understanding, communication, and interaction during the administrative decision-making process. 6.4 Simulation Setup 6.4.1 Experimental Setup and Validation Methodology Since the study was conducted as part of an ongoing administrative proceeding, the experimental setup was designed to ensure full compliance with the provisions of Legislative Decree 127/2016, which governs the Services Conference. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management All mandatory procedural steps, including the formal convocation of the competent authorities, the technical investigation, the acquisition of institutional opinions, the collegial deliberation, and the official recording of the minutes, were conducted in accordance with established legal and administrative practices. The experiential simulation was introduced exclusively as a decision-support component, alongside conventional administrative documentation, thus preserving the full procedural and legal validity of the proceeding and its outcomes. In this context, the Representative Ecosystem did not replace existing administrative tools, but rather complemented them by providing an additional level of representation aimed at supporting interpretation, discussion, and coordination among stakeholders. A dedicated information structure was developed, in accordance with the framework of prerequisites and requirements defined in this research. In collaboration with RFI's technical staff, the requirements parameters were specified and calibrated based on the procedural objectives and the expertise of the participating stakeholders. These parameters were used as technical specifications for configuring the Representative Ecosystem. This approach enabled field validation of the framework under real-world operating conditions, allowing us to observe its effects on usability, perceived support for decision-making activities, and communication dynamics between institutional stakeholders, without altering the formal structure of the administrative procedure. 6.4.2 Objectives and Context The primary objective of the pilot application was to support a Conference of Services aimed at evaluating the urban and environmental impacts of the passenger building of the Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata railway station. The structured representation environment was configured to provide a shared collaborative space in which stakeholders could jointly analyze project implications in relation to regulatory constraints, spatial relationships, and landscape integration. Rather than focusing on technological immersion, the simulation emphasized clarity of information, coherence of spatial representation, and accessibility across different professional profiles. Compared to traditional two-dimensional documentation, the Representative Ecosystem enabled a more integrated and navigable representation of the project, supporting technical and institutional discussion through interactive exploration while remaining aligned with the formal requirements of the administrative decision-making process. 6.4.3 Definition of Prerequisites and Requirements 6.4.3.1 Prerequisites In the pilot application for the Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata station, the prerequisites (Table 40) were identified and structured according to the theoretical categorization defined by the framework, namely: purpose, temporal constraints, stakeholders, object of evaluation, and spatial context. The purpose of the activity was the execution of an experimental Conference of Services aimed at assessing the urban and environmental impacts of the new passenger building. The specific objective of the experiential integration was to provide a shared and collaborative representation environment capable of supporting the examination of the project in relation to its spatial context and applicable technical and regulatory constraints. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management A 60-day experimental timeframe was defined, during which the Representative Ecosystem remained asynchronously accessible. This configuration allowed institutional stakeholders to independently analyze the aspects within their respective domains of responsibility and to formulate observations and technical opinions within the legally defined procedural window. The institutional actors involved included ARPA Lombardia, Arexpo S.p.A., ATS – Metropolitan City of Milan (Department of Hygiene and Health Prevention), the Municipality of Milan (Urban Regeneration Directorate), the Ministry of Infrastructure and Transport (MIT), the Lombardy Region, and RFI S.p.A The experience was designed to be accessible to non-technical users, prioritizing intuitive and visually oriented interaction mechanisms that did not require prior expertise in advanced digital tools. The evaluation focused on the passenger building and its immediate urban surroundings within the Milano Innovation District (MIND). The simulation area was delimited by an operational radius of approximately 50–60 m from the main building. This boundary was defined according to technical criteria provided by RFI and corresponds to the safety perimeter and area of direct infrastructural influence within which morphological, functional, and environmental impacts are formally assessed during administrative procedures. Stakeholder needs and procedural objectives were translated into configurable parameters governing information structure, interaction logic, and representational scope. This process made it possible to examine how procedural requirements and participant competencies are reflected in the configuration of the Representative Ecosystem, enabling adaptation to different decision-making functions without modifying the underlying technological setup. In this context, the concept of presence was operationalized as a cognitive–operational condition rather than as sensory immersion. Presence was therefore associated with clarity of spatial representation, coherence between information and evaluation tasks, and perceived control over navigation and exploration, particularly within a Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management desktop-based environment. Presence and information density were differentiated according to three spatial bands: Central area (Passenger Building): high level of geometric and architectural detail, configured for collaborative navigation and discussion. Surrounding area (MIND context): intermediate level of detail, supporting contextual interpretation of the intervention within the immediate urban fabric. Extended area (general urban context): static spherical panoramic representation, intended to provide spatial orientation and perceptual continuity without interactive exploration. Within the central area, a shared collaborative mode was implemented, allowing users to freely navigate the space and jointly examine design elements. In contrast, the extended urban context was intentionally configured as non- interactive, functioning as a background reference rather than as an evaluative environment. Interaction mechanisms were calibrated to ensure accessibility across different access modes, primarily through keyboard, mouse, and touchscreen interfaces, which proved effective for desktop and tablet use. Synchronous audio communication was integrated to support real-time verbal exchange among participants within the same Representative Ecosystem. From a sensory perspective, the configuration focused on visual and auditory channels, adopting a deliberately minimal setup compatible with remote participation. Visual content was based on static geometric models and panoramic imagery, without animation or dynamic environmental effects. Ambient sound and additional sensory stimuli were excluded to avoid interpretive bias and to maintain consistency across users. Information content was organized across differentiated levels of technical detail. The passenger building and immediate MIND context were reconstructed with sufficient architectural resolution to support evaluation activities, while the extended urban environment was represented at a lower level of detail to provide territorial reference. Lighting conditions were fixed and unmodifiable to ensure a consistent visual perception across sessions. The depth of field was limited to approximately 50 m from the main building, focusing attention on elements directly relevant to the administrative evaluation. Alphanumeric data layers were not included in this experimental phase. Selected technical documents, including the landscape commission report and the data collection questionnaire, were made accessible within the Representative Ecosystem. In accordance with stakeholder needs, the system was required to support multi- device accessibility (desktop PCs, tablets, smartphones, and head-mounted displays), asynchronous access, and integrated audio communication, while maintaining a low entry barrier for non-technical users. Among the platforms evaluated (Table 42), Spatial.io was selected as a suitable solution due to its ability to support shared three-dimensional environments with stable cross-device access and integrated communication features. The platform was not selected for its technological sophistication per se, but for its capacity to host the Representative Ecosystem configured according to the framework’s prerequisites and requirements. Compared to more specialized BIM-oriented or rendering-engine-based solutions, Spatial.io provides a balanced trade-off between usability, interoperability, and representational clarity. While it does not offer native BIM semantics or advanced parametric control, it proved adequate for the objectives of the case study, which focused on supporting collaborative interpretation and administrative evaluation rather than detailed technical authoring. The platform therefore functioned as an enabling infrastructure, while the structure, levels of representation, interaction logic, and experiential configuration were governed by the Structured Experiential Framework rather than by platform-specific features. Representation Ecosystems Coding and Design System Unlike the previous case study conducted in a simulated context, the application of the framework within a real administrative procedure required the explicit operationalization of the theoretical constructs into a formalized coding and design system. This need emerged from organizational and procedural constraints associated with institutional decision-making processes, which demand clarity, repeatability, and traceability. The coding system was developed to align information levels and experiential requirements with the evaluation needs of the involved institutions, enabling procedural requirements to be translated into implementable and replicable configuration parameters. This approach allows public clients to request representation solutions that are consistent with predefined representation levels and that can be unambiguously interpreted by all stakeholders. The configuration adopted for the Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata case study was defined through the following code: CdS–P1–E2–I2–C1+F1, corresponding to the experimental setup specified in the framework (Table 43). In this coding system, presence (P1) is defined as virtual presence based on static three-dimensional representations without animation. Engagement (E2) corresponds to a multi-user collaborative mode, while immersion (I2) is limited to visual and auditory channels. Communication (C1) relies on traditional interaction devices (mouse, keyboard, touch), and information (F1) is conveyed primarily through visual geometric representations without overlayed data layers. This explicit definition clarifies that presence is configured as a cognitive–operational condition rather than as immersive sensory simulation, and that its determinants are related to information structure, spatial coherence, and interaction accessibility rather than to technological immersion. Tab. 43 Structure of the Coding System. Dimension Coding Levels CdS Specification Presence P P1: Presence, P2: Virtual (P1) Virtual presence with static 3D model without animations Engagement E E1: Interactive, E2: Collaborative E3: Mixed (E2) Only multi-user collaborative mode Immersion I I1: Monosensory, (I2) Sight + Hearing Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management I2: Multisensory Communication C C1: Traditional, C2: Gestural, C3: Advanced (C1) Traditional (mouse/keyboard/touch) Information F F1: Visual, F2: Audio F3: Multi-modal (F1) Mesh geometries without graphic overlays The Spatial.io platform was used to instantiate the Representative Ecosystem due to its capability to support shared three-dimensional environments with multi-device access and integrated audio communication. In line with the research objectives, the platform functioned as an enabling infrastructure rather than as an experimental variable. The configuration of presence, interaction, and information levels was governed by the framework requirements and remained independent of platform-specific features. To facilitate collaborative sessions, personalized avatars with functional color coding were implemented to distinguish institutional roles and support interaction management. Privacy, security, and confidentiality were addressed through standard organizational and procedural measures. In particular, RFI formally notified the participating administrations of the activation of the ecosystem, specifying its experimental purpose and participation modalities. Institutional consent, role-based access control, and the use of non-personal avatar identifiers were adopted, while no sensitive information beyond that already admissible within standard administrative procedures was exchanged. Tab. 44 Excerpt from the texture mapping and categorization table. Object Name.format Base Map Height Map Diffuse Map Normal Map Roughness Metallic Emessive Opacity External walls Passenger Building Tiles.png nn nn nn 90% 0 0 100% Internal walls Passenger Building Plaster-w.png nn nn nn 75% 0 0 90% External glazing Passenger Building Glass.png nn nn nn 0% 0 0 22% Flooring Passenger Building Travertine.png nn nn nn 99% 0 0 100% The construction of the Representative Ecosystem required the conversion and optimization of the architectural models provided by RFI. The original models, developed in Autodesk Revit, were exported in .dae (Collada) format to preserve geometric and material relationships and subsequently processed in Blender to reduce geometric complexity while maintaining spatial coherence. This workflow reflects current practices in public- sector digital projects, which increasingly rely on interoperable formats in line with Legislative Decree 36/2023 and Ministerial Decree 312/2021. Texturing and material assignment were carried out in accordance with the Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management technical specifications defined by the framework requirements, ensuring consistency with the objectives of the experimental Conference of Services. Once optimized, the models were exported in .glTF format and integrated into the platform. To provide territorial orientation without increasing interaction complexity, georeferenced spherical panoramic images of the surrounding urban context were incorporated as non-interactive background elements. The implemented architecture and coding system represent the operational translation of the theoretical framework of prerequisites and requirements. This configuration enabled the framework to be tested under real procedural conditions, supporting collaborative evaluation activities without altering the formal structure of the administrative process. The ecosystem remained accessible for sixty days, during which stakeholders could independently explore the environment and contribute observations. Seven participants completed the structured questionnaire, providing data for the qualitative and exploratory quantitative validation of the framework. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 51 Collaborative session on Spatial.io: 3D model of the Travelers Building with multiple connected avatars for design evaluation in a shared virtual environment. 6.4.5 Experimental Protocol Il protocollo sperimentale è stato articolato in una sequenza di fasi coordinate, progettate per rendere operativo il Framework all'interno di una procedura amministrativa reale. Nella prima fase, il framework è stato tradotto in un sistema di codifica esplicito utilizzato per definire le caratteristiche della simulazione. Il codice di configurazione risultante (CdS–P1–E2–I2–C1+F1) ha funzionato come specifica tecnica che ha guidato la configurazione dell'Ecosistema Rappresentativo, garantendo coerenza tra obiettivi procedurali, requisiti esperienziali e scelte rappresentazionali. Da una prospettiva metodologica, questo approccio dimostra come le dimensioni esperienziali possano essere specificate formalmente accanto ai tradizionali requisiti informativi. Pur essendo di natura esplorativa, il sistema di codifica suggerisce un potenziale percorso verso la definizione di specifiche di riferimento strutturate per la configurazione degli Ecosistemi Rappresentativi all'interno dei processi amministrativi e di approvvigionamento, da convalidare ulteriormente attraverso future applicazioni. La seconda fase ha riguardato la costruzione dell'Ecosistema Rappresentativo. I modelli architettonici sviluppati in Autodesk Revit sono stati esportati e ottimizzati in Blender per la preparazione e la texturizzazione della mesh, quindi convertiti in formato .glTF e importati nella piattaforma Spatial.io. Il contesto urbano circostante è stato rappresentato attraverso immagini panoramiche sferiche generate utilizzando Street View 360 ed elaborate per ottenere una rappresentazione visiva neutra e non dominante, coerente con gli obiettivi valutativi della simulazione. Nella fase finale, sono stati creati avatar personalizzati, differenziati tramite codifica a colori per riflettere i ruoli istituzionali dei partecipanti. L'ecosistema rappresentativo è stato quindi condiviso con gli stakeholder tramite un link di accesso dedicato, dando inizio alla fase sperimentale. I partecipanti hanno potuto esplorare l'ambiente in modo asincrono durante i tempi procedurali predefiniti. Al termine dell'esperienza, sette utenti hanno compilato un questionario, fornendo dati per la valutazione esplorativa qualitativa e quantitativa del framework. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 6.5 Data Collection and Sampling Data collection was designed according to a multidimensional and exploratory approach, consistent with the nature of the pilot study and with the objective of examining the perceived effects of the Structured Experiential Framework within a real administrative procedure. Rather than aiming at statistical generalization, the data collection strategy focused on capturing qualitative and self-reported evaluations related to understanding, communication, and interaction dynamics among institutional stakeholders. The data collection plan translated the research questions into survey instruments aligned with the framework of prerequisites and requirements, enabling a structured investigation of how the configured Representative Ecosystem was perceived and used in practice. Given the limited sample size (n = 7), the protocol explicitly prioritized informational richness and contextual relevance over statistical power. Participants were professionals directly involved in the administrative procedure and accessed the simulation asynchronously from their workplace. Interaction times were not recorded, as access duration varied depending on institutional role and individual evaluation tasks. The allocation between desktop PCs and advanced experiential devices depended on technological availability, representing a methodological limitation acknowledged and addressed in the discussion of results. Tab. 45 PLUS/DELTA Feedback Framework. Question PLUS DELTA Using the ecosystem approach, do I have benefits and/or deteriorations compared to traditional methods? Which aspects of the system seemed clearly superior to traditional methods? Have you noticed any limitations or shortcomings compared to classical methods that could slow adoption? What could facilitate or hinder the official adoption of this tool? Which features could facilitate official integration? What criticalities do you think could represent a brake in public or professional contexts? Additional suggestions or considerations? Which components already work well and deserve to be enhanced? What would you add or which parts of the system would you modify or eliminate? In addition to the structured questionnaire, a qualitative feedback framework based on the Plus/Delta methodology was integrated and adapted to the specific context of the Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata case study (Table 45). Unlike previous applications aimed at comparative evaluation between different simulation configurations, in this case the Plus/Delta framework was employed as a reflective and interpretative tool. Its purpose was not to assess performance or superiority, but to identify perceived strengths, limitations, and barriers to adoption of the Representative Ecosystem when compared with traditional administrative procedures. The questionnaire underwent a revision and adaptation process to ensure coherence with the experimental setup and the non-comparative nature of the study. The initial version, consisting of 77 items, was reduced to 57 variables through the removal of redundant or contextually irrelevant questions. Introductory items related to professional background, experience with BIM, and familiarity with representation systems were retained to support contextual interpretation of responses. Unlike earlier evaluation instruments that included objective usage metrics, the final questionnaire focused on subjective, contextual, and perceptual indicators. No objective cognitive performance measures were collected. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management The evaluation therefore reflects participants’ self-reported perceptions rather than measured cognitive outcomes, in line with the exploratory objectives of the study. Data were collected through a structured questionnaire administered via Microsoft Forms and accessed directly from within the Representative Ecosystem through a QR code. Participants received clear instructions and information regarding data processing in compliance with the GDPR (EU Regulation 2016/679), ensuring continuity of the experience and minimizing disruption during data collection. The questionnaire investigated nine theoretical dimensions: System Activity (8 items), Presence (6 items), Engagement (4 items), Immersion (3 items), Emotion (9 items), Ability (4 items), Usability (4 items), Practical Consequences (8 items), and Physical Consequences (6 items). In this context, presence was operationalized as a self-reported cognitive–operational condition related to perceived spatial understanding, control, and engagement, rather than as sensory immersion.. How understandable was the model compared to a traditional method? Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Practical-Operational Consequences How much did the simulation impact your ability to express technical judgment? Do you consider this tool reliable and professional for use in official contexts? To what extent did the structure help you: Identify criticalities or inconsistencies? Do you believe this approach can improve decision-making process times? Did the Representation Ecosystems make the key elements of the project clearer? Did you notice greater ease in using technologies thanks to this new approach? Do you believe the environment increased transparency and information sharing? Physical and Cognitive Consequences I found the experience effortless and fatigue-free. During the experience I did not have headaches. My eyes remained relaxed and without signs of fatigue. Sweating during interaction remained normal. I did not experience any nausea symptoms during interaction. I did not experience dizziness during the interaction. 6.6 Data Analysis and Interpretation of Results 6.6.1 Introduction The effects associated with the application of the Structured Experiential Framework in this second case study emerged as more heterogeneous than those observed in the Borgo Pirelli case. This variability is primarily attributable to the operational and institutional complexity of the real administrative context, which introduced multiple factors influencing user perception and interaction. Rather than indicating linear effects, the dataset reflects a range of differentiated responses that require cautious and context-aware interpretation. In line with the exploratory nature of the study and the limited sample size (n = 7), the analysis does not aim to establish statistically significant effects or causal relationships. Instead, it focuses on identifying perceptual patterns, reported tendencies, and interpretative insights relevant to understanding how the framework is experienced and evaluated by institutional stakeholders. The analysis of results (Figure 52) is structured across three complementary dimensions: Qualitative Analysis: based on the Plus/Delta methodology, aimed at identifying perceived strengths, limitations, and adoption barriers associated with the structured simulations when compared to traditional administrative procedures. User Analysis: examining relationships between participants’ self-reported experience levels and selected experiential dimensions, with the objective of exploring how professional background and familiarity with digital tools influence perception and interaction. System and Experience Analysis: exploring associations between perceptual dimensions within the questionnaire, interpreted as indicative trends rather than statistically significant interdependencies. The sample consisted of seven participants with an average age of 37.7 years (range: 29–53), reflecting a typical profile of professionals involved in administrative and technical evaluation processes within the AEC sector. Descriptive statistics related to participant characteristics are reported in Tables 47 and 48 and are provided to contextualize the findings rather than to support inferential analysis. With respect to familiarity with BIM and representation systems, most participants reported basic to intermediate levels of BIM experience (levels 1–3 on a five-point Likert scale), while experience with AR and VR technologies Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management was more evenly distributed. This heterogeneity reflects the non-standardized and project-specific adoption of immersive technologies in current professional practice. During the experiment, four participants accessed the Representative Ecosystem via desktop PCs and three via VR devices. This distribution was determined by technological availability and individual preferences and was not controlled experimentally. Consequently, observed differences in perception are interpreted as contextual observations rather than as evidence of device-related performance effects. Across both access modalities, the evaluated dimensions relate to the configured framework and its representational logic rather than to the technological interface itself.
