Several participants reported that, compared to traditional procedures based on two-dimensional documentation and isolated three-dimensional views, the structured representation supported a more continuous understanding of spatial relationships. Participants also noted that the availability of a coherent spatial representation at a one-to-one scale facilitated reflection on specific design and material choices. These observations are based on self-reported perceptions and do not constitute evidence of measurable performance improvement. Rather, they suggest that a structured experiential configuration may support reflective evaluation processes during administrative discussions. In this sense, the feedback points toward a potential shift in how spatial qualities are perceived and discussed within decision-making activities, without implying a paradigm change or a technological determinism. This interpretation is consistent with the view that architectural understanding engages multiple perceptual dimensions beyond visual inspection alone, as discussed in Balin et al. [7], and aligns with the use of extended representation systems as cognitive support tools rather than as immersive substitutes. Some participants reported a perceived reduction in the time required to understand the project. Given the qualitative nature of the data and the absence of objective temporal measurements, this result should be interpreted as an indicative tendency rather than as evidence of increased cognitive efficiency. Similar tendencies have been reported in the literature on visuospatial processing in architectural simulations (e.g., Heng et al. [141]). An additional aspect highlighted by participants concerns the possibility of recording experiential sessions, which was perceived as potentially beneficial for traceability and documentation within administrative procedures. In this context, traceable archiving of interactions was regarded as a supporting feature for transparency and accountability, rather than as a direct outcome of the experiential configuration. The sense of presence reported during the simulation should be interpreted as a self-reported cognitive–operational condition related to engagement, spatial clarity, and perceived control over exploration, rather than as sensory immersion. This perception reflects the configured structure of the Representative Ecosystem and its interaction logic, rather than the use of specific devices or immersive technologies (Table 51). Alongside perceived strengths, several operational and methodological limitations emerged. Initial difficulties in accessing and navigating the environment were reported, particularly by participants using advanced experiential devices. These observations are consistent with concerns raised in the literature regarding learning curves and onboarding requirements for such systems (Khan et al. [5]). The absence of a moderator—intentionally maintained to observe autonomous use—was reported to limit immediate understanding of available functions, suggesting that human mediation remains a relevant factor during early adoption phases of structured representation systems. A further limitation concerns the need to generate experiential representations that are not directly linked to the source models. Participants highlighted potential risks related to information loss, procedural misalignment, and temporal inconsistencies due to asynchronous updates between the authoritative model and the experiential environment. Difficulties in conveying information hierarchy and filtering content within the Representative Ecosystem were also reported. This issue reflects a broader challenge already recognized in BIM-based processes, namely Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management balancing information completeness with cognitive manageability. In addition, managing and formalizing observations generated during the simulation was perceived as procedurally demanding, particularly with respect to traceability and accountability requirements in administrative decision-making contexts. Among the proposals emerging from participant feedback, interest was expressed in integrating AI-based assistance to support information retrieval and interpretation, pointing toward hybrid human–AI interaction models consistent with current research trends (Pan et al. [142]). Participants also suggested closer integration between Representative Ecosystems and Common Data Environments, either by extending CDE functionalities or by aligning experiential environments with existing professional data structures (Table 52). At a technical level, suggestions concerned improvements in interaction fluidity and in the ability to associate observations with structured information layers. While these aspects indicate ongoing technological maturation, participants emphasized that the absence of clear regulatory and contractual frameworks remains a significant barrier to the formal adoption of structured representation systems in public and private procedures. Given the limited sample size (n = 7) and the reliance on self-reported perceptual measures, the analysis is exploratory in nature and does not aim to establish statistically significant correlations or causal relationships. Simple descriptive regression plots were used as interpretative tools to visualize tendencies within the dataset. The combined experience level is treated as a contextual variable to support the interpretation of variability in perceived presence, immersion, engagement, and physical and practical consequences. The results are therefore intended to highlight indicative patterns and to inform future hypotheses, rather than to provide confirmatory evidence. 6.6.3.1 Physical Consequences and Sustainability of Extended Use The scatterplot shown in Figure 53 suggests a downward tendency between combined experience level and perceived physical discomfort during the use of the Representative Ecosystem. Participants with lower levels of experience reported higher levels of physical discomfort, while more experienced users tended to report lower values. The range of reported scores indicates considerable variability, particularly among users with intermediate experience levels, suggesting the influence of individual adaptation processes. While more experienced participants appeared to tolerate prolonged interaction more comfortably, this tendency should not be interpreted as a direct effect of the framework. Device characteristics and familiarity with experiential environments likely play a relevant role in shaping physical comfort, and the framework alone cannot compensate for intrinsic technological constraints. From an applied perspective, these observations highlight the importance of considering team composition, onboarding strategies, and usage duration when planning collaborative sessions, especially in administrative contexts requiring extended participation. Similar considerations have been discussed in the literature, where structured planning and user preparation are identified as key factors in mitigating physical fatigue during extended use of representation systems (Bille et al. [10]). Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 53 Correlation Between Experience Level and Physical Consequences. 6.6.3.2 Practical Benefits and Operational Effectiveness The scatterplot in Figure 54 shows a near-horizontal distribution of perceived practical consequences across the entire range of experience levels. Reported scores are concentrated within a relatively narrow and high range, indicating limited variability between less experienced and more experienced participants. This pattern suggests that participants perceived comparable levels of practical usefulness from the structured representation, regardless of their prior technical expertise. Within this exploratory context, perceived operational benefits appear not to be strongly dependent on experience level. Rather than indicating intrinsic effectiveness of the framework, this tendency reflects that the configured Representative Ecosystem was accessible and usable across heterogeneous professional profiles. External factors, such as interface usability and integration with existing workflows, may have influenced these perceptions more than experiential aspects alone. Fig. 54 Correlation Between Experience Level and Pratical Consequences. 6.6.3.3  Presence and Cognitive Amplification Mechanisms Figure 55 illustrates an increasing tendency between combined experience level and self-reported presence scores. Participants with higher levels of experience tended to report higher presence values, while less experienced users still achieved moderate scores. In this study, presence is interpreted as a cognitive–operational condition related to perceived spatial clarity, engagement, and control over exploration, rather than as sensory immersion. The Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management observed tendency suggests that familiarity with representational environments may facilitate navigation and interpretation of spatial information, contributing to higher self-reported presence. However, experience alone does not fully account for the variability observed. Individual factors, such as cognitive predisposition, motivation, and contextual engagement, likely contribute to perceived presence. The relatively gradual slope of the trend further supports an interpretation based on progressive adaptation rather than on qualitative shifts attributable to technology or device type. Fig. 55 Correlation Between Experience Level and Presence. 6.6.3.4 Perception and Equalizing Effects The scatterplot presented in Figure 56 shows a negative tendency between combined experience level and perceived skill development. Participants with lower levels of experience reported higher perceived learning benefits, while participants with more consolidated expertise reported more limited gains. Reported scores range between 3.1 and 4.7, with a difference of slightly more than one point across the experience spectrum. Users with low experience levels (1–2) reported higher values, whereas participants with higher experience levels (above 4) tended to report lower scores. This pattern may reflect a saturation effect, whereby individuals with established skills perceive fewer incremental benefits from the interaction. These observations are based on self-reported perceptions and should be interpreted cautiously. Rather than indicating a reduction of skill gaps, the results suggest that structured experiential representations may provide supportive learning conditions for less experienced stakeholders, particularly during initial exposure to complex spatial and procedural information. In this sense, the findings are consistent with studies discussing the potential of experiential representation systems to facilitate participation and comprehension in collaborative settings (e.g., Teo et al. [143]), without implying that such systems inherently equalize competencies. In administrative contexts, this tendency may contribute to more inclusive participation by supporting understanding among stakeholders with heterogeneous levels of digital experience, while preserving existing expertise-based roles. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 56 Correlation Between Experience Level and Skill. 6.6.3.5 Experiential Dimensions and Qualitative Adaptation The Figures, 57, 58, 59 illustrate exploratory tendencies between experience level and the experiential dimensions of immersion, engagement, and emotion. The three dimensions exhibit different patterns of variability, which should be interpreted as indicative rather than as evidence of systematic effects. Immersion displays a wider range of reported scores, increasing from lower values among less experienced participants to higher values among more experienced users. Engagement follows a similar but more gradual tendency, while emotional responses remain relatively stable within a consistently high range, showing limited sensitivity to experience level. These patterns suggest differentiated sensitivities among experiential dimensions. Familiarity with representational environments may support more effective cognitive involvement and sustained attention, contributing to higher self-reported immersion and engagement. Emotional responses, by contrast, appear less dependent on prior experience and may be influenced by more general factors related to interest, motivation, or contextual relevance. Variability observed in the intermediate experience range, including lower immersion scores for some participants, indicates the influence of individual factors such as cognitive adaptation, interaction preferences, or access modality. Given the exploratory nature of the dataset, these deviations should not be interpreted as anomalies but as signals of contextual complexity. The presence of participants with higher experience levels may have contributed to shared engagement during collaborative sessions; however, this observation remains interpretative and cannot be isolated from group dynamics or procedural conditions. Overall, the stability of emotional responses suggests a baseline level of positive engagement across participants, while immersion and engagement appear more sensitive to individual familiarity with structured representation environments. From an applied perspective, these tendencies point toward the potential usefulness of calibrating representational complexity and interaction density to the expected profile of participants. Such considerations, however, remain hypothetical at this stage and would require validation through larger samples and controlled experimental designs. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 57 Correlation Between Experience Level and Immersion. Fig. 58 Correlation Between Experience Level and Engagement. Fig. 59 Correlation Between Experience Level and Emotion. 6.6.4  Analysis of Professional Profiles The multiple bar chart shown in Figure 60 illustrates how self-reported experiential dimensions varied across participants with different professional profiles. Given the limited sample size and the uneven distribution of roles, the analysis does not aim to compare performance or effectiveness between profiles, but rather to explore how professional background may influence the way the structured representation was perceived and used. The results indicate differentiated perceptual patterns rather than hierarchical outcomes. Profiles characterized by a stronger familiarity with digital coordination and information management processes, such as BIM Managers and Researchers, reported higher self-assessed levels of presence and emotional engagement, together with lower Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management perceived physical consequences. These responses may reflect greater familiarity with abstract representations and complex information environments, rather than superior outcomes attributable to the framework itself. More heterogeneous patterns were observed among Project Engineers and Architects. Project Engineers reported higher perceived practical benefits but lower immersion scores, suggesting a pragmatic orientation toward task- related aspects of the simulation. Architects, by contrast, reported higher engagement and emotional involvement, accompanied by higher levels of perceived physical discomfort, which may be associated with more prolonged or intense interaction during exploration. Engineering and Financial Resources profiles reported intermediate and relatively balanced scores across most dimensions. These patterns are consistent with roles that typically engage with representational systems in a more selective or analytical manner, focusing on specific aspects relevant to their professional responsibilities rather than on continuous spatial interaction. Variations in perceived physical consequences across profiles highlight considerations relevant for operational deployment. While some profiles reported moderate levels of discomfort, others reported higher values, suggesting that expectations, interaction styles, and usage duration may influence ergonomic perceptions. These observations point to the importance of considering professional diversity when planning collaborative experiential sessions, particularly in administrative contexts involving extended participation. Across all profiles, reported scores generally remained above the midpoint of the scale for most experiential dimensions. This indicates that the structured representation was perceived as accessible and usable across heterogeneous professional backgrounds, without implying uniform effects or inherent effectiveness. The observed differences should therefore be interpreted as context-dependent perceptions rather than as evidence of differential performance or superiority linked to professional role. Given the exploratory nature of the study, the limited sample size, and the absence of controlled experimental conditions, the analysis does not aim to compare performance or effectiveness between technologies. Instead, it explores how different access modalities were perceived by participants when interacting with the same structured Representative Ecosystem. Participants accessing the environment through VR devices reported higher values for presence, immersion, emotional involvement, and engagement. These perceptions are consistent with the characteristics of immersive interfaces, which emphasize embodied interaction and spatial continuity. However, such responses should be interpreted as self-reported experiential tendencies rather than as indicators of superior cognitive or decisional outcomes. Participants using desktop PC interfaces reported lower values in perceptual dimensions, but lower perceived physical consequences and higher perceived skill development. This pattern suggests that desktop-based interaction may support more sustainable and analytically oriented engagement, particularly during extended sessions or repeated access. These observations do not imply enhanced cognitive performance, but rather reflect differences in how participants experience and reflect upon the interaction. Perceived practical consequences were reported at comparable levels across both access modalities, indicating that participants considered the structured representation useful for administrative evaluation tasks regardless of the device used. This result supports the interpretation that perceived operational usefulness is primarily related to the configuration of the framework rather than to the specific technological interface. Differences observed between access modalities should therefore be understood as complementary experiential tendencies rather than as indicators of technological superiority. VR devices appear to emphasize perceptual and emotional engagement, while desktop interfaces appear to support sustained interaction and reflective use. These tendencies are shaped by interaction duration, physical comfort, and user expectations, rather than by inherent properties of the framework. From an applied perspective, these observations suggest that combining different access modalities within the same procedural framework may support diverse participation needs. VR-based access may be suitable for exploratory spatial discussions and shared understanding, while desktop access may be more appropriate for extended analytical tasks and integration into routine administrative workflows. Such considerations remain exploratory and would require validation through larger and more controlled studies. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 61 Average of Presence, Emotion, Immersion, Engagement, Skill, Practical and Physical Consequences by Device Type. 6.6.6 Correlation Matrix Between Dimensions of the Experience Desktop PC e VR The correlation matrix presented in Figure 62 explores associations between experiential dimensions across the combined dataset, including both desktop PC and VR access modalities. Given the exploratory nature of the study and the limited sample size, these associations should be interpreted as indicative patterns rather than as statistically significant relationships. Within the combined dataset, relatively high associations were observed between presence, engagement, and immersion, with correlation coefficients equal to or above 0.72. These values suggest that, when the Representative Ecosystem is perceived as spatially and informationally coherent, participants tend to report concurrent increases across these perceptual dimensions. This pattern reflects self-reported experiential coherence rather than a reinforcing causal mechanism. A negative association emerged between the Ability dimension and the perceptual dimensions, with coefficients ranging from −0.70 to −0.98. This tendency suggests that environments perceived as more perceptually engaging may coincide with a reduced sense of operational control or technical mastery. In the context of administrative procedures, this observation highlights a potential tension between perceptual richness and perceived manageability of information. 4,56 3,71 4,37 3,86 4,67 2,75 4,42 3,5 3,3 4,6 4,35 4,25 2,61 0,38 0 1 2 3 4 5 Headset VR PC Desktop Headset VR PC Desktop Average of Physical Consequences 2,61 0,38 Average of Practical Consequences 4,35 4,25 Average of Skill 3,3 4,6 Average of Engagement 4,42 3,5 Average of Immersion 4,67 2,75 Average of Emotion 4,37 3,86 Average of Presence 4,56 3,71 Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Practical Consequences exhibited weaker and more variable associations (ranging from −0.40 to +0.51), indicating that perceptual coherence alone does not directly translate into perceived operational benefits. Such variability is likely influenced by external factors, including interface ergonomics, prior experience, and task specificity, which were not isolated within the experimental design. Overall, the combined analysis suggests that structured experiential configurations are perceived as supporting perceptual coherence, while also requiring careful calibration to preserve users’ sense of agency and control. These observations support a differentiated interpretation of access modalities rather than a uniform assessment of effectiveness. Desktop PC The correlation matrix computed for the desktop-only subset (Figure 63) highlights a specific pattern of associations characteristic of non-immersive access modalities. Presence shows a strong association with engagement (r = 0.91), suggesting that, even in the absence of immersive devices, a well-structured representation can support sustained cognitive involvement. In contrast, presence exhibits negative associations with immersion and emotion (ranging from −0.87 to −0.99), indicating that desktop-based interaction, while supporting focused engagement, does not elicit strong sensory or affective responses. This pattern is consistent with an interaction mode that prioritizes analytical attention over perceptual transport. Associations involving the Ability dimension range from −0.57 to +0.58, suggesting no stable directional relationship. Rather than indicating a leveling effect, these values reflect heterogeneous perceptions of operational control across users with different backgrounds. Physical Consequences display strong negative associations (from −0.97 to −0.75), indicating that interaction through familiar desktop tools was perceived as physically sustainable and less demanding. Overall, the desktop configuration appears to support perceptual coherence and prolonged engagement, while remaining limited in its capacity to activate multisensory experience. VR Headset The correlation matrix derived from the VR-only subset (Figure 64) should be interpreted with particular caution due to the very limited number of observations (n = 3). In this subset, presence shows a strong positive association with physical consequences (r = 1.00), suggesting that higher perceived presence may coincide with increased physical or cognitive strain. This observation aligns with existing literature highlighting the potential for fatigue or discomfort in highly immersive environments if exposure is not carefully managed (e.g., Schneider and Kammler [59]). Engagement exhibits weak or inconsistent associations with other dimensions, a pattern that likely reflects sample limitations and contextual variability rather than meaningful experiential dynamics. Immersion shows a broader range of associations (from −0.87 to +0.76), indicating variability in how perceptual richness, emotional response, and involvement were experienced across participants. ositive associations between immersion, emotion, and practical consequences (0.76 and 0.98) suggest that participants perceived immersive configurations as potentially supportive of collaborative understanding. However, these values should not be interpreted as evidence of improved decision-making outcomes, but rather as indicative of perceived experiential alignment. As observed in the combined dataset, the Ability dimension shows a negative association with immersion (−0.87), reinforcing the observation that higher perceptual engagement may be accompanied by a reduced sense of technical control. This Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management tendency highlights the importance of interface design, training, and gradual exposure when immersive devices are employed in formal decision-making contexts. Fig. 62 Correlation Matrix Result with Both Tools. Fig. 63 Correlation Matrix result with PC data only. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 64 Correlation Matrix result with VR Headset data only. 6.6.7 Iterative refinements of the framework derived from the case studies The two case studies did not lead to a redefinition of the conceptual foundations of the Framework. Instead, they contributed to a set of targeted refinements concerning its structure, scope of application, and operational articulation. In line with the Design Science Research methodology, the framework evolved through iterative empirical testing, with adjustments informed by observations emerging from both simulated and real-world contexts. First, the framework was refined to explicitly support a modular and selective application logic. The first case study (Borgo Pirelli) adopted a sequential configuration in which different representational tools were applied independently across separate simulation phases. While this approach enabled controlled observation of individual configurations, it limited the possibility of examining combined or overlapping effects within a single decision-making process. Based on this limitation, the framework was refined to account for hybrid and combined configurations, as explored in the second case study (Milano MIND – Cascina Merlata). This refinement clarified that not all components of the framework need to be activated simultaneously and that selective activation may be more appropriate depending on procedural objectives, institutional context, and stakeholder roles. The framework therefore now explicitly supports modular application rather than prescriptive completeness. Second, the framework was extended through the introduction of an applicative layer addressing organizational and procedural constraints. While the initial formulation of the framework was intentionally independent of specific institutional settings, its application within a real administrative procedure revealed operational, coordination, and contractual requirements that were not fully represented in the simulated case study. This led to the integration of a formalized coding structure, enabling experiential and informational configurations to be explicitly aligned with procedural requirements and stakeholder competencies. This refinement strengthened the distinction between the framework as a conceptual construct and its empirical implementation, supporting its Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management applicability in regulated administrative environments without conflating methodological principles with technological solutions. Third, the framework was refined through the calibration of its evaluation instruments. The first case study highlighted limitations in the initial questionnaire used to investigate experiential and decision-support dimensions, particularly with respect to alignment between theoretical constructs and empirical indicators. As a result, the data collection instrument was revised and adapted in the second case study to improve coherence with the framework’s dimensions and to better reflect the exploratory nature of the investigation. This refinement did not aim to increase statistical rigor, but to enhance interpretative consistency between the framework’s theoretical assumptions and the self-reported perceptions collected during empirical application. Overall, these refinements reflect an incremental and context-sensitive evolution of the framework. Rather than demonstrating effectiveness, the case studies contributed to clarifying conditions of applicability, configurability, and evaluation, providing a more robust methodological basis for future investigations and practical deployments. Response to RQ2 & RQ3: The qualitative feedback suggests that the framework can support collaborative discussion and shared understanding; however, professional adoption appears to be influenced by usability conditions and by the current lack of formal regulatory references, which represents a systemic barrier rather than a technological limitation. Exploratory Associations between Experience Level and Perceptual Dimensions Main Findings: Self-reported perceptions indicate that prior experience is associated with lower perceived physical consequences and lower perceived skill development (indicative saturation effect), while higher experience levels are associated with higher reported presence and immersion. Perceived practical usefulness remains relatively stable across experience levels. Response to RQ2 & RQ3: These exploratory tendencies suggest that the framework is perceived as usable across heterogeneous experience levels, while perceptual intensity varies depending on familiarity with representational environments. Perceived training value among less experienced users may contribute to acceptance, without implying equalization or effectiveness. Profiles with greater familiarity with digital coordination and information management tended to report higher perceived presence and lower physical consequences, while other profiles showed more heterogeneous responses across dimensions. Response to RQ3: Acceptance factors appear to vary across professional roles. The findings suggest that structured experiential configurations may need to be adapted to disciplinary backgrounds and institutional responsibilities, rather than assuming uniform benefits. Comparative Analysis by Device Type (PC vs. Advanced Experiential Devices) Main Findings: Participants reported different experiential tendencies depending on access modality. Perceived practical usefulness was reported at comparable levels across modalities. Response to RQ1 & RQ2: These observations suggest that experiential requirements may need to be differentiated to support hybrid strategies. Differences should be interpreted as complementary perceptions rather than as indicators of technological superiority or measurable impact. Correlation Matrix between Experiential Dimensions Main Findings: Exploratory associations indicate that presence and immersion tend to co-vary, while both may be inversely associated with perceived ability or control. These patterns vary across access modalities and should be interpreted cautiously given the limited sample size. Response to RQ1 & RQ2: The findings suggest that information structuring and experiential configuration should consider potential tensions between perceptual engagement and perceived control. Collaborative quality appears to depend on balancing experiential dimensions rather than maximizing individual variables. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 7 DISCUSSION Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 7.1 Findings and Research Questions The previous chapters laid the empirical foundation for this research by presenting the case studies of Borgo Pirelli and Milano MIND and analyzing the data collected through structured experimental protocols. Based on these findings, this chapter provides a critical discussion of the results (see Figure 65), with the explicit aim of interpreting the empirical evidence generated in the context of administrative procedures, rather than introducing new data. The discussion pursues a twofold objective. First, it interprets the findings in relation to the objectives and research questions defined at the outset of the study. Second, it situates these findings within the broader scientific debate on information management, representation, and collaborative decision-making, highlighting points of convergence and divergence with the existing literature. The investigation is guided by the following overarching research question: What are the effects of a structured configuration on the integration of representation systems in collaborative and decision-making processes, particularly in administrative procedures? To systematically address this question, a structured discussion framework was developed. Based on a synthesis of the literature and empirical evidence from the two case studies, the framework identifies the key factors influencing collaborative decision-making within administrative procedures. Following the approach proposed by Kim et al. [59], the discussion is organized through a multilevel structure composed of phases, areas, criteria, and evaluation metrics (see Figure 67). The framework divides the discussion into three main phases, each directly linked to a research question: Commissioning Phase (RQ1): Considering the methodological and informational structure of Information Management, how can information requirements and prerequisites be systematically organized to enable effective Representative Ecosystems in collaborative and administrative decision- making processes? Use Phase (RQ2): What effects does the application of a structured framework have on collaboration dynamics, decision-making effectiveness, and stakeholder experience during the operational use of representation systems? Post-use phase (RQ3): What factors influence professional acceptance, perceived usefulness, and potential institutional adoption of structured representation frameworks, beyond individual case implementations? This phased structure supports a progressive interpretation of the findings, moving from framework configuration to operational use and, finally, to broader considerations related to professional adoption and long-term applicability. Although the discussion primarily relates to the administrative procedures examined in the case studies, references to broader implications for the field are explicitly treated as interpretative and prospective, rather than as direct empirical generalizations. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Fig. 65 framework for data discussion. 7.1.1 Commissioning Phase (RQ1): Structuring experiential dimensions through requirements To address RQ1, the commissioning phase is analysed by focusing on how experiential dimensions can be systematically enabled through the definition of prerequisites and requirements. The discussion is structured around two complementary evaluation perspectives derived from the framework: (i) technological compatibility and (ii) the suitability of the data preparation process. The analysis draws on empirical evidence collected during the ecosystem configuration phases of the Borgo Pirelli and Milano MIND case studies, both conducted within administrative procedure contexts. 7.1.1.1 The Compatibility Challenge The definition of prerequisites and requirements for effective experiential representation begins with the identification of the entities involved—users, digital objects, and technological systems—and the consequent structuring of a technological infrastructure consistent with operational and procedural needs. Within the proposed Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management framework, compatibility does not refer to the isolated performance of individual components, but to their ability to operate as part of an integrated ecosystem that supports collaboration, interpretation, and decision-making. In both case studies, a heterogeneous technological ecosystem was deliberately adopted to test the robustness of the framework under different experiential configurations. The infrastructure included: Hardware, ranging from standalone immersive devices (e.g. Meta Quest 3) to collective environments such as the Virtual Theater of the Labora Laboratory (CAVE system), as well as holographic tables and desktop-based setups. This diversity enabled the assessment of compatibility across varying levels of immersion and interaction. Software, starting from BIM authoring environments (Autodesk Revit) and extending to experiential representation engines such as Unreal Engine (Collab Viewer Template), Twinmotion, and cloud-based collaborative platforms (e.g. Spatial.io). Empirical evidence from the case studies indicates that the primary criticalities did not stem from the selection of individual technologies, but from the management of their interconnections. The transition of information from BIM authoring tools to experiential environments emerged as the most fragile phase of the process, frequently requiring manual interventions and non-standardised procedures. Although exchange tools such as Datasmith and emerging formats such as USD partially mitigated these issues, they did not fully resolve them. In several instances, virtual counterparts were generated without a direct and persistent link to the original BIM models, increasing the risk of information loss and procedural inconsistencies. This condition reflects what has been described in the literature as the translation gap [112,144], namely the discrepancy between the structured rigor of BIM models and the flexibility required by Representative Ecosystems. Within administrative procedures, where traceability, accountability, and information consistency are essential, such gaps represent a critical limitation to scalability and institutional adoption. A further compatibility-related issue concerned overall experiential performance, which proved to be strongly influenced by the balance between hardware capabilities and software complexity. For example, maintaining stable frame rates in immersive environments required extensive model optimisation, sometimes at the expense of visual detail. Conversely, limitations in collective display systems, such as projector resolution in the virtual theater, affected users’ perceptual clarity. These findings confirm that technological infrastructure constitutes a structural prerequisite for experiential quality [52], rather than a neutral operational layer. From a commissioning perspective, the results highlight that compatibility must be addressed as a design requirement of the framework itself. Inadequate alignment between technological systems, data structures, and procedural needs can generate inefficiencies, increase implementation costs, and undermine the effectiveness of experiential solutions. Conversely, explicitly defining compatibility-related prerequisites at the commissioning stage represents a key condition for the reliable integration of experiential representation within administrative decision-making processes. 7.1.1.2 The Challenge of Convenience Empirical evidence from both case studies confirms that simply exporting an information model to a Representative Ecosystem is neither sufficient nor effective. To support understanding and decision-making, Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management models must be curated and adapted to become not only visualizable, but also interpretable and interactive, in accordance with representation criteria explicitly defined in relation to users, objectives, and the administrative context of application. In the proposed framework, the affordability metric is interpreted as the efficiency, sustainability, and feasibility of the preparation process required to configure the Ecosystem. From this perspective, ecosystem preparation emerged as a multi-stage operational process, comprising the following main activities: Geometric optimization. The information models, typically rich in construction-level detail, proved unsuitable for real-time rendering without prior simplification. In the case of Milano MIND, this required dedicated operations in external environments (e.g., Blender), including polygon reduction, mesh correction, and UV mapping. While essential to ensuring performance, these activities require significant resources, specialized skills, and additional costs, limiting their applicability in standard administrative procedures. Semantic filter. Not all information contained in BIM models is relevant to the decision-making process. In the case of Milano MIND, for example, the client explicitly requested a simulation focused on the perception of the façade and its urban impact, intentionally excluding alphanumeric attributes and technical documentation. This highlights that model preparation is not a neutral operation, but a selective process driven by procedural objectives. Information enrichment. In several cases, additional representational elements, such as textures, visual cues, arrows, hotspots, and two-dimensional references, were introduced to improve orientation and readability. This confirms that preparation involves not only simplification, but also an active redesign of information aimed at supporting experiential understanding. Device adaptation. Preparation strategies varied significantly depending on the target devices (desktop systems, immersive headsets, holographic tables). PC-based models required further simplification for standalone immersive devices with limited computational capacity. Consequently, preparation became a calibration process balancing detail, interactivity, and hardware constraints. Assessing the feasibility of this preparation process proved to be a critical commissioning activity, rather than a purely technical phase. To support large-scale adoption, experiential solutions must remain manageable in contexts characterized by limited financial, human, and time resources [52,145]. In both case studies, model preparation required significant investments in time and hybrid skills, combining information management expertise with representation engine development. This reliance on highly specialized profiles constitutes a significant obstacle for public administrations, where procurement constraints and organizational rigidity often limit access to such expertise. As noted by Abbasnejad [2], the value of BIM-based representation systems remains largely potential in the absence of adequate human capital. Another limitation concerns scalability. The observed workflows remain largely artisanal, based on non- standardized procedures that reduce replicability and complicate model updates. The lack of shared protocols for optimization and data exchange limits efficiency and hinders the institutionalization of advanced representation in administrative processes [2]. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Qualitative feedback collected during the case studies also revealed operational and cultural challenges, including initial learning curves, the need for ongoing support, and the perception of rapid skill obsolescence. These aspects, although not directly quantifiable, indicate that technological innovation must be accompanied by structured training and digital literacy strategies to mitigate resistance to adoption. 7.1.1.3 Implications for RQ1 The combined analysis of the two key commissioning metrics compatibility and convenience reveals a scenario that is more complex than a mere aggregation of technical requirements. Rather than producing a fixed checklist of prerequisites, the findings indicate the need to rethink how experiential representation is commissioned and structured within construction-related administrative processes. From a compatibility perspective, the case studies highlight a structural paradox. As the technological ecosystem expands ranging from immersive devices such as Meta Quest 3 to collective CAVE environments, and from real- time engines like Unreal Engine to collaborative platforms such as Spatial.io the criticality of integration increases accordingly. The translation gap observed between BIM models and Representative Ecosystems is therefore not only technical in nature, but also symptomatic of the sector’s limited systemic maturity. Empirical evidence confirms that data transfer from BIM authoring tools to experiential environments remains dominated by manual and inefficient procedures, corroborating existing findings on the tension between the structured rigidity of BIM and the fluidity required by experiential systems [2,144]. This fragmentation raises a methodological concern regarding the coherence between the sector’s industrialisation objectives and the persistence of artisanal workflows lacking shared and standardised structures. The frequent creation of virtual counterparts disconnected from the original BIM models represents a further contradiction. While BIM is intended to ensure information centrality and updateability, the proliferation of derivative models undermines these principles, particularly in administrative procedures where traceability and information consistency are critical. This unresolved tension between information fidelity and experiential performance remains one of the main limitations identified in current research and practice [144]. Beyond technological integration, the findings emphasise the role of organisational and human factors. The case studies show that aligning experiential requirements with expected outcomes requires specific interdisciplinary skills and a minimum level of digital culture among operators. As reported in the literature, a significant share of BIM–XR project failures can be attributed to inadequate workflow management rather than to technological limitations [146]. Consequently, the response to RQ1 cannot be framed solely in technological terms, but must integrate organisational, procedural, and training-related dimensions. The convenience analysis further deepens this perspective. Activities such as geometric optimisation, semantic filtering, and information enrichment demonstrate that model preparation is not a secondary task, but a form of information redesign tailored to a different representational medium. In the Milano MIND case, the deliberate exclusion of technical data in favour of spatial understanding illustrates that experiential content must be explicitly aligned with procedural objectives, particularly in decision-making contexts such as Service Conferences. However, this necessary customisation introduces a tension with the goal of process standardisation, raising questions about how tailored solutions can coexist with replicable protocols. The need for hybrid professional profiles, capable of operating across design, information management, and experiential representation domains [147], further challenges the accessibility of these solutions. A paradox Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management emerges: the actors who could benefit most from the democratization of information enabled by experiential systems are often those least able to sustain the economic and organisational costs of adoption. Qualitative feedback collected during the case studies also points to learning curves, dependence on expert mediation, and perceptions of rapid skill obsolescence. While these aspects fall beyond direct quantitative validation, they provide relevant interpretative insights into adoption dynamics. Taken together, these observations suggest that the prerequisites for effective experiential commissioning cannot be conceived as static or universal. Instead, they should be articulated as components of an adaptive framework capable of balancing efficiency, flexibility, and experiential quality. In response to RQ1, the findings indicate that prerequisites need to be structured across three complementary levels: Systemic level, defining compatibility protocols to ensure interoperability between BIM environments, representation engines, and hardware, thereby reducing artisanal fragmentation; Processual level, establishing standardised workflows for recurring operations (e.g. optimisation, data filtering) combined with modular components that allow contextual customisation; Contractual level, integrating experiential requirements into EIR and procurement-related documents, with explicit definitions of responsibilities, data formats, and validation procedures. Within this perspective, the most significant result is that commissioning prerequisites should be understood as elements of a dynamic system that evolves in relation to objectives, users, and decision-making contexts. The empirical evidence from the case studies does not point toward a single optimal solution, but rather toward the identification of recurring patterns a form of pattern language for experiential representation in construction processes that can provide methodological consistency without constraining contextual adaptability 7.1.2 Usage Phase (RQ2): Areas of the Representative Ecosystem This This section focuses on the analysis and discussion of the empirical results related to the Usage phase of the proposed framework. Its objective is to address the second research question (RQ2): What measurable impacts does a structured framework have on the effectiveness of decision-making processes, collaboration, and the overall stakeholder experience? The discussion builds on the quantitative and qualitative evidence presented in the previous chapters, including questionnaire data, comparative analyses between structured and unstructured conditions, and observations collected during the case study implementations. No new data are introduced at this stage; rather, the section interprets the observed effects in relation to the framework’s underlying principles and to the specific context of administrative and collaborative decision-making. Given the multidimensional nature of the collected evidence, the analysis is organised around three conceptual areas that, according to the framework, characterise the Usage phase of a Representative Ecosystem. This structure enables a systematic examination of how different aspects of use are influenced by the introduction of structured experiential configurations. Each area is discussed in a dedicated subsection, where empirical results are interpreted in relation to RQ2 and to relevant literature. The three areas are defined as follows: Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Experiential dimensions. This area examines how the framework influences users’ subjective experience of the project, with particular attention to clarity, shared understanding, and goal orientation. The discussion interprets perceptual and experiential indicators to assess how structured representation affects the way information is understood and navigated within administrative procedures. Collaboration. The second area addresses the impact of the framework on group dynamics and collaborative practices. It analyses how communication fluidity, interaction quality, and co-creation processes among heterogeneous stakeholders are affected during the use of the Representative Ecosystem, relating empirical observations to the framework’s theoretical assumptions. Decision-making. The third area focuses on the effects of structured experiential representation on decision-making processes. Indicators related to awareness, confidence, and efficiency are discussed to evaluate how increased informational clarity and experiential alignment support collective evaluation and deliberation in the operational context of the case studies. Through this structured interpretation, the section aims to identify the main effects generated during the Usage phase and to clarify the contribution of the framework to collaborative decision-making within administrative procedures. Broader reflections on longer-term effects, adoption dynamics, and institutional implications are deliberately deferred to the next chapter, which addresses the Post-Usage phase (RQ3). 7.1.2.1 Discussion of Experiential Dimensions The The first area of discussion, Experiential Dimensions, examines the quality of the subjective experience reported by participants during interaction with the framework in the Usage phase. This perspective is central to addressing RQ2, as experiential perception directly influences engagement, collaboration, and the effectiveness of collective decision-making. The discussion builds on empirical evidence collected through questionnaires, correlation analyses, and comparative evaluations across different representational configurations. Rather than focusing on the functional performance of individual tools, this section adopts a human-centred perspective, analysing how structured experiential representation affects users’ understanding, orientation, and interaction within administrative decision-making contexts [140]. In line with existing research, advanced representation systems can be interpreted as shifting interaction from predominantly symbolic mediation toward perceptual and experiential engagement [125]. In traditional practices, spatial understanding relies on the interpretation of abstract symbols and graphic conventions, which require cognitive translation into real-world conditions. In Representative Ecosystems, spatial properties are instead perceived in relation to the user’s embodied experience, supporting more immediate contextual understanding. It is important to note that experiential representation does not replace the quantitative evaluations required for regulatory compliance. Instead, it complements them by supporting preliminary interpretation, alignment of perspectives, and identification of potential criticalities prior to formal verification. From this perspective, the cognitive flow traditionally described as Data → Information is extended to include an experiential layer (Data → Information → Experience), which supports interpretation without substituting formal validation procedures. Within the proposed framework, this experiential layer here referred to as the Experiential Dimension (XD) is not introduced as a new geometric dimension, but as an interpretative construct describing the interaction between users, information, and representational environments. XD therefore captures how spatial, perceptual, and Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management collaborative factors contribute to understanding during decision-making processes. The framework operationalises this construct through six evaluation criteria Presence, Engagement, Emotion, Skills, Usability, and Immersion supported by two cross-cutting metrics, Capability and Convenience. These categories enable a structured interpretation of empirical results across different technologies and usage conditions. Presence Presence, defined as the subjective sensation of “being there” within a mediated environment [148], emerged as a foundational experiential criterion. Empirical analysis indicates that Presence is strongly influenced by both Convenience and Capability, which act as enabling conditions rather than independent variables. In line with the literature, technical or ergonomic disruptions were found to undermine perceptual continuity, reducing the sense of Presence and reintroducing awareness of technological mediation [148]. The data show that higher levels of procedural fluidity (Convenience) supported the maintenance of Presence, while increased Presence, in turn, reinforced users’ perceived Capability to orient themselves and interact effectively. The introduction of the framework strengthened this relationship, as reflected by the increase in correlation between Presence and overall experience evaluation (from r = 0.83 to r = 0.96). Advanced experiential devices generated higher levels of Presence (+24%) compared to desktop interaction, but also introduced ergonomic trade-offs, evidenced by correlations between Presence and reported physical discomfort [149]. These results highlight the need to balance perceptual intensity with long-term cognitive and physical sustainability. Engagement Engagement was analysed as an indicator of active participation and sustained involvement in the collaborative process. The framework contributed to higher engagement levels primarily by improving procedural clarity and reducing technological friction. Empirical evidence indicates reduced execution times and a lower need for technical mediation, enabling users to focus on decision-relevant tasks. Capability played a complementary role by strengthening users’ confidence in their ability to contribute meaningfully, thereby reducing asymmetries between expert and non-expert participants. Comparative analysis shows that immersive devices generated higher engagement peaks (+26%), particularly during spatial evaluation phases, while desktop interfaces supported more stable and distributed engagement across professional roles. These findings suggest that engagement is not a uniform outcome, but a configurable dimension that can be modulated according to context and objectives. Immersion Immersion was analysed as the continuity and depth of experiential involvement. Empirical results confirm that advanced experiential devices provide significantly higher immersion levels than desktop interfaces (4.7/5 versus 2.8/5, +68%), supporting findings in the literature [149]. However, correlation analyses also reveal a potential trade-off between immersion intensity and perceived control, indicating that excessive perceptual involvement may reduce users’ sense of agency. This suggests that immersion should be considered a design variable rather than an absolute objective. Emotion Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management The Emotion criterion explored affective responses associated with interaction. Results indicate that reduced procedural friction (Convenience) mitigated negative emotions such as frustration, while increased Capability supported positive affective states, including confidence and satisfaction. Notably, emotional responses were relatively independent of participants’ prior experience, suggesting that the framework provides a shared emotional baseline across heterogeneous stakeholders. VR-based configurations generated higher emotional resonance (+13%), particularly during spatial decision-making tasks, consistent with existing studies on experiential amplification [125]. Skills The Skills criterion addressed the relationship between users’ pre-existing competencies and perceived ability to contribute. The framework did not aim to introduce new skills, but to enable the effective application of existing ones. Empirical evidence indicates a reduction in interaction asymmetries, supporting more inclusive participation. A comparative “learning paradox” emerged: desktop users reported higher perceived competence than VR users, likely due to familiarity with traditional interfaces. This highlights the importance of hybrid configurations that balance experiential intensity with reflexive engagement. Usability Usability was analysed as a transversal condition influencing all experiential dimensions. Structured simulations showed significantly higher usability than unstructured ones, including a 50% reduction in support requests. Desktop interfaces ensured higher baseline usability due to familiarity, while VR systems exhibited greater variability linked to learning curves. Importantly, the framework’s contribution operates at the level of information structure and process logic rather than device selection, enabling hybrid configurations that combine different technologies while maintaining consistency in decision-making processes. 7.1.2.2 Discussion of the Collaboration Area While experiential dimensions describe the individual perceptual layer of interaction, the Collaboration area represents their progression into collective action. This section addresses one of the core objectives of experiential representation systems in the AEC sector: supporting collaborative decision-making in complex, multi- stakeholder contexts [114]. The analysis focuses not on the mere co-presence of participants in a shared digital space, but on how structured interaction enables coordinated communication, mutual understanding, and collective evaluation. Within the proposed framework, collaboration is interpreted as a governed process rather than a spontaneous outcome. The framework does not simply provide a shared environment, but structures how actors interact within it, aiming to reduce ambiguity and support coordinated action. To assess its impact systematically, the discussion is organised around three criteria that characterise collaborative use in the Usage phase: Multiple Users, Real-time Communication, and Real-time Modification. Each criterion is interpreted through the cross-cutting metrics of Capability and Convenience. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Multiple Users The effectiveness of multi-user environments was not assessed solely in terms of technical capacity, but in relation to the quality of interaction they enabled. Consistent with Whyte [24], empirical evidence confirms that virtual co-presence supports collaboration only when accompanied by an appropriate interaction structure. From a Capability perspective, the technological ecosystem adopted in the case studies (e.g. Unreal Engine Collab Viewer, Spatial.io) enabled simultaneous participation across heterogeneous devices. However, the framework’s primary contribution lies in transforming co-presence into effective collaboration by reducing interpretative asymmetries among participants with different professional backgrounds. Data indicate that less experienced stakeholders benefited disproportionately from the structured approach, confirming an equalising effect that is particularly relevant in administrative procedures, where inclusive participation contributes to procedural legitimacy [150]. The entity-based structure of the framework (user, model, environment) proved effective in managing the complexity of multi-user interaction and supporting interdisciplinary collaboration. Moreover, hybrid configurations allowing participation through both desktop interfaces and immersive devices emerged as a strategic asset, enabling participants to engage through tools aligned with their skills and comfort levels. From a Convenience perspective, the results highlight that multi-user configurations are effective only when they are both necessary and accessible. Desktop interfaces ensured high baseline accessibility, while immersive devices introduced steeper learning curves and greater variability in usability. Qualitative feedback consistently pointed to the continued need for human mediation during technological interaction, indicating that fully autonomous adoption remains limited under current conditions. Real-time Communication The Real-time Communication criterion evaluates how effectively the framework supports dialogue and information exchange among participants. In line with existing literature, results indicate that communication quality depends not only on technical features, but on the overall structure of the collaborative environment [149]. The framework supports what can be described as augmented communication, in which dialogue is grounded in a shared perceptual reference. From a Capability perspective, empirical evidence shows a reduction in misunderstandings and interpretative errors, as participants simultaneously perceived and referenced the same spatial information. This shared experiential context supported faster alignment and more informed consensus-building. From a Convenience perspective, the structured approach produced measurable operational benefits, including a 30.43% reduction in average simulation times and a 50% reduction in requests for technical assistance. These results indicate that clearly defined objectives and interaction guidelines reduce redundancy and cognitive load, enabling more efficient communication [149]. At the same time, the analysis reveals a methodological limitation often described as a user experience paradox. While structure improves efficiency, excessive rigidity can constrain conversational flexibility when unanticipated needs emerge during collaborative sessions [151]. This tension highlights the need to balance procedural guidance with adaptability, particularly in complex decision-making contexts. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Real-time Modification The Real-time Modification criterion represents the most advanced—and currently most constrained—dimension of experiential collaboration. Although participants consistently expressed expectations for direct and immediate interaction with the model, empirical evidence shows that this capability remains limited in practice. From a Capability perspective, the primary constraint lies in the disconnection between the source BIM model and the virtual representation used for experiential interaction. Truly bidirectional workflows remain rare, and available solutions are still experimental. In the case studies, maintaining operational stability required compromises, such as limiting the scope of modifications or reducing graphic complexity. These constraints reflect a persistent translation gap between the semantic rigor of BIM and the interactive fluidity expected in experiential environments [109]. As modifications performed in the virtual space are not automatically reflected in the authoritative information model, issues of traceability and procedural consistency arise [109]. From a Convenience perspective, users frequently reported the emergence of new requirements during interaction, such as the desire to compare alternatives or access regulatory information in real time. The inability to accommodate these needs reinforced perceptions of rigidity and limited co-creation potential. This dynamic exemplifies the user experience paradox most clearly: structures that support less experienced users may simultaneously constrain more exploratory practices. The artisanal and non-scalable nature of current data preparation workflows further exacerbates this limitation [152]. Overall, the Real-time Modification criterion highlights the primary weakness of current collaborative experiential ecosystems. Overcoming this limitation will require advances in bidirectional interoperability as well as the development of adaptive methodological frameworks capable of balancing guidance, flexibility, and information continuity. 7.1.2.3 Decision-Making Discussion The Decision-Making area addresses the final stage of the Usage phase, where experiential perception and collaborative interaction converge into collective judgment and action. Building on the experiential and collaborative layers discussed previously, this section examines how the proposed framework influences the efficiency, effectiveness, and inclusiveness of decision-making processes, thereby addressing RQ2. Rather than treating decision-making as a direct outcome of visualization technologies, the analysis focuses on how a structured methodological approach shapes the cognitive and procedural conditions under which decisions are formed. The discussion follows a logical sequence aligned with established sensemaking and information- processing models [116], articulated through three sequential criteria: Information Inspection, Information Interpretation, and Information Evaluation. The impact of the framework on each criterion is assessed using the dual metrics of Efficiency and Effectiveness. Information Inspection Information Inspection represents the exploratory phase in which stakeholders navigate and interrogate the model to identify information relevant to the decision task. Empirical evidence indicates that the framework positively influenced both efficiency and effectiveness at this stage, while also revealing a tension between procedural guidance and exploratory flexibility. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management From an efficiency perspective, the presence of explicit objectives, structured interaction paths, and consistent interfaces reduced cognitive load and improved user autonomy, contributing to a measurable reduction in average simulation times. Qualitative feedback further indicates that inspection effectiveness increased when simulations were calibrated to specific decision-making objectives rather than attempting to represent the entire project at once. In the Borgo Pirelli case, task-oriented configurations—such as focused 360° views in the virtual theatre— supported targeted analysis of spatial and contextual issues. Conversely, simulations that exposed the full project without task-oriented filtering tended to increase cognitive load, particularly for non-technical stakeholders. Given the costs and complexity associated with experiential representations, these findings suggest that selective and hybrid deployment strategies are more sustainable and effective for decision-oriented use. In terms of effectiveness, the framework functioned as a cognitive equalizer, reducing information asymmetries among participants. Even in contexts characterised by anticipatory technological dissonance—where a majority of participants had no prior experience with immersive systems—the ability to inspect environments at a 1:1 scale supported spatial understanding and facilitated the identification of critical issues that are difficult to infer from two-dimensional representations alone. At the same time, the user experience paradox re-emerged: while structured inspection enhances effectiveness for predefined tasks, excessive rigidity may constrain autonomous exploration and limit the discovery of unforeseen issues. Information Interpretation The interpretation phase concerns the transformation of inspected data into shared and actionable understanding. Analysis based on efficiency and effectiveness metrics indicates that the framework accelerated comprehension and supported more inclusive interpretation processes. From an efficiency standpoint, participants reported faster understanding of design conditions compared to traditional approaches. This effect can be attributed to the shift from symbolic abstraction to perceptual incorporation [147], whereby spatial information is experienced rather than decoded. While immersive devices enhanced perceptual intensity, desktop interfaces supported more stable and reflective interpretation, highlighting the importance of aligning technological configurations with interpretative objectives. Effectiveness emerged most clearly in terms of shared understanding. The framework enabled participants with different levels of technical expertise to access a common interpretative ground, supporting collective sensemaking. This equalising effect is particularly relevant in administrative contexts such as Service Conferences, where decision legitimacy depends on the informed participation of heterogeneous stakeholders. The statistically significant improvement observed in spatial and design understanding confirms the effectiveness of the entity-centric approach (user, model, environment) in fostering situated and holistic interpretation. Information Evaluation The evaluation phase translates shared understanding into judgment and decision. Empirical evidence indicates that the framework positively influenced both the efficiency and the effectiveness of this process. In terms of efficiency, the structured approach supported faster consensus-building by reducing ambiguity and unproductive discussions. By providing a common experiential reference, cognitive effort was redirected from interpretative alignment toward the evaluation of alternatives, resulting in more focused deliberation. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Effectiveness was observed across three interconnected dimensions: decision quality, inclusiveness, and legitimacy. Decision quality benefited from direct experiential evaluation, allowing participants to identify issues and opportunities that were not apparent in traditional two-dimensional representations. In several instances, this led to reconsideration of materials or design solutions, indicating the framework’s role as a platform for experiential verification rather than mere review. Inclusiveness was enhanced through the cognitive equaliser effect, enabling non-technical stakeholders to participate meaningfully in evaluation processes. This contributed to more robust and resilient decisions, potentially reducing the likelihood of subsequent disputes. Decision legitimacy was supported by participants’ perception of the system as reliable and appropriate for formal administrative contexts. This perception extends the concept of transparency beyond document availability toward the comprehensibility and negotiability of design choices, reinforcing institutional trust. Overall, the results indicate that the framework contributes to more efficient, informed, and inclusive decision- making during the Usage phase. These effects are attributable not to specific technologies, but to the structured orchestration of information, interaction, and experiential alignment provided by the framework. 7.1.2.4 Implications for RQ2 The results associated with the second research question (RQ2) suggest a shift away from a predominantly technocentric evaluation of advanced representation systems. Empirical evidence indicates that the effectiveness of such systems does not primarily depend on representational fidelity, but on their capacity to orchestrate human experience by integrating cognitive, emotional, and relational dimensions during use. From this perspective, technology functions as an experiential mediator rather than as an end in itself. The framework operates not as a passive data container, but as an active methodological device that structures interaction, guides interpretation, and supports decision-making. User experience therefore emerges as a structural variable of the Representative Ecosystem, influencing trust, engagement, and awareness of individual contribution. Consistent with research in Human Computer Interaction and User Experience [38,153], the quality of interaction depends less on the elimination of friction than on the presence of coherent and rewarding flows that foster perceived control, competence, and self-efficacy. At the same time, the results highlight that experiential orchestration is not neutral. Designing guided and supportive environments inevitably involves value-based choices that shape how users interact, learn, and make decisions. This introduces methodological and ethical considerations, particularly in administrative contexts where inclusiveness, transparency, and accountability are essential. The framework improves communicative fluidity and operational alignment by acting as an infrastructure for augmented communication; however, empirical observations also reveal a functional paradox. While structure facilitates convergence and efficiency, excessive rigidity may constrain exploration and co-design, especially in early or exploratory decision phases. This tension, also observed in collaborative BIM–XR studies [114], reflects the unresolved gap between the structured logic of information models and the interactive flexibility expected in experiential environments. The findings therefore suggest that the response to RQ2 does not lie in a monolithic or uniform approach, but in the development of modular and adaptive frameworks. Such frameworks should support the dynamic alternation between guided, validation-oriented modes and more open, exploratory modes of interaction, depending on Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management procedural objectives. The challenge is to maintain methodological coherence while ensuring sufficient operational flexibility to accommodate diverse decision-making needs. A central implication concerns the framework’s role as a cognitive equalizer. The results show a consistent reduction in information asymmetries related to age, technical background, and prior experience, supporting more inclusive participation in decision-making processes. This equalising effect does not eliminate the role of expertise; rather, it reshapes it. While reliance on specialised skills for symbolic interpretation and mediation is reduced, expert knowledge remains essential for regulatory interpretation, formal validation, accountability, and critical judgment. In this sense, professional authority is not diminished but reoriented toward higher-level evaluative and governance functions within technology-supported processes. From a broader perspective, the framework can be interpreted as a governance-oriented support tool for complex administrative procedures rather than as a visualization system alone. Decision legitimacy emerges not solely from formal compliance, but also from the quality of the shared experiential process that precedes formal approval. This shift from object-centric, document-based logic toward an entity-centric approach centred on users, information models, and contextual interaction supports what can be described as experiential transparency. Unlike procedural transparency based on document access, experiential transparency relates to the comprehensibility and negotiability of spatial and procedural information within structured deliberative environments [9,116]. Finally, the analysis highlights structural limitations that warrant critical consideration. These include the risk of cognitive homogenisation, potential exclusion due to technological and organisational complexity, and increased dependence on mediated interaction. Addressing these limitations will be essential to ensure that experiential frameworks support informed, inclusive, and resilient decision-making without undermining critical reflection or procedural plurality 7.1.3 Post-Usage Phase (RQ3): Professional Adoption This section addresses the Post-Usage phase, which represents the final step in evaluating the sustainability and scalability of the proposed framework beyond its immediate application.
