The primary objective is to answer the third research question (RQ3): What factors influence the acceptance and effectiveness of structured experiential solutions among different professional categories involved in administrative procedures? Following the analysis of experiential, collaborative, and decision-making effects observed during the Usage phase, the focus here shifts toward the longer-term implications of professional adoption. In the Post-Usage phase, experiential technologies are assessed not in terms of their immediate performance, but with respect to their integration into operational workflows, their acceptance by heterogeneous professional profiles, and their potential to consolidate as usable and repeatable tools within everyday administrative practice. The discussion is structured around the framework’s final conceptual area, Effects and Consequences, which is interpreted through two complementary criteria: Practical consequences, referring to operational and functional impacts emerging from use, analysed by distinguishing observed benefits from criticalities and constraints; Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Physical consequences, addressing ergonomic aspects, comfort, and physical sustainability associated with prolonged or repeated use. Through this structure, the section provides a critical interpretation of adoption dynamics by correlating empirical evidence from the case studies with observed barriers and enabling factors. Particular attention is given to differences across professional profiles, organisational constraints, and systemic limitations typical of administrative and regulatory contexts. From this perspective, the Post-Usage phase should be understood not only as a moment of verification, but also as a reflective space in which the conditions for realistic and sustainable integration of the framework into administrative procedures are examined. Rather than asserting broad generalisability, the analysis aims to clarify under which conditions, and with which limitations, structured experiential approaches may evolve from experimental implementations into stable components of collaborative and regulatory decision-making processes. 7.1.3.1 Practical Consequences: Balancing Operational Benefits and Structural Limitations The analysis of practical consequences examines the tangible operational effects associated with the adoption of the proposed framework during the Post-Usage phase. Empirical evidence highlights a dual outcome: on the one hand, the emergence of inclusive and measurable operational benefits; on the other, the persistence of structural limitations that constrain scalability and co-creative potential. Positive aspects One of the most consistent findings concerns the framework’s equalising effect. Across the case studies, basic operational effectiveness was found to depend less on the specific technology employed or on individual expertise levels, and more on the underlying information structure. This observation aligns with existing research on digital collaborative systems, which identifies the democratization of information access as a key enabling factor [154]. Two results emerge particularly clearly. First, increased involvement and decision quality: the 1:1 spatial perception enabled participants to reassess materials and design solutions, confirming previous findings on the role of Representative Ecosystems in reducing interpretation errors and supporting more informed decisions [60]. Second, process efficiency: a 30.43% reduction in average simulation time and a 50% reduction in requests for technical assistance were observed, consistent with studies on immersive and experiential design reviews and their impact on collaborative process efficiency [106]. From an administrative perspective, these effects contribute to strengthening the legitimacy of experiential approaches in public decision-making processes. In line with the literature [66,150], accountability, transparency, and traceability emerge as necessary conditions for adoption within public administrations. Rather than presenting the framework as a technological solution alone, the findings suggest its interpretation as an experiential governance ecosystem, in which elements such as interaction traceability, role-based access, and procedural logging support legal and institutional compatibility. Negative aspects Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management At the same time, several operational limitations were identified. A recurring issue is the user experience paradox, whereby the same structural elements that enable efficiency and convergence may become constraining when unanticipated needs arise [65]. The main criticalities observed include: Limited management of emerging requirements, reducing support for dynamic exploration and alternative scenario development; Weak dynamic interoperability, as the persistent disconnect between BIM models and virtual representations prevents real-time bidirectional updates, a limitation also identified in BIM–XR literature [155]; Reduced workflow scalability, due to largely manual and non-standardised model preparation pipelines, consistent with findings on the scalability challenges of digital twins [156]; Implementation costs, both initial and recurring. One-time costs include hardware acquisition, software licensing, and system configuration, while recurring costs relate primarily to organisational and human factors, such as specialised personnel for simulation setup, updates, and support. Although the framework contributes to reducing decision-making time and coordination overhead, it does not eliminate the need for specialised competencies. These structural costs therefore remain a critical factor for scalability, particularly in public-sector contexts characterised by budgetary and regulatory constraints. 7.1.3.2 Physical Consequences: Ergonomics as a Critical Challenge to Adoption The  analysis of physical consequences focuses on the ergonomic and physiological impact of experiential systems on users, a dimension that is essential for long-term professional sustainability. As noted in the literature, no system can be considered effective if it systematically generates physical or cognitive discomfort [2]. The results reveal a clear differentiation between technologies, positioning ergonomics among the most critical and still unresolved challenges for professional adoption. Positive aspects Traditional interfaces, particularly desktop PCs, exhibited the highest ergonomic performance. The low average discomfort level recorded (0.4/5) confirms their suitability for prolonged work sessions. Familiarity with established interfaces reduces both cognitive and physical load [157], making desktop-based interaction a stable and sustainable baseline for professional use. Negative aspects By contrast, immersive VR devices emerged as the primary ergonomic barrier to adoption. Four critical aspects were identified: Significant discomfort, with average values (2.6/5) markedly higher than desktop configurations, confirming the lower suitability of immersive devices for extended sessions [158]; Rapid fatigue, with physical and visual strain appearing after 15–20 minutes, consistent with ergonomic studies [158]; Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Immersion–discomfort paradox, whereby the same factors that enhance presence also intensify motion sickness symptoms [158]; Structural technological limitations, such as headset weight, field of view, and posture constraints, which lie beyond the informational scope of the framework and cannot be compensated through methodological structuring alone [158]. It is important to distinguish between transient and structural issues. Some limitations such as initial discomfort or interaction uncertainty were partially mitigated through habituation and training. Others, including rapid fatigue and motion sickness, persisted even among experienced users, indicating that they are primarily linked to current device characteristics rather than to users’ familiarity or information design. 7.1.3.3 Implications for RQ3 The third research question (RQ3) What factors influence the professional acceptance of experiential solutions in administrative processes? reveals a complex and multilevel adoption landscape. The findings indicate that professional acceptance does not depend on a single determinant, but rather on the dynamic interaction between three interrelated dimensions: perceived effectiveness, technological maturity, and socio-professional mediation. The first dimension concerns perceived effectiveness. The framework was consistently evaluated as a cognitive equaliser, capable of reducing information asymmetries and making project understanding more accessible across heterogeneous professional profiles. However, empirical evidence confirms that perceived usefulness alone is insufficient to ensure stable adoption. As observed by Seo et al. [27], effectiveness must be accompanied by organisational compatibility and institutional embedding. Experiential solutions become routine practices only when they align with established decision-making models, procedural logics, and accountability structures. The second dimension relates to technological maturity. Ergonomic constraints such as reduced comfort beyond short usage periods rigid workflows, and limited BIM–experiential interoperability indicate that the current ecosystem remains partially immature. Technologies that require continuous expert mediation or highly specialised competencies are difficult to scale within public-sector contexts characterised by regulatory rigidity and limited resources. From this perspective, adoption is contingent upon the evolution toward more interoperable, lightweight, and operationally autonomous systems that can integrate into existing administrative processes with reduced dependency on technical support. The third dimension concerns socio-professional mediation. The analysis confirms significant differences among researchers, architects, and technical staff, each interpreting experiential technologies through distinct professional cultures and organisational practices [2]. Systemic adoption therefore requires forms of cultural mediation capable of adapting tools, interfaces, and workflows to specific professional contexts. Rather than a single universal solution, the findings point toward the need for modular and situated approaches that can communicate effectively across roles and disciplines. These three dimensions do not operate independently, but in mutual tension: systems perceived as effective but technologically immature tend to generate frustration; technologically advanced solutions lacking cultural mediation often encounter resistance; socio-professional mediation without tangible perceived utility fails to justify sustained investment. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Consistent with Assila et al. [87], professional adoption emerges from the co-evolution of technologies, practices, and institutional frameworks. Within this interpretative perspective, Blanks Theory is employed as a conceptual lens to describe the unresolved structural spaces that technology alone cannot address such as the absence of consolidated standards for experiential data, regulatory ambiguity, and persistent uncertainties regarding privacy and economic sustainability. In this sense, Blanks Theory does not represent an empirical outcome of the study, but an interpretative construct used to articulate conditions that currently limit large-scale institutional adoption. As noted by Abbasnejad [2], without adequate cultural and institutional infrastructures, even promising innovations tend to remain confined to isolated experimental contexts. Professional adoption should therefore be understood as a progressive and systemic process rather than as a one- off implementation. Effective strategies must operate simultaneously at technical, organisational, and regulatory levels; adapt to local professional cultures; and be conceived as long-term transformations rather than episodic interventions. From this analysis, three persistent tensions emerge as critical for future development: Standardisation versus customisation: balancing shared procedural rules with contextual adaptability; Innovation versus stability: introducing new solutions without destabilising institutional equilibria; Certain investments versus uncertain benefits: justifying immediate costs against often long-term and indirect advantages. The response to RQ3 is thus not reducible to a finite list of adoption factors. Instead, professional acceptance is shaped by an ecosystem of tensions that defines adoption as a co-evolutionary and systemic process. Only the alignment of technological maturity, socio-cultural adaptation, and institutional infrastructure can enable the transition from isolated experiments to stable and scalable adoption of experiential solutions within public administration. 7.2 Implication of Results 7.2.1 Theoretical Implications: Towards an Ecosystem Paradigm At a theoretical level, this research contributes to a shift in how advanced representation systems are conceptualised within collaborative and administrative processes. Rather than interpreting representation technologies as isolated tools, the study supports an ecosystem-oriented perspective in which interaction between users, information models, and representational environments is treated as an integrated system. This shift can be understood as a transition from the traditional object-centric logic commonly associated with BIM focused primarily on the digital model as a self-contained artefact towards an entity-centric configuration. In this configuration, users, models, and environments are interdependent components of a Representative Ecosystem, better suited to addressing the cognitive, organisational, and procedural complexity of contemporary collaborative decision-making. Within this perspective, the proposed framework is not conceived as a data container, but as a methodological device that structures experience, collaboration, and decision-making. The findings indicate that the effectiveness of experiential implementations depends less on technological sophistication alone and more on the ability to Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management design coherent information architectures in which human experience is explicitly considered as part of the decision-making process. From this standpoint, the research extends the interpretative scope of the Level of Information Need concept (ISO 19650 / ISO 7817-1) by operationalising dimensions that are already implicit in the definition of an “object” as part of the perceivable and conceivable world. Elements such as use scenarios, interaction conditions, and users’ cognitive experience are treated as preconditions for the design of Representative Ecosystems, alongside geometric and informational requirements. This extension should be understood as a conceptual and methodological contribution rather than a normative reinterpretation of existing standards. By integrating experiential factors such as presence, engagement, and interaction, the framework contributes to addressing a methodological gap between information modelling and information interpretation. Information is thus not only represented, but structured in a way that supports its transformation into shared and perceived knowledge. In this sense, the framework enables the evaluation not only of what is represented, but also of how representation supports understanding and collaboration among stakeholders. Finally, the research contributes to a redefinition of administrative transparency. Beyond document accessibility, the concept of experiential transparency is introduced to describe conditions in which spatial and procedural information becomes collectively understandable and negotiable. Within this model, decision legitimacy derives not only from procedural correctness, but also from the quality of the shared experiential process that precedes formal validation. 7.2.2 Practical Implications: Tools for Effective Digitalization From a practical perspective, the framework provides actionable insights into how digitalisation processes can be structured to address recurring barriers in collaborative practices across the AEC sector. Rather than proposing abstract concepts, the framework identifies processes, requirements, and organisational strategies that can be integrated into existing workflows to reduce uncertainty, inefficiency, and interpretative asymmetries. For public administrations, the results highlight practical opportunities to improve the efficiency, inclusiveness, and transparency of Service Conferences. Structured experiential configurations support faster and better- informed deliberation, aligning with the “principle of results” introduced by Legislative Decree 36/2023. By acting as a cognitive equaliser, the framework reduces information asymmetries and enables more equitable participation, particularly for less experienced actors involved in complex administrative procedures. For AEC professionals, the research provides an initial methodological scaffold to navigate the increasing proliferation of digital artefacts. By systematically defining prerequisites, processes, and information implications, the framework helps mitigate data loss, distortion, and inequality that often occur when transitioning between physical datasets, information models, and experiential representations. In this context, the study also highlights the relevance of emerging hybrid professional roles, combining information management competencies (e.g. BIM management) with experiential and creative-directive skills. For technology providers, the findings suggest functional requirements oriented toward greater interoperability, adaptability, and user-centred design. The persistence of the translation gap between the structured rigidity of information models and the fluidity of Representative Ecosystems emphasises the need for solutions that manage not only data and geometry, but also interaction logic, user behaviour, and experiential continuity. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management A further practical implication concerns the potential evolution of the Common Data Environment. Evidence from the case studies suggests that traditional file-based CDEs may evolve toward more experiential configurations conceptualised here as experiential CDEs in which data, models, and interactions coexist within shared representational environments. In this perspective, the platform itself becomes a Representative Ecosystem, supporting verification, validation, and collaborative interaction through immersive and interactive simulations. This interpretation should be understood as a prospective implication, and  empirical observations but requiring further validation. Overall, the shift from managing data to structuring user experience expands the possibilities for collaborative and decision-making processes, particularly within public and administrative contexts where transparency, inclusiveness, and procedural robustness are critical. 7.2.3 Regulatory Implications: Standards for Experience The results of this research highlight a significant regulatory gap in relation to experiential representation systems. While established codes of practice and standards exist for information management (e.g. the ISO 19650 series), they primarily address the organisation and exchange of digital objects, whereas experiential dimensions remain largely unaccounted for. This misalignment limits the formal integration of Representative Ecosystems into institutional and administrative practices and represents a non-trivial barrier to systematic adoption. Within this context, the proposed framework does not aim to redefine existing standards, but rather to inform their potential evolution by introducing a structured way of conceptualising prerequisites and requirements related to experience. By explicitly linking experiential objectives to information structures and decision-making contexts, the framework provides a methodological basis for the future development of encoding rules and standards-based guidelines for experiential representation. A key regulatory implication concerns the integration of experience-related requirements into contractual and procedural documents, such as Information Specifications, Employer’s Information Requirements, and BIM Execution Plans. Formalising experiential objectives and quality criteria within these instruments could contribute to reducing interoperability barriers, clarifying responsibilities, and introducing greater procedural consistency in the design and use of Representative Ecosystems. The analysis also reveals several regulatory “blank spaces6” that remain largely unaddressed in current frameworks, a condition that can be interpreted through Blanks Theory as the presence of unresolved structural gaps. These include, in particular: the legal status and validity of decisions supported by experiential environments; data protection and privacy issues related to personal and behavioural information; the economic sustainability of immersive infrastructures; and the ergonomic and occupational conditions associated with prolonged use of advanced devices such as AR and VR systems. At present, these unresolved aspects constitute regulatory and contractual constraints rather than enablers for large-scale institutional adoption. Rather than framing these issues as immediate prescriptive requirements, the findings suggest that addressing experiential dimensions within regulatory and standardisation processes represents a necessary area for future 6 The term “blank spaces” is not used here in reference to the Blank Slate Theory, but rather to denote areas where technological capabilities have advanced faster than the corresponding regulatory, contractual, and organizational frameworks. These unresolved gaps currently represent significant barriers to the systematic adoption of experiential representation within AEC practice. Addressing them is therefore not optional, but a necessary step to fully realize the potential effects of experiential systems. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management research, policy development, and institutional experimentation. Clarifying these aspects would be a prerequisite for the responsible and sustainable integration of experiential representation systems into formal AEC practices. 7.2.4 Generalizability and adaptation potential of the framework Although the framework was conceived and empirically validated within the context of collaborative decision- making in public administrative procedures, its methodological architecture suggests a broader potential for adaptation. The challenges addressed by the framework information complexity, multi-stakeholder coordination, and the need for informed and transparent decisions are not exclusive to the public sector, but recur across multiple domains where traditional document-based approaches prove insufficient. This section does not propose a direct or mechanical extension of the framework to other contexts. Instead, it aims to distinguish between elements that are methodologically transferable and those that require contextual calibration. The framework’s potential generalisability derives from its scalable and adaptive structure, which is based on prerequisites and requirements rather than on fixed technological solutions. This structure can be reinterpreted in relation to different regulatory, organisational, and cultural conditions while preserving its underlying logic. Three methodological pillars support this adaptability: the Prerequisite–Requirement structure, which enables contextual reconfiguration without altering the framework’s core logic; the Ecosystem approach, which frames User, Model, and Environment as interdependent entities rather than isolated components; the Operationalisation of experiential dimensions, transforming experiential variables from implicit effects into explicit and assessable requirements. The articulation of the framework through fundamental questions—why, when, who, what, and where— constitutes a general taxonomy applicable to complex decision-making processes across domains. Experiential requirements derived from these questions (e.g. participation, interactivity, sensory engagement, representation modes, and information content) can be configured independently of the specific application field. Similarly, the transition from an object-centric paradigm to an ecosystem-oriented configuration represents an abstract conceptual model that retains validity across different types of systems, whether architectural projects, energy digital twins, or other information-intensive environments. Transforming variables such as presence, engagement, and usability into explicit and measurable requirements constitutes a transferable methodological contribution wherever user experience has a demonstrable influence on decision quality. This shift enables experience to be intentionally designed and evaluated, rather than treated as a secondary effect of technological choices. At the same time, transferability does not imply automatic replicability. Applying the framework in other contexts requires specific enabling conditions, including: high information complexity that exceeds the communicative capacity of two-dimensional documentation; the involvement of multiple stakeholders with heterogeneous roles and objectives; Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management decision processes with sufficient strategic or economic relevance to justify infrastructural investment; a minimum level of digital maturity, evidenced by the presence of structured information models (e.g. BIM, digital twins, GIS, or equivalent systems). When these conditions are met, the framework may act as a catalyst for more transparent, informed, and inclusive decision-making processes, while remaining sensitive to contextual constraints and adaptation requirements. 7.2.4.1 The Private Sector: Strategic Investment Decisions One area in which the framework shows potential for methodological adaptation is the private sector, particularly in contexts involving strategic investment decisions related to architectural and infrastructural developments. Real estate developers, investment funds, corporate real estate departments, and private equity actors are routinely required to evaluate complex alternatives that balance architectural quality, environmental performance, technical feasibility, and financial sustainability. In current practice, such decisions are largely informed by static documentation floor plans, elevations, renderings, financial models, and cost–benefit analyses which often struggle to convey the spatial, experiential, and operational implications of alternative scenarios. While an investor may analytically identify that one option yields a higher expected return than another, this assessment frequently remains abstract and disconnected from the spatial and functional realities the alternatives represent. Similarly, sustainability indicators may be quantitatively evaluated without a clear experiential understanding of how technical solutions affect spaces and users. Within this context, the framework can be interpreted as a decision-support structure that enables a more integrated understanding of investment alternatives. Adapting the framework would require a redefinition of prerequisites in relation to the specific decision-making context. The why would focus on supporting strategic investment decisions through multidimensional understanding; the when would correspond to early project phases, such as feasibility analysis or scenario comparison, where decisions are highly impactful yet still reversible. The who would encompass a heterogeneous ecosystem of stakeholders, including financial decision-makers, analysts, sustainability managers, and design teams, each characterised by distinct cognitive profiles and evaluation criteria. The what would consist of an ecosystem integrating alternative project models, diverse stakeholder roles, and configurable representational environments, ranging from equipped meeting spaces to distributed and cloud-based platforms. The where reflects the increasingly distributed nature of corporate decision-making, requiring both co- located and remote interaction modalities. Experiential requirements would therefore be configured in relation to these prerequisites, rather than predefined by the technology itself. This example does not imply direct transferability, but illustrates how the framework’s structure can be reinterpreted to support investment-related decision processes where experiential understanding complements quantitative evaluation. 7.2.4.2 Other Sectors: Energy, Mobility, Healthcare, Environmental Management Beyond the private real estate domain, the framework may be adapted to other sectors characterised by high information complexity and multi-stakeholder decision-making. In the energy sector, for example, experiential representations could support the planning and evaluation of complex networks and renewable integration scenarios. Energy digital twins that integrate real-time performance data may enable operators and decision- Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management makers to better understand the systemic implications of alternative interventions through visual, spatial, and temporal representations. Similarly, in sectors such as mobility, healthcare, and environmental management, the framework could be reconfigured to support shared understanding and coordination among heterogeneous actors. In each case, the specific prerequisites and experiential requirements would need to be carefully calibrated to sector-specific objectives, regulatory conditions, and organisational practices, while maintaining the underlying logic that links information content, representation strategies, and user experience. Importantly, effective application in these domains cannot be assumed a priori. Further empirical testing would be required to assess the robustness, scalability, and operational impact of the framework in contexts characterised by different levels of digital maturity, governance fragmentation, or data availability. In less mature ecosystems, adjustments to information granularity, experiential metrics, or data exchange protocols may be necessary. Accordingly, the framework should be understood not as a fixed or universal model, but as a dynamic and open methodological structure. Its value lies in its capacity to evolve and adapt to diverse interdisciplinary and international contexts, while preserving a coherent logic for structuring experiential representation in complex decision-making processes. 7.3 Limitations of the Study To mitigate potential methodological weaknesses, the research adopted a mixed-methods strategy combining quantitative and qualitative approaches. Cross-validation was performed between the demonstrative and operational case studies, and qualitative tools such as Plus–Delta Analysis were used to contextualize and balance quantitative indicators. Methodological transparency was ensured through detailed documentation of sampling procedures, technical configurations, data collection protocols, and statistical analyses. Despite these measures, the study presents several limitations that affect the interpretation and generalizability of the findings. Consistent with its exploratory and pilot-oriented nature, these limitations should be considered when evaluating the results and their applicability beyond the investigated contexts. The limitations are grouped into methodological aspects and practical–operational constraints. 7.3.1 Methodological Limitations A primary limitation concerns the restricted sample size. While the quantitative questionnaire collected 59 responses, the qualitative phases involved 12 participants in the first case study and 7 in the second. Although some quantitative results reached statistical significance (p < 0.05), the limited scale reduces statistical robustness and requires caution in interpretation. The sample also exhibits a profile bias toward technical expertise, with most participants being BIM managers, engineers, architects, or researchers. In the first case study, recruitment through trade fairs resulted in a convenience sample composed of professionals already inclined toward digital technologies. Consequently, key actors in administrative procedures—such as public officials, legal representatives, decision-makers, or citizens— were underrepresented. The second case study partially mitigated this bias by involving professionals engaged in a real administrative procedure; however, the small number of participants remains insufficient for broad generalization. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Another limitation derives from the controlled and expert-assisted testing environment. Continuous technical support and the absence of real operational constraints (e.g. time pressure, legal responsibility, budgetary limits) may have positively influenced user experience, potentially masking issues that would emerge under routine administrative conditions. Finally, the study adopts a cross-sectional perspective, assessing impacts at a single point in time. This limits insight into learning effects, long-term perception stability, and the persistence of benefits. Two aspects are particularly relevant: a potential novelty effect, whereby positive responses may partly reflect initial curiosity toward immersive technologies; the limited duration of testing sessions (15–45 minutes), which may not fully capture collaborative dynamics or fatigue effects associated with prolonged use. 7.3.2 Practical Limitations and Complexity of the Framework A further set of limitations relates to the practical complexity of the framework. While its granularity supports methodological completeness, it may hinder adoption in small-scale projects or organisations with limited resources. In such contexts, the effort required to define and manage detailed prerequisites and requirements may outweigh the expected benefits. Some experiential requirements such as presence, engagement, and interaction are inherently subjective, making it difficult to translate them into objective or legally binding specifications. Moreover, technological constraints remain significant, particularly physical discomfort during immersive sessions and limited accessibility for certain user groups (e.g. motion sickness, visual impairments), confirming barriers widely reported in the literature. Dependence on specific hardware and software platforms introduces additional challenges related to technological obsolescence and interoperability. Experiential platforms do not yet function as full Common Data Environments, while traditional CDEs lack native experiential capabilities. This results in parallel workflows and the creation of derived virtual representations that may diverge from authoritative information models. Importantly, these issues do not represent entirely new problems within administrative procedures. Contemporary AEC workflows already rely on multiple representations, derived datasets, and partially interoperable platforms. In this sense, advanced experiential systems do not introduce fragmentation per se, but rather make existing procedural discontinuities more explicit. Nevertheless, these factors influence professional acceptance and reinforce the perception of experiential systems as experimental rather than fully consolidated tools for routine administrative use 7.3.3 Contextual Limitations and Barriers to Adoption A major contextual limitation identified in this study concerns the absence of consolidated standards and shared guidelines for the use of information models within extended and experiential representation environments. This gap limits the definition of common evaluation criteria, reduces reproducibility across platforms, and constrains the institutionalisation of tested practices. In administrative contexts, the lack of a clear regulatory framework also complicates the formal recognition of experiential simulations as legitimate decision-support tools, where transparency, traceability, and procedural rigor are mandatory requirements. Economic constraints represent a further barrier to adoption. The implementation of Representative Ecosystems entails non-negligible costs related Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management to hardware, software licences, system configuration, and the availability of specialised personnel. For many public administrations, these costs remain difficult to sustain within rigid budgetary frameworks, contributing to a misalignment between technological potential and actual organisational feasibility. The study did not include a detailed quantification of the investments required for large-scale deployment. Beyond initial acquisition costs, additional factors such as staff training, change management, system maintenance, technological updates, and medium- to long-term return on investment (ROI) were not systematically assessed. These aspects are particularly critical in public-sector decision-making, where financial commitments must be justified within complex approval and accountability cycles. Finally, the cross-sectional nature of the study represents a methodological limitation. Observations were conducted at a single point in time, preventing assessment of learning effects, adaptation processes, or the long- term stability of the observed experiential and cognitive impacts. As a result, it was not possible to determine whether effects related to presence, engagement, and decision-making quality persist, diminish, or evolve with prolonged use. Longitudinal investigations would therefore be required to better understand how Representative Ecosystems integrate into routine administrative practices over time. 7.3.4 Unresolved Factors and the "Empty Space Theory" This section addresses unresolved factors that emerged during the interpretation of the results and that could not be fully addressed within the scope of the present research. In this context, Blanks Theory is used as an interpretative device to explicitly acknowledge the existence of structural gaps that lie beyond the empirical validation achieved in this study. It does not represent an outcome of the research, but rather a means to ensure methodological transparency regarding its current boundaries. Several unresolved factors concern the institutional and regulatory domain. The absence of a consolidated legal framework governing the formal validity of decisions supported by experiential environments, the definition of legal liability, and the management of intellectual property within complex digital ecosystems remain significant obstacles to large-scale adoption. These aspects were not addressed empirically in the case studies and therefore limit the framework’s immediate applicability within formal administrative procedures. Additional uncertainties relate to ethical and data protection considerations associated with advanced experiential platforms. While biometric or behavioural data (e.g. gaze tracking, facial features, body movements) were neither collected nor required in the proposed framework or in the conducted case studies, their potential use in real-world deployments raises relevant concerns. Issues related to data privacy, identity management, anonymity, and possible algorithmic bias at platform level remain open questions that require dedicated regulatory and empirical investigation. Economic sustainability represents a further unresolved dimension. The study did not assess long-term business models, return on investment (ROI), or scale effects associated with widespread adoption of Representative Ecosystems. As a result, the economic feasibility of systemic implementation particularly within public administrations characterised by rigid budgetary constraints cannot be inferred from the present findings. Finally, the contextual scope of the study introduces additional limitations. The research was conducted exclusively within the Italian administrative framework and focused on building permit procedures. Regulatory, cultural, and procedural differences across national contexts may significantly affect both the applicability and acceptability of the framework. Moreover, the selected case studies Borgo Pirelli and Milano MIND are Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management characterised by high urban complexity and resource availability, conditions that may not be representative of smaller-scale or less resourced administrative contexts. Overall, these unresolved factors delineate the boundaries within which the proposed framework should be interpreted. Explicitly acknowledging these “empty spaces” does not weaken the contribution of the study; rather, it clarifies its current limits and identifies concrete directions for future research required to support broader institutional adoption. 7.3.5 Implications of Limitations on Results The limitations identified in this study require a cautious interpretation of the reported results. Observed improvements such as the 30.43% reduction in decision-making time and the 50% reduction in requests for technical support should be interpreted as indicative trends rather than as definitive evidence of the framework’s superiority over alternative approaches. Similarly, the observed cognitive equalisation effect requires validation on larger and more heterogeneous samples, particularly involving non-technical actors typically engaged in administrative procedures. The effectiveness of the entity-centric configuration also requires confirmation under more variable operational conditions and across different procedural settings. Three main areas of uncertainty remain: the long-term stability of the observed benefits and their evolution under routine operational conditions; the transferability of the framework beyond the specific administrative procedures investigated; the economic scalability of the approach, which requires more robust cost–benefit and return-on- investment models. Taken together, these aspects outline a framework that demonstrates concrete potential within the investigated context, but whose scientific robustness and operational reliability require further empirical consolidation. 7.3.6 Limitations as Research Opportunities The limitations discussed should not be interpreted solely as constraints, but also as indications of future research directions. In particular, they highlight the need for longitudinal studies capable of assessing learning effects, adaptation processes, and the persistence of experiential benefits over time. A second research direction concerns the development of shared and standardised methodologies for evaluating experiential representation systems in collaborative and administrative processes. The absence of common evaluation protocols currently limits the comparability of results across studies and contexts. Finally, the findings suggest the relevance of further investigation into governance-oriented frameworks for experiential systems, focusing on regulatory, organisational, and procedural integration rather than on technological performance alone. Such research would be necessary to support the transition from experimental implementations to stable and institutionally embedded practices, particularly within public administration. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 8 CONCLUSIONS Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 8.1 Summary of Key Points This research investigated how structured Representation Ecosystems can be integrated into collaborative and decision-making processes in the AEC sector, with a specific focus on administrative procedures. The study was guided by three research questions addressing: the structuring of prerequisites and requirements; the measurable effects of applying a systematic framework in real case studies; and the factors influencing professional adoption in administrative contexts. The first contribution of the research is the development of a structured framework that explicitly connects information management with representation. Rather than proposing a new definition of information objects, the framework operationalises elements already implicit in existing standards (ISO 7817-1), by treating the User, the Model, and the Environment as interdependent components of a single information ecosystem. This approach addresses a key gap identified in the literature, namely the lack of methodological guidance for the structured and informed adoption of advanced representation systems. The second contribution concerns the empirical validation of the framework through two administrative case studies, Borgo Pirelli and Milano MIND. Within the limits of the investigated sample, the results indicate that a structured experiential configuration improves clarity, supports collaboration, and enhances decision-making quality (Figure 66). Quantitative analyses show reductions in decision-making time and technical support requests, while qualitative evidence highlights improved shared understanding among heterogeneous stakeholders. These effects are attributable to the structured orchestration of information and interaction, rather than to specific technologies. The third contribution relates to professional adoption. The findings indicate that technological capability alone is insufficient to ensure sustainable uptake of Representation Ecosystems in administrative procedures. Organisational readiness, regulatory alignment, and methodological clarity emerge as critical enabling factors. In this context, the framework demonstrates its potential to function as a cognitive equaliser, supporting the accessibility of complex technical information for non-specialist actors, including public administrators, and contributing to more transparent and informed decision-making processes. The study confirms the value of a systematic, ecosystem-oriented approach to representation in administrative decision-making, while also highlighting the need for cautious interpretation of the results and further empirical validation to support broader applicability. Fig. 66 Information flow diagram illustrating the transition from standard information, managed through traditional systems, to embedded information, obtained through an Experiential Dimension (XD) in Representative Ecosystems and metaverse platforms. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management 8.2 Contributions to the Field This research contributes to the field by providing a systematic approach to integrating information management and structured experiential representation within collaborative and administrative decision-making processes. Rather than proposing a new technological solution, the study advances a methodological framework that supports the informed and structured use of Representation Ecosystems. A first contribution lies in the development of a replicable and adaptable framework that connects information structure with experiential representation. The findings indicate that the effectiveness of experiential systems depends not primarily on technological sophistication, but on the coherence of the underlying information architecture. This challenges the prevailing technology-centred view of digital innovation and highlights the role of structured information in shaping perception, collaboration, and decision-making. At a theoretical level, the research supports a shift from an object-centred BIM perspective toward an ecosystem- oriented configuration, in which User, Model, and Environment are treated as interdependent elements of a single information system. In line with existing standards (ISO 7817-1), the framework does not introduce new conceptual categories, but operationalises dimensions that are typically implicit such as usage context and cognitive experience by treating experiential aspects as design and information requirements rather than subjective effects. From an applicative perspective, the two administrative case studies (Borgo Pirelli and Milano MIND) demonstrate the framework’s potential benefits within the investigated context. The results indicate improved shared understanding, reduced ambiguity in decision-making, greater inclusiveness among stakeholders with different expertise levels, and increased time efficiency. These effects suggest that structured experiential representation can function as a cognitive equaliser, supporting accessibility and transparency in administrative procedures without replacing formal decision-making mechanisms. Tab. 55 Summary of the main findings related to the three research questions (RQ1–RQ3). Research Question Objective Key Findings (Within the Case Studies) RQ1 – Framework Development (Commissioning) Structure prerequisites and requirements for experiential representation through information management Transition from object-centric to ecosystem-oriented configuration Ecosystem defined by User, Model, and Environment Experiential dimensions treated as information and design requirements• Conceptual extension of Level of Information Need to include experiential aspects RQ2 – Implementation Effects (Usage) Assess the effects of the framework in administrative case studies Framework acts as a cognitive equaliser across roles and experience levels Introduction of experiential transparency supporting shared understanding• Reduction of the gap between technical accuracy and human interpretation RQ3 – Professional Adoption (Post-Usage) Explore factors influencing adoption in administrative contexts Effectiveness depends on contextual and organisational conditions Hybrid technological configurations are more sustainable than single-solution approaches Cultural and organisational adaptation is critical for adoption• Framework effectiveness relies on flexibility rather than rigid application 8.3 Recommendations The findings of this research suggest several recommendations aimed at consolidating and extending the use of structured Representation Ecosystems, while remaining within the limits identified in the study. From a scientific perspective, further research should prioritise the expansion of the empirical base through longitudinal studies capable of observing how experiential effects such as shared understanding, collaboration Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management quality, and decision-making efficiency evolve over time. Additional validation across a broader range of administrative procedures, professional roles, and regulatory contexts would be necessary to assess the robustness and stability of the framework. Particular attention should be given to refining experiential parameters (e.g. presence, engagement, cognitive involvement) and translating them into shared and reproducible evaluation criteria. From a professional and organisational perspective, the results indicate that experiential systems should not be approached as stand-alone visualisation tools, but as components of a broader information management strategy. This implies investment in training, the development of hybrid competencies, and the gradual integration of experiential practices into existing administrative workflows. Such an approach may help reduce information asymmetries and support more inclusive and transparent decision-making processes. From a regulatory perspective, the study highlights the need for greater alignment between experiential practices and existing standards for information management. Collaboration between national and international standardisation bodies could support the gradual integration of experiential dimensions within established BIM- related frameworks, while maintaining the balance between procedural certainty and technological flexibility required in public administrative contexts. Finally, from a technological perspective, software and platform providers are encouraged to focus on interoperability, data continuity, and usability. Reducing the translation gap between information models and experiential environments, while ensuring accessibility for non-specialist users, would be a prerequisite for broader and more sustainable adoption. These recommendations do not represent immediate prescriptions, but indicate priority areas for future research, policy development, and technological refinement necessary to support the responsible evolution of experiential representation systems in administrative decision-making. 8.4 Directions for Future Research The findings of this study indicate several directions for future research aimed at consolidating and extending the proposed framework within its scope. A first priority concerns methodological consolidation. Future studies should focus on longitudinal investigations capable of assessing how the observed experiential, collaborative, and decision-making effects evolve over time in real administrative contexts. Extending validation to a larger number of cases and to different types of administrative procedures would support a more robust assessment of the framework’s stability, scalability, and boundary conditions. A second direction relates to the evolution of information infrastructures supporting experiential representation. The results suggest the need to explore how Common Data Environments might evolve to better accommodate experiential interactions, while preserving the traceability, reliability, and procedural control required in administrative workflows. This research direction should prioritise interoperability and information continuity rather than the introduction of new technologies per se. Further research is also needed to examine the applicability of the framework across different organisational and regulatory contexts. While this study focused on building permit procedures within the Italian administrative system, comparative investigations across jurisdictions could clarify how regulatory, cultural, and institutional differences influence adoption and effectiveness. Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management Finally, economic, ethical, and governance-related aspects require dedicated investigation. Future work should assess cost structures, long-term sustainability, and return on investment, as well as issues related to data protection, accountability, and decision legitimacy in experiential environments. These dimensions are essential to support the responsible integration of Representative Ecosystems into formal decision-making processes. Category Potential Contributions (Derived from the Study) Aligned Future Research Directions Theoretical • Ecosystem-oriented perspective that extends beyond object-centric BIM configurations Experiential transparency supporting shared understanding in administrative decision-making Explicit linkage between information management and structured representation Conceptual consolidation of ecosystem-oriented models within information management research Validation of experiential requirements as assessable information dimensions Replication of the framework in different administrative and regulatory contexts to test adaptability Practical • Structured integration of experiential representation within administrative procedures Clarification of experiential requirements during commissioning phases (e.g. EIRs) Support for inclusiveness and accessibility in complex decision-making processes Longitudinal assessment of operational impacts in real administrative workflows Economic evaluations (costs, effort, organisational impact) of structured experiential adoption Analysis of emerging hybrid competencies related to information and experiential management Regulatory • Identification of gaps between existing information standards and experiential practices Contribution to the discussion on extending information requirements to experiential dimensions Investigation of legal validity, traceability, and accountability in experience-supported decisions Comparative analysis of administrative frameworks to support future standardisation initiatives Enabling Representative Ecosystems with Structured Information Management ACKNOWLEDGMENTS This research would not have been possible without the support, guidance, and collaboration of many individuals and institutions, to whom I would like to express my sincere and deepest gratitude.
